This is an original, paraphrased study guide to the concepts taught in the Angular Core Deep Dive course, rewritten for reading and fast reference. It preserves the technical substance of each lecture — the ideas, sequence, and example intent — while the prose is entirely reworded. Runnable examples are reimplemented (and, where a matching course branch exists, cited) from the companion repository angular-university/angular-course. Code samples pair source on the left with the expected rendered or console output on the right.

Run the examples locally

Every code sample targets modern Angular (v17+, standalone + signals). To run the course's own examples, clone the repo and check out the branch cited above each example:

git clone https://github.com/angular-university/angular-course.git
cd angular-course
npm install
git checkout ng17-recordings   # or the branch cited on an example
npm start                       # ng serve → http://localhost:4200

To bundle this guide plus a demo as a ZIP: place this HTML file at the repo root, then zip -r angular-deep-dive.zip angular-course.html src package.json angular.json.

Introduction

This opening section frames the Angular Core Deep Dive: it explains what the course covers, gets a development environment running with Node and the Angular CLI, and introduces Angular's two foundational ideas — custom HTML elements and the clean separation between the data (model) and the template (view).

Angular Core Deep Dive — Introduction

Objective: Understand the scope and teaching approach of the course, and why it deliberately covers both the modern and the classic ways of building Angular.

Taught by Vasco from Angular University, the course builds a solid grounding in Angular by focusing on the core and common modules — the base layers that every other part of the Angular ecosystem is built upon. It starts from fundamentals and moves quickly into intermediate and advanced territory, so it stays useful regardless of your current experience level.

Angular has evolved substantially. The arrival of signals introduced an entirely new component-authoring style, while the older decorator-based approach remains fully backward-compatible and will keep appearing in real projects for a long time. To prepare you for both, each feature is generally shown the modern way first, then the classic way — sometimes in alternating lectures, sometimes in alternating sections.

  • Centers on Angular's core and common modules, the foundation of the framework.
  • Progresses from beginner concepts to intermediate and advanced topics.
  • Covers both signals (new) and decorators (classic), since both appear in production code.
  • New approach is typically demonstrated first, followed by the legacy equivalent.
Common pitfallAssuming decorator-based code is obsolete. It is still fully supported and widespread — knowing both styles is essential for day-to-day work.
AdvancedSignals bring fine-grained reactivity and a path away from Zone.js-based change detection, but the two styles interoperate, so codebases commonly mix signal-driven and decorator-driven components during migration.

Recommended Software Versions

Objective: Install the runtime and tooling versions the course relies on so your setup matches the lessons.

This short reference lecture (no captions in the source) points to the software you need before writing any Angular code: a current Node.js runtime and the globally installed Angular CLI. Aligning versions up front avoids tooling mismatches later in the course. The specific numbers printed below are illustrative example values, not pinned requirements — the course recommends installing the latest Node.js release (not the LTS), since it tends to work best with front-end tools like the Angular CLI, together with the latest Angular CLI, rather than matching these exact digits.

  • Install Node.js — a recent release paired with the Angular CLI works best for front-end tooling.
  • Install the Angular CLI globally so the ng command is available everywhere.
  • Verify both installations before generating a project.
node --version
npm --version
ng version
Outputv22.x.x (example — use a current release)
10.x.x
Angular CLI: <latest>
Common pitfallRunning an outdated Node version can break the CLI. If ng is not recognized after install, ensure your global npm bin directory is on your PATH.

Course Kickoff — Development Environment Setup

Objective: Install Node and an IDE, install the Angular CLI, then scaffold and serve a first Hello World application.

To see Angular in action you first need a working environment. Download and install Node.js from nodejs.org — prefer the latest release, since it tends to play well with front-end tools like the Angular CLI. Then install an IDE; the course uses WebStorm, but Visual Studio Code is an equally good free alternative.

With Node in place, install the Angular CLI globally. This gives you the ng executable, a command-line tool that scaffolds and runs Angular projects. Use ng new to generate a project (answering the prompts for routing and stylesheet format), then ng serve — exposed here through the npm start script — to launch a development server on localhost:4200. Running ng on its own (no arguments) prints the full list of available CLI commands, a handy way to discover what the tool can do, and ng new angular-course generates a folder named angular-course holding the new project.

  • Angular is more than a web framework — it ships a CLI and a whole tooling ecosystem.
  • npm install -g @angular/cli installs the global ng command.
  • ng new scaffolds the app and installs dependencies; it prompts for routing and CSS choices.
  • npm start (which runs ng serve) hosts the app at http://localhost:4200.
npm install -g @angular/cli
ng                               # run with no args to list every CLI command
ng new angular-course
# ? Would you like to add Angular routing? No
# ? Which stylesheet format? CSS
cd angular-course
npm start
OutputApplication bundle generation complete.
Watch mode enabled. Watching for file changes...
➔ Local: http://localhost:4200/

Setup mirrors branch master of angular-university/angular-course · reimplemented for clarity.

Common pitfallForgetting to cd into the newly generated project folder before running npm start — the script only exists inside the project directory.
Advancedng serve uses an incremental dev server with hot reload. Under modern Angular it defaults to the esbuild-based application builder, which produces noticeably faster rebuilds than the older webpack pipeline.

The TypeScript Jumpstart Ebook

Objective: Point learners to a companion resource for getting comfortable with TypeScript before diving deeper into Angular.

This lecture (no captions in the source) references a bundled TypeScript primer. Because Angular components, services, and templates are all written in TypeScript, a working grasp of the language — types, classes, decorators, and modules — makes the rest of the course much easier to follow.

  • Angular is authored in TypeScript, so language familiarity is a prerequisite.
  • Key concepts to review: static types, classes, interfaces, generics, and decorators.
  • Use the companion ebook to fill any gaps before continuing.
Common pitfallTreating TypeScript as "just JavaScript." Ignoring the type system leads to confusing compiler errors that are actually pointing out real bugs.

Introduction to Angular — Custom HTML Elements, Model vs View

Objective: Explore the generated app to understand two core Angular ideas: creating your own HTML tags via components, and separating the model (data) from the view (template).

The browser loads index.html, whose body contains almost nothing except a single custom tag such as <app-root>. That tag is not a standard browser element — it is a component. All the visible content (title, image, links) is injected there by Angular, not written in index.html. Inspecting the page in Chrome DevTools confirms everything lives inside the app root. A quick confirmation that index.html is the page being served: its <title> element is exactly what appears as the browser tab's caption, even though the document body itself is otherwise empty.

A component pairs three files: a template (HTML), a stylesheet (scoped CSS), and a TypeScript class decorated with @Component. The decorator's selector defines the custom tag name, while the template renders the class's data.

The template accesses class data through interpolation — the double-curly-brace syntax. Anything inside {{ }} is evaluated as a JavaScript expression in the context of the component class. So {{ title }} reads the class's title property; editing that property and saving triggers an instant reload with the new value. The lecture walks the property through a small progression: it starts as a plain title string, is renamed to courseTitle (at which point the still-unchanged {{ title }} silently renders nothing), and is finally moved onto a data object read as {{ data.title }} — showing that the expression can reach into any object.

This illustrates the model-versus-view split: the model is the data held on the component class, and the view is the template markup. They live in separate files, in different languages, bound together by interpolation.

  • Components let you define custom HTML tags, extending the browser with your own elements.
  • A component = template (HTML) + scoped styles (CSS) + a @Component-decorated class.
  • The selector becomes the usable tag name (e.g. <app-root>).
  • Interpolation {{ expr }} evaluates a JS expression against the component instance.
  • Model (class data) and view (template) are cleanly separated and bound by interpolation.

Just as ng new generates it, the component keeps its template and styles in their own files, wired up by the templateUrl and styleUrl properties. That is the concrete form of the model/view split: the class (model) is TypeScript, the template (view) is HTML, and they live side by side as separate files in different languages.

// app.component.ts  (the model + wiring)
import { Component } from '@angular/core';

@Component({
  selector: 'app-root',
  standalone: true,
  templateUrl: './app.component.html',
  styleUrl: './app.component.css'
})
export class AppComponent {
  data = { title: 'Angular Core Deep Dive' };
}
OutputThe class exposes data.title; its template and styles are loaded from the two referenced files.
<!-- app.component.html  (the view) -->
<h1>{{ data.title }}</h1>
OutputRendered page:
<app-root><h1>Angular Core Deep Dive</h1></app-root>

Setup mirrors branch master of angular-university/angular-course · reimplemented for clarity.

Common pitfallBinding to a property name that no longer exists on the class. Renaming title to data.title but leaving {{ title }} in the template renders nothing, silently.
AdvancedInterpolation is one-way binding from model to view. Expressions must be side-effect free and are re-evaluated during change detection, so avoid calling expensive functions directly inside {{ }}.

Why Angular? Key Features of Angular Core

Objective: See Angular's headline capabilities in action — property binding, event handling, template reference variables, automatic change detection, and built-in security.

Building on the previous lesson, this lecture demonstrates several template features. Property binding passes component data into an element property using square-bracket syntax, e.g. [value]="data.title"; the brackets tell Angular the string is an expression rather than a literal. A plain literal needs no special syntax — you would just write standard HTML.

Event binding handles native browser events with round-bracket syntax, e.g. (click)="onLogoClick()". The named handler is resolved against the component class, where you place the response logic (such as showing an alert).

Template reference variables use the #name syntax to label an element (e.g. #titleInput). The reference points to the native DOM element, so you can read its current value elsewhere in the template — for instance passing titleInput.value into an (keyup) handler that updates the model. The same reference is not limited to the template: it can also be injected into the component class via @ViewChild (covered later), giving your TypeScript code a direct handle to that element.

Automatic change detection keeps the view synchronized with the model without any manual update code. When a keyup handler assigns a new value to data.title, Angular re-renders only the affected parts of the view, transparently and efficiently.

Built-in security: Angular escapes interpolated values by default. Injecting HTML or a <script> tag through bound data is neutralized — the markup is escaped and displayed as text rather than executed, protecting against common XSS attacks with no extra work.

Scoped styles (view encapsulation): CSS written in the component's own stylesheet is encapsulated to that component — it applies only inside this component's rendered DOM and does not leak out to the rest of the page. In the demo, adding a demo class to the input and styling input.demo in app.component.css restyles only this component's input; the same class name used elsewhere is untouched.

  • Property binding [prop]="expr" passes component data into element properties.
  • Event binding (event)="handler()" responds to native DOM events via class methods.
  • Template reference variables #ref expose the underlying DOM element within the template.
  • Change detection auto-syncs model and view, updating only what changed.
  • Interpolated data is HTML-escaped by default, guarding against injection attacks.
  • Component styles are encapsulated — they apply only within that component's own rendered DOM.
<input class="demo" #titleInput
       [value]="data.title"
       (keyup)="onKeyUp(titleInput.value)">

<img src="logo.png" (click)="onLogoClick()">

<h1>{{ data.title }}</h1>
OutputTyping in the input instantly updates the <h1>.
Injected "<script>alert(1)</script>" is shown as escaped text, not run.
export class AppComponent {
  data = { title: 'Angular Core Deep Dive' };

  onKeyUp(newTitle: string) {
    this.data.title = newTitle;   // change detection updates the view
  }

  onLogoClick() {
    alert('Hello world');
  }
}
OutputClicking the logo opens an alert: "Hello world".
/* app.component.css */
input.demo {
  border: 2px solid #1976d2;
  padding: 6px;
}
OutputOnly the input inside this component gets the blue border; the same .demo class elsewhere on the page is unaffected (styles are scoped to the component).

Setup mirrors branch master of angular-university/angular-course · reimplemented for clarity.

Common pitfallConfusing the binding brackets: [value]="'text'" is an expression (note inner quotes for a literal string), whereas value="text" is a plain HTML attribute. Mixing them up passes the wrong value.
AdvancedInterpolation is always escaped because Angular inserts its value as text content, not as parsed HTML — this doesn't involve DomSanitizer at all. DomSanitizer separately guards security-sensitive bindings such as [innerHTML], [src], and [style]; you can opt out with the matching bypassSecurityTrust… method (e.g. bypassSecurityTrustHtml for [innerHTML]), but doing so reintroduces XSS risk — only bypass for content you fully trust.
Cheat-sheet
npm install -g @angular/cli      # install the ng CLI globally
ng new angular-course            # scaffold a new standalone app
cd angular-course && ng serve    # run dev server at localhost:4200
ng generate component my-widget  # scaffold a new component
ng version                       # check installed CLI / Angular versions

Quick check

Which command installs the Angular CLI so the ng tool is available globally?

  1. ng install cli
  2. npm install -g @angular/cli
  3. npm start @angular/cli
Answerb — the global npm install exposes the ng executable system-wide.

What does the double-curly-brace {{ }} syntax do in a template?

  1. Declares a new component selector
  2. Handles a browser click event
  3. Interpolates a JS expression evaluated on the component class
Answerc — interpolation binds the view to model data by evaluating the expression against the component instance.

When bound data containing a <script> tag is interpolated, Angular by default will:

  1. Execute the script in the browser
  2. Escape it and render it as plain text
  3. Throw a compile error
Answerb — automatic HTML escaping neutralizes the markup, defending against XSS.

Angular Components, Core Directives and Pipes

This section is the heart of building Angular user interfaces. You start by creating your own custom HTML element — a reusable course-card component — then wire data into it with @Input and bubble events out of it with @Output and EventEmitter. From there the section walks through every template building block twice: first the modern Angular 17+ control-flow blocks (@for, @if, @switch) and then their legacy structural-directive predecessors (*ngFor, *ngIf, *ngSwitch), because real projects still contain both. It closes with styling directives (ngClass, ngStyle), ng-container, and a complete tour of Angular's built-in pipes. Throughout, we reuse a single domain — a Course model rendered by a course-card component with a course-image — so every idea builds on the last.

Section Introduction — The Starter Repository

Objective: Understand how to obtain and run the pre-built playground project used for the rest of this section instead of scaffolding a fresh app each time.

Up to this point the course used a freshly generated CLI project. From here on the lessons switch to a ready-made starter repository hosted on the Angular University GitHub organization. It is essentially the same as the app you already built, with three additions: some CSS to style components, a small dataset of courses to render, and a lightweight HTTP server used much later for the HTTP module lessons.

  • Each course section lives on its own Git branch in the repo (for example a components branch).
  • You can either download a branch as a ZIP or clone the repo and check the branch out locally.
  • After getting the code, run npm install to fetch dependencies, then npm start to launch the dev server on port 4200.

Example — branch ng17-recordings (or 1-components), src/app/courses/… · reimplemented for clarity.

# check out the section branch into a new local branch
git checkout -b 1-components origin/1-components
npm install
npm start
OutputLocal dev server running at http://localhost:4200 — the starter playground loads.
Common pitfallForgetting to run npm install after switching branches. Dependencies can differ between branches, so a missing install often causes "module not found" errors on npm start.
AdvancedInstead of a full clone you can use a sparse or shallow checkout to grab only the branch you need. For teaching repos with many independent branches, git clone --single-branch --branch 1-components avoids downloading every section's history.

Building Our First Angular Component

Objective: Create a reusable custom element — a course-card component — so the same markup is not duplicated everywhere a card appears.

One of Angular's core strengths is letting you define your own HTML elements. Rather than copy-pasting a block of card markup across screens (an "all courses" page, a "promotions" page, and so on), you extract it once into a component and reuse the tag. Generate the component with the CLI; it produces four files — a TypeScript class, an HTML template, a CSS file, and a test spec.

  • The @Component decorator marks the class as a component and configures it.
  • selector defines the custom tag name that represents the component in templates.
  • templateUrl points to the HTML file that renders on screen; move the static card markup there.
  • Once defined, dropping <course-card></course-card> multiple times renders multiple instances.

Example — branch 1-components, src/app/courses/course-card/… · reimplemented for clarity.

ng generate component courses/course-card
import { Component } from '@angular/core';

@Component({
  selector: 'course-card',
  standalone: true,
  templateUrl: './course-card.component.html',
  styleUrl: './course-card.component.css'
})
export class CourseCardComponent {}
<!-- app.component.html -->
<course-card></course-card>
<course-card></course-card>
<course-card></course-card>
OutputThree identical course cards render, each showing the same hard-coded title and image.
Common pitfallHard-coding the card's title and image directly in the template. This makes every instance identical — the very next step is to make that content configurable via inputs.
AdvancedAngular component selectors follow CSS selector rules. Besides element selectors (course-card), you can use attribute selectors ([courseCard]) or class selectors. Element selectors are conventional for full components; attribute selectors are handy when enhancing an existing native element.

Component Inputs and the @Input Decorator

Objective: Pass data from a parent into a child component using property binding and the @Input decorator, so each card shows its own course.

Before a template can display data, that data must exist as a member property on the component class. In the parent, expose the course objects as member variables. Then bind a value into the child using square-bracket syntax — Angular treats the string inside the quotes as a JavaScript expression evaluated in the parent's context. The child declares a matching property annotated with @Input so Angular knows it is a configurable input. Rather than pass many separate inputs (title, image, description), it is cleaner to pass the whole strongly-typed Course object at once, which also gives you autocomplete in both the class and the template.

  • Square brackets [course]="c" bind an expression from the parent into a child input.
  • The child marks the receiving property with @Input().
  • Each component instance has its own isolated data scope.
  • Passing a typed model object beats passing many primitive inputs — fewer bindings, full type safety.
  • Modern Angular can mark an input as required: @Input({ required: true }) produces a compile-time error if it is omitted.

Example — branch 1-components, src/app/courses/course-card/course-card.component.ts · reimplemented for clarity.

// model/course.ts
export interface Course {
  id: number;
  description: string;
  longDescription?: string;
  iconUrl?: string;
  category: 'BEGINNER' | 'INTERMEDIATE' | 'ADVANCED';
}

// course-card.component.ts
import { Component, Input } from '@angular/core';
import { Course } from '../model/course';

@Component({ /* ... */ })
export class CourseCardComponent {
  @Input({ required: true }) course!: Course;
}
<!-- app.component.html -->
<course-card [course]="coreCourse"></course-card>
<course-card [course]="rxjsCourse"></course-card>

<!-- course-card.component.html -->
<h2 class="course-title">{{ course.description }}</h2>
<img class="course-image" [src]="course.iconUrl">
<p>{{ course.longDescription }}</p>
OutputEach card now shows its own title, image, and description from the bound Course object.
Common pitfallExpecting an input to be visible in the template before assigning it to a member variable. Data must first live on the component instance; a value that only exists locally elsewhere will not render.
AdvancedMarking an input required only forces the template to bind the property — the bound value can still be null or undefined at runtime. Angular's newer signal-based inputs (input.required<Course>()) provide the same compile-time guarantee with reactive semantics.

@Output — Custom Events and EventEmitter

Objective: Emit a custom event from a child component that the parent can handle, enabling child-to-parent communication.

Inputs send data down; outputs send events up. Suppose each card has a "View Course" button that should tell the parent which course was chosen. Inside the child, handle the button's native click, then re-emit a custom event carrying the selected course. Declare an EventEmitter property, annotate it with @Output, and call .emit(value). In the parent template, bind to that custom event with parentheses and read the payload via $event.

Why is @Output even necessary? Native DOM events already travel on their own. Put a (click) handler on the button and another on the <course-card> tag in the parent, and a single physical click fires both — the native click bubbles up the DOM from the button, through the card, to the parent. Custom EventEmitter events behave differently: they do not bubble through the component tree at all. @Output is precisely what exposes the emitter to the parent as a bindable event, and only the component that declares the binding can listen for it.

By default Angular derives the event's public name from the EventEmitter variable name — courseSelected in the class becomes (courseSelected) in the parent. Rename that variable and the parent binding silently stops matching, unless you pin a stable public name with a string alias: @Output('courseSelected').

  • @Output() exposes an EventEmitter as a bindable event on the component.
  • The type parameter (EventEmitter<Course>) declares the payload type.
  • In the parent, (courseSelected)="onSel($event)" receives the emitted value.
  • Without @Output, the emitter is invisible to parent templates and the binding does nothing.
  • Custom Angular events do not bubble up the component tree the way native DOM events do.
  • The public event name defaults to the EventEmitter variable name; pass a string alias to override it: @Output('courseSelected').

Example — branch 1-components, src/app/courses/course-card/course-card.component.ts · reimplemented for clarity.

import { Component, Input, Output, EventEmitter } from '@angular/core';
import { Course } from '../model/course';

@Component({ /* ... */ })
export class CourseCardComponent {
  @Input({ required: true }) course!: Course;
  @Output() courseSelected = new EventEmitter<Course>();

  onViewCourse() {
    this.courseSelected.emit(this.course);
  }
}
<!-- course-card.component.html -->
<button (click)="onViewCourse()">View Course</button>

<!-- app.component.html -->
<course-card
  [course]="coreCourse"
  (courseSelected)="onCourseSelected($event)">
</course-card>
Outputconsole › clicking "View Course" logs the selected Course object at the parent level.
// course-card.component.ts (child)
onViewCourse() {
  console.log('child: button clicked');     // native handler on the button
  this.courseSelected.emit(this.course);    // custom event — needs @Output
}

// app.component.ts (parent)
onCourseSelected(course: Course) {
  console.log('parent: courseSelected ->', course.description);
}
onNativeCardClick() {
  console.log('parent: native click reached <course-card>');
}
<!-- app.component.html -->
<course-card
  [course]="coreCourse"
  (click)="onNativeCardClick()"
  (courseSelected)="onCourseSelected($event)">
</course-card>
Outputconsole › one click on "View Course" logs: child: button clicked, parent: courseSelected -> …, and parent: native click reached <course-card>. The native click is caught at both the button and the parent's <course-card> because it bubbled up; the courseSelected line appears only because @Output exposes the emitter — delete the decorator and just the native logs remain.
Common pitfallForgetting the @Output decorator on the EventEmitter. The child code compiles and emits, but the parent binding silently never fires because Angular does not treat the property as an output.
AdvancedBecause custom outputs do not bubble, an intermediate component must explicitly re-emit an event to forward it further up. The signal era replaces this pattern with the output() function (courseSelected = output<Course>()), which is functionally equivalent but avoids the EventEmitter class entirely.

Angular 17 Control Flow Syntax — Overview

Objective: Understand the new built-in control-flow blocks introduced in Angular 17 and why the course teaches both the new and the legacy forms.

Angular 17 introduced dedicated template primitives for control flow: @if for conditional rendering, @for for looping, and @switch for multi-branch selection. These blocks are built into the template compiler, so unlike the older directives they need no imports on standalone components. They also read like plain JavaScript and, in the case of @for, are safer than the directive they replace.

  • The new syntax — @if, @for, @switch — replaces *ngIf, *ngFor, and *ngSwitch.
  • New projects should prefer the block syntax; existing projects still contain the old directives, so you must know both.
  • An Angular CLI migration can automatically upgrade templates to the new syntax.
  • The lessons introduce each new primitive first, then its legacy directive counterpart.

Example — branch ng17-recordings, src/app/app.component.html · reimplemented for clarity.

<!-- new built-in control flow, no imports needed -->
@if (course) {
  <course-card [course]="course"></course-card>
}

@for (c of courses; track c.id) {
  <course-card [course]="c"></course-card>
}
OutputTemplates use JavaScript-like blocks directly — nothing added to the component's imports array.
Common pitfallAssuming the legacy directives are obsolete and skippable. They remain in the vast majority of existing codebases, so both forms are required knowledge for the foreseeable future.
AdvancedThe block control flow is compiled, not a runtime directive, so it produces smaller and faster output and eliminates the micro-syntax quirks of structural directives. It is also fully tree-shakable — unused branches contribute nothing to the bundle.

The Angular 17 @for Syntax

Objective: Iterate over an array in the template with @for instead of declaring one variable per item.

When you need to render every item in a list, declaring a separate member variable for each is impractical. @for loops over an iterable using a syntax that closely mirrors plain JavaScript. Crucially, it requires a track expression — a value that uniquely identifies each item — so Angular can update the DOM optimally (for example, appending a new item without re-rendering the whole list). Omitting track is a compile error.

  • @for (item of items; track item.id) { … } is the basic form.
  • The track expression is mandatory and uniquely identifies each element.
  • Angular self-closing component tags let you write <course-card />.
  • Once looping works, the per-item member variables can be deleted entirely.

Example — branch ng17-recordings, src/app/app.component.ts · reimplemented for clarity.

@Component({ /* ... */ })
export class AppComponent {
  courses: Course[] = COURSES; // up to ten courses
}
@for (course of courses; track course.id) {
  <course-card [course]="course" />
}
OutputAll courses in the array render on screen, one card each.
Common pitfallLeaving out the track expression. The compiler stops with "for loop must have a track expression" — unlike *ngFor, tracking is not optional here.
AdvancedUnder the hood track feeds Angular's list-diffing algorithm. Choosing a stable, unique key (a database id) yields minimal DOM operations; choosing an unstable key (like the item object itself when new references are created each render) can defeat the optimization entirely.

@for — @empty, $index and the Contextual Variables

Objective: Use the @empty block and the implicit loop variables ($index, $count, $first, $last, $even, $odd) that @for exposes.

The @empty block renders a fallback when the collection has no items — handy as a placeholder for an empty list. Inside the loop, Angular provides special variables: $index (zero-based position), $count (total items), $first/$last (boundary flags), and $even/$odd (parity). You can alias any of them with let, or reference them directly. They are commonly used to style boundary or alternating rows. Reading one directly needs no let alias — dropping {{ $count }} into a ten-item loop simply prints 10 on screen, and $index or $first work inline the same way. To watch the @empty block appear during development, temporarily set the array to empty; spreading a fresh copy back in (this.courses = [...COURSES]) restores the populated list.

  • @empty { … } displays only when the iterable is empty.
  • $index is zero-based; $count holds the total number of items.
  • $first, $last, $even, $odd are boolean flags useful for conditional styling.
  • Alias with let i = $index, or just use $index inline.
  • @for works with any JavaScript iterable, not only arrays.

Example — branch ng17-recordings, src/app/app.component.html · reimplemented for clarity.

@for (course of courses; track course.id;
      let i = $index, isFirst = $first, isLast = $last,
      isEven = $even, isOdd = $odd) {
  <course-card
    [course]="course"
    [index]="i"
    [class.ease-first]="isFirst"
    [class.ease-last]="isLast"
    [class.even]="isEven"
    [class.odd]="isOdd" />
} @empty {
  <h2>No courses found</h2>
}
OutputCards are numbered from 0; first/last get a border, even/odd rows get alternating backgrounds. An empty array shows "No courses found".
Common pitfallReassigning a contextual variable expecting it to persist — these variables are read-only per iteration. To pass one to a child, bind it to an input rather than mutating it.
AdvancedThe alias variables are lexically scoped to the loop body, so nested @for loops each get their own $index. When you need the outer index inside an inner loop, alias the outer one (let outerIndex = $index) so the name is not shadowed.

Understanding @for Tracking Functions

Objective: Explain what the mandatory track expression does and how to write a full tracking function when a simple property is not enough.

When the source array changes, Angular diffs the previous rendered list against the new one and updates only what differs. To do that it must uniquely identify each element. If it cannot, it may be forced to re-render the entire list — potentially expensive for large datasets. Making track mandatory prevents developers from accidentally introducing hard-to-diagnose performance problems. The simplified form track course.id covers most cases; when a unique id must be computed from several properties, define a method on the component and reference it.

  • Tracking lets Angular add, remove, or reorder items without rebuilding the whole list.
  • Prefer the simplified inline form (track item.id) — it is the most readable.
  • A tracking method receives (index, item) and returns a unique identifier.
  • For a list of primitives (strings), track the value itself.
  • As a last resort with no unique key, use track $index — less optimal but valid.

Example — branch ng17-recordings, src/app/app.component.ts · reimplemented for clarity.

export class AppComponent {
  courses = COURSES;

  trackCourse(index: number, course: Course) {
    return course.id;
  }
}
<!-- simplified form (preferred) -->
@for (course of courses; track course.id) { <course-card [course]="course" /> }

<!-- custom tracking function -->
@for (course of courses; track trackCourse($index, course)) { <course-card [course]="course" /> }

<!-- last resort with no unique key -->
@for (tag of tags; track $index) { <span>{{ tag }}</span> }
OutputBoth forms render identically; Angular now performs minimal DOM mutations when the array changes.
Common pitfallDefaulting to track $index when a stable id exists. Index-based tracking breaks when items are inserted or reordered, causing Angular to reuse the wrong DOM nodes and stale component state.
AdvancedIf an item's tracked identity stays constant but its content changes, Angular keeps the existing DOM node and updates bindings in place — preserving focus, scroll position, and animation state. This is why tracking by a mutable field (like a title that a user edits) can subtly disable in-place updates.

What's Next — The ngFor Directive

Objective: Preview the legacy *ngFor structural directive that predates @for and explain why it is still worth learning.

The next lesson repeats the same list-rendering exercise using *ngFor, the predecessor of @for. Because many existing projects mix both syntaxes and will for years, you need to read and write both. After introducing *ngFor, a comparison lesson highlights the advantages of the newer block syntax.

  • *ngFor is the older structural directive replaced by @for.
  • Real codebases contain a mix of both for the foreseeable future.
  • The same starter code will be refactored from separate variables to a loop.

Example — branch 1-components, src/app/app.component.html · reimplemented for clarity.

<!-- coming next: the legacy directive form -->
<course-card *ngFor="let course of courses" [course]="course"></course-card>
OutputPreview only — the following lesson implements this in full.
Common pitfallTreating "legacy" as "irrelevant." Interview questions, older tutorials, and maintenance work all still assume familiarity with *ngFor.
AdvancedStructural directives like *ngFor are syntactic sugar for an <ng-template> wrapper. Understanding that desugaring explains why they need to be imported and why the block syntax, being compiler-native, avoids that overhead.

The Angular ngFor Core Directive

Objective: Loop through a collection with *ngFor and use its auxiliary features (index, first, last, even, odd).

*ngFor is a structural directive — the leading asterisk is shorthand for a template that changes the page structure based on an expression. The looping expression reads exactly like TypeScript iteration. It creates a local loop variable for each item, which you bind into the child. Alongside iteration, *ngFor exposes local values you alias with let: index, and the booleans first, last, even, and odd — the directive equivalents of the @for contextual variables.

  • The * is shorthand structural-directive syntax that rewrites the host into a template.
  • let course of courses declares the per-iteration loop variable.
  • Alias extras with let i = index, let isFirst = first, and so on.
  • Combine several with semicolons: *ngFor="let c of courses; let i = index".
  • Use the boolean flags with [class.x] to style boundary or alternating rows.

Example — branch 1-components, src/app/app.component.html · reimplemented for clarity.

<course-card
  *ngFor="let course of courses;
          let i = index;
          let isFirst = first;
          let isLast = last;
          let isEven = even;
          let isOdd = odd"
  [course]="course"
  [cardIndex]="i + 1"
  [class.ease-first]="isFirst"
  [class.ease-last]="isLast"
  [class.even]="isEven"
  [class.odd]="isOdd">
</course-card>
OutputAll courses render numbered 1..10; the first and last cards get borders and even/odd rows alternate background color.
Common pitfallWriting index directly in the template instead of aliasing it with let i = index. The bare context keys are only accessible through a let alias in *ngFor.
Advanced*ngFor supports trackBy as an optional performance hint: *ngFor="let c of courses; trackBy: trackCourse". Unlike @for's mandatory track, forgetting trackBy compiles fine — which is exactly the silent-performance-trap the newer syntax was designed to prevent.

Comparison — @for vs ngFor

Objective: Weigh the new @for block against the legacy *ngFor directive and learn the automated migration path.

The Angular CLI ships an automatic migration that converts most templates from the old control-flow directives to the new block syntax in one command. It handles @for, @if, @switch, and their predecessors together. For most projects the migration runs cleanly, though a few edge cases may need manual touch-ups; expect it to handle roughly 95% of the work. The new syntax is more concise, reads like JavaScript, needs no imports, and makes tracking mandatory — so it is the recommended default.

  • Run ng generate @angular/core:control-flow to migrate automatically.
  • The migration covers all new control-flow blocks at once.
  • Around 95% converts transparently; some templates may need manual fixes.
  • @for is safer (mandatory tracking) and cleaner than *ngFor.

Example — branch ng17-recordings, project root · reimplemented for clarity.

ng generate @angular/core:control-flow
OutputTemplates across the project are rewritten from *ngFor/*ngIf/*ngSwitch to @for/@if/@switch.
Common pitfallRunning the migration and assuming zero review is needed. Always diff the result — complex templates with aliased context variables or nested directives occasionally need hand-editing.
AdvancedCommit before running the migration so the rewrite lands as an isolated, reviewable changeset. Pair it with the standalone-components and inject-function migrations for a full modernization pass; each is independent and can be applied incrementally.

@if — Section Introduction

Objective: Introduce conditional rendering and preview both the modern @if block and the legacy *ngIf directive.

Conditional rendering shows or hides parts of a template based on data. The lessons follow the established pattern: first the recommended new @if block, then its predecessor *ngIf, and finally a short comparison. Knowing both is essential because both appear in real projects.

  • @if handles conditional rendering; *ngIf is its predecessor.
  • New projects should prefer @if; existing ones still rely on *ngIf.
  • A comparison lesson follows once both have been covered.

Example — branch ng17-recordings, src/app/courses/course-card/course-card.component.html · reimplemented for clarity.

@if (course.iconUrl) {
  <img class="course-image" [src]="course.iconUrl">
}
OutputThe image renders only when the course actually has an icon URL.
Common pitfallConfusing hiding with removing. Both @if and *ngIf remove the element from the DOM entirely — they do not merely hide it with CSS.
AdvancedFor toggling frequently-shown-and-hidden content where teardown is expensive, CSS visibility ([hidden] or display:none) can outperform @if, which destroys and recreates the subtree — and its component instances — on every toggle.

The Angular 17 @if / @else Syntax

Objective: Conditionally render content with @if, add fallbacks with @else, and chain conditions with @else if.

If a course lacks an icon, showing an <img> with a missing source produces a broken-image UI. Guard it with @if, whose truthiness rules match JavaScript — every object (even an empty {} or []) and any non-empty string is truthy; only null, undefined, 0, the empty string, NaN and false are falsy. Provide a fallback with @else (for example a "no image available" placeholder), and handle multiple branches with @else if. The whole construct reads almost exactly like ordinary JavaScript.

  • @if (condition) { … } renders when the expression is truthy.
  • @else { … } supplies a fallback block.
  • @else if (condition) { … } chains additional branches.
  • Truthiness follows JavaScript semantics — objects and non-empty strings are truthy.

Example — branch ng17-recordings, src/app/courses/course-card/course-card.component.html · reimplemented for clarity.

@if (course.id === 1) {
  <div>Course with id one</div>
} @else if (course.iconUrl) {
  <img class="course-image" [src]="course.iconUrl">
} @else {
  <div class="course-image placeholder">No image available</div>
}
OutputCourse #1 shows the special text; courses with an icon show the image; the rest show the placeholder.
Common pitfallRelying on truthiness for numbers. A valid 0 value is falsy, so @if (count) hides the block when the count is legitimately zero — test @if (count != null) instead.
Advanced@if supports binding the evaluated expression to a variable: @if (user$ | async; as user) { {{ user.name }} }. This captures the result once so the template can reuse it without re-evaluating the (possibly async) expression.

What's Next — The ngIf Directive

Objective: Preview the legacy *ngIf directive and the Elvis (safe-navigation) operator covered alongside it.

The next lesson repeats the conditional-rendering exercise with *ngIf, the predecessor of @if, and introduces a related tool: the Elvis operator ?. for safely accessing potentially-undefined properties. After covering *ngIf, a comparison weighs the two syntaxes.

  • *ngIf is the structural-directive predecessor of @if.
  • The same lesson introduces the safe-navigation (Elvis) operator ?..
  • A comparison of both conditional syntaxes follows.

Example — branch 1-components, src/app/courses/course-card/course-card.component.html · reimplemented for clarity.

<!-- coming next: the legacy directive plus safe navigation -->
<img *ngIf="course?.iconUrl" [src]="course.iconUrl">
OutputPreview only — implemented fully in the following lesson.
Common pitfallExpecting ?. to guard against all undefined access. It short-circuits one level; deeply nested chains still need a guard at each risky step or an *ngIf around the whole block.
AdvancedThe template Elvis operator predates TypeScript's own optional-chaining. In class code prefer TypeScript ?.; in templates Angular's version additionally suppresses errors during the brief window before async data arrives.

Angular ngIf Directive and the Elvis Operator

Objective: Conditionally include or exclude DOM with *ngIf, guard against undefined data with the Elvis operator, and use an else template.

*ngIf removes an element from the DOM when its expression is falsy — the content is gone, not merely hidden with CSS. The expression need not be a boolean; strings, objects, or a method call are coerced to a boolean (an undefined object is falsy). When an input object may be undefined, accessing its properties in the template throws; the Elvis operator ?. avoids that by short-circuiting to undefined. A cleaner fix is to wrap the whole card in *ngIf="course" so nothing renders until the data exists. An else clause paired with an <ng-template> supplies a fallback.

  • *ngIf fully removes the element from the DOM when false — verify in dev tools.
  • Any expression is coerced to boolean; undefined/null objects are falsy.
  • The Elvis operator course?.description prevents "cannot read property of undefined" errors.
  • Guarding the outer container with *ngIf="course" beats scattering ?. everywhere.
  • *ngIf="cond; else ref" renders a named <ng-template #ref> when false.

Example — branch 1-components, src/app/courses/course-card/course-card.component.html · reimplemented for clarity.

<!-- guard the whole card so undefined data never renders -->
<div class="course-card" *ngIf="course">
  <h2>{{ course?.description }}</h2>

  <img *ngIf="course.iconUrl; else noImage" [src]="course.iconUrl">
  <ng-template #noImage>
    <div class="placeholder">No image is available</div>
  </ng-template>
</div>
isImageVisible(): boolean {
  return !!this.course && !!this.course.iconUrl;
}
OutputAn undefined item is skipped entirely; cards without an icon show "No image is available"; the rest show their image.
Common pitfallSprinkling ?. across many bindings to silence errors. If the object being undefined means the component should not render at all, a single outer *ngIf is clearer and avoids displaying a half-broken card.
Advanced*ngIf allows binding its result: *ngIf="course as c" exposes the value as c inside the block, which pairs well with the async pipe to unwrap an observable once and reuse it without repeated subscriptions.

Comparison — @if vs ngIf

Objective: Contrast the new @if block with the legacy *ngIf directive and note the automated migration.

The @if block is less verbose and reads like plain JavaScript, so any JavaScript or TypeScript developer understands it immediately. It needs no imports in the template and natively supports @else if chains — something *ngIf handles only awkwardly with template references. It also drops the peculiar *ngIf asterisk syntax. The same CLI control-flow migration converts *ngIf usages automatically, handling most cases with only occasional manual cleanup.

  • @if is more concise, import-free, and JavaScript-like.
  • @else if chains are first-class in @if but clumsy with *ngIf.
  • The same ng generate @angular/core:control-flow migration upgrades *ngIf.
  • Expect most usages to convert automatically; review edge cases.

Example — branch ng17-recordings, src/app/… · reimplemented for clarity.

<!-- legacy: else needs a named template -->
<img *ngIf="course.iconUrl; else noImage" [src]="course.iconUrl">
<ng-template #noImage><div>No image</div></ng-template>

<!-- modern: inline else, no template ref -->
@if (course.iconUrl) {
  <img [src]="course.iconUrl">
} @else {
  <div>No image</div>
}
OutputBoth produce the same rendered result; the block form needs no <ng-template> and no import.
Common pitfallPorting an *ngIf="x as y" alias to @if without adjusting syntax — the block form uses @if (x; as y), and mechanical find-and-replace will not catch it.
AdvancedBecause @if is compiled rather than instantiated as a directive, its else/else-if branches share a single generated conditional, avoiding the extra embedded-view bookkeeping that multiple chained *ngIf directives incur.

The ngClass Core Directive

Objective: Apply CSS classes conditionally with ngClass, using a string, an array, or a configuration object.

ngClass toggles state classes on an element based on data — for example marking a card as a beginner course. It accepts three shapes: a space-separated string of class names, an array of class names, or an object whose keys are class names and whose boolean values decide whether each is applied. The object form is the most useful for conditional styling, and returning it from a component method keeps the template readable. ngClass is meant only for conditional (state) classes; permanent classes belong on the plain class attribute. This lecture is also where component-scoped styling first appears: the beginner class lives in the course-card component's own CSS file — previously empty — rather than in the global styles.css, so Angular's view encapsulation scopes it to this component's template alone.

  • String form: [ngClass]="'beginner highlighted'" applies the listed classes.
  • Array form: [ngClass]="['beginner', 'highlighted']".
  • Object form: [ngClass]="{ beginner: true }" — key is class, value toggles it.
  • Prefer calling a component method that returns the object to keep templates clean.
  • Use ngClass only for conditional classes; keep static classes in class="…".

Example — branch 1-components, src/app/courses/course-card/course-card.component.ts · reimplemented for clarity.

private get isBeginner() {
  return this.course.category === 'BEGINNER';
}

// 1) object form — key is the class, boolean value toggles it
cardClasses() {
  return { beginner: this.isBeginner };
}

// 2) string form — an if-clause returns the class name(s)
cardClassesString() {
  if (this.isBeginner) {
    return 'beginner';
  }
  return '';
}

// 3) array form — same if-clause, returning a string[]
cardClassesArray() {
  if (this.isBeginner) {
    return ['beginner'];
  }
  return [];
}
<!-- any of the three methods binds identically -->
<div class="course-card" [ngClass]="cardClasses()">
  …
</div>
OutputAll three methods produce the same result: only courses whose category is BEGINNER receive the highlighted "beginner" style.
Common pitfallUsing ngClass for classes that are always present. Constant styling should go on the static class attribute — reserve ngClass for data-driven state classes.
AdvancedFor a single conditional class, the native [class.beginner]="isBeginner" binding is lighter than ngClass and needs no directive import. ngClass earns its keep only when several classes toggle together from one computed object.

The ngStyle Core Directive

Objective: Apply inline CSS styles conditionally with ngStyle, and know when to use it versus plain CSS classes.

A single style can be set with the native [style.property] binding, but applying several at once is cleaner with ngStyle, which takes a configuration object mapping CSS properties to values. As with ngClass, returning that object from a component method keeps template logic minimal. ngStyle is for genuinely dynamic properties that depend on data — a classic example is setting a card's background-image from a course's icon URL. For everything else, prefer ordinary CSS classes; use class most, ngClass for state classes, and ngStyle only for data-driven individual properties.

  • Single style: [style.text-decoration]="'underline'".
  • Multiple styles: [ngStyle]="{ 'text-decoration': 'underline' }".
  • Return the style object from a method to keep templates readable.
  • Ideal for dynamic values like background-image: url(...) built from data.
  • Reach for plain CSS classes first; ngStyle is the least-used of the three.

Example — branch 1-components, src/app/courses/course-card/course-card.component.ts · reimplemented for clarity.

cardStyles() {
  return {
    'background-image': `url(${this.course.iconUrl})`
  };
}
<div class="course-card" [ngStyle]="cardStyles()">
  …
</div>
OutputThe card renders the course icon as its CSS background image.
Common pitfallHard-coding constant styles in ngStyle. Static appearance belongs in a stylesheet — reserve ngStyle for properties whose values genuinely change with the data.
AdvancedBoth ngClass and ngStyle run their differ on every change-detection cycle. For a single dynamic property the native [style.background-image] binding is cheaper; ngStyle pays off when a computed object drives several properties together.

@switch — Section Introduction

Objective: Introduce the @switch block for multi-branch conditional rendering and preview its predecessor *ngSwitch.

@switch is to Angular templates what a switch statement is to JavaScript: it selects among several cases based on an expression. As with the other primitives, the lessons present the modern @switch first — easier to read, JavaScript-like, no import required — then the legacy ngSwitch directive. The same CLI migration converts it.

  • @switch renders one of several @case blocks based on an expression.
  • It mirrors a JavaScript switch statement.
  • The legacy equivalent is the ngSwitch directive, covered afterward.

Example — branch ng17-recordings, src/app/courses/course-card/course-card.component.html · reimplemented for clarity.

@switch (course.category) {
  @case ('BEGINNER') { <div class="category">Beginner</div> }
  @default { <div class="category">All Levels</div> }
}
OutputExactly one branch renders per card, chosen by its category value.
Common pitfallExpecting fall-through like a C-style switch. Angular's @switch renders only the matching @case and needs no break.
Advanced@switch uses strict equality (===) to match cases, so a numeric expression will not match a string case. Coerce the expression or the case labels to the same type to avoid an unexpected fall to @default.

The Angular 17 @switch Syntax in Action

Objective: Render different UI per category using @switch, @case, and @default.

Given a course category that can be beginner, intermediate, advanced — or something unexpected — @switch picks one badge to display. You supply the expression to switch on, list a @case per known value, and add a @default for anything unmatched. Each case can contain arbitrarily different markup — its own icon, color, or CSS class. Only the matching branch renders, exactly like a JavaScript switch.

  • @switch (expr) { … } takes the value to match against.
  • Each @case (value) { … } renders when the expression strictly equals that value.
  • @default { … } handles unknown values.
  • No import is needed; it works anywhere in an Angular template.

Example — branch ng17-recordings, src/app/courses/course-card/course-card.component.html · reimplemented for clarity.

<div class="course-category">
  @switch (course.category) {
    @case ('BEGINNER')     { <div class="category">Beginner</div> }
    @case ('INTERMEDIATE') { <div class="category">Intermediate</div> }
    @case ('ADVANCED')     { <div class="category">Advanced</div> }
    @default               { <div class="category">All Levels</div> }
  }
</div>
OutputEach card shows a single category badge; a course with an unrecognized category shows "All Levels".
Common pitfallForgetting a @default. Without it, any value not covered by a @case renders nothing — leaving an invisible gap that is easy to miss.
AdvancedWhen several categories should share one badge, list content in each relevant @case or refactor the shared markup into a small component — @switch has no case-grouping/fall-through, so duplication is handled by composition rather than syntax.

The ngSwitch Core Directive in Detail

Objective: Render multi-branch UI with the legacy [ngSwitch], *ngSwitchCase, and *ngSwitchDefault directives.

Where *ngIf handles a two-way choice, ngSwitch handles many. Apply [ngSwitch] to a container with the expression to evaluate, then mark child elements with *ngSwitchCase for each known value and *ngSwitchDefault for the rest. A case is active when its value strictly matches the switch expression. This lets you show a category-specific badge above the "View Course" button, and a fallback badge when the category is missing or unknown.

  • [ngSwitch]="expr" goes on the parent container.
  • *ngSwitchCase="'BEGINNER'" marks each candidate branch.
  • *ngSwitchDefault covers unmatched values.
  • Its advantage over *ngIf is easy handling of many cases plus a default.

Example — branch 1-components, src/app/courses/course-card/course-card.component.html · reimplemented for clarity.

<div class="course-category" [ngSwitch]="course.category">
  <div class="category" *ngSwitchCase="'BEGINNER'">Beginner</div>
  <div class="category" *ngSwitchCase="'INTERMEDIATE'">Intermediate</div>
  <div class="category" *ngSwitchCase="'ADVANCED'">Advanced</div>
  <div class="category" *ngSwitchDefault>All Levels</div>
</div>
OutputEach card shows its matching category label; a course with the category removed shows "All Levels".
Common pitfallPutting [ngSwitch] and *ngSwitchCase on the same element. The switch expression must live on a parent container, with each case on a child element inside it.
AdvancedngSwitch is one directive ([ngSwitch]) plus two structural directives (*ngSwitchCase, *ngSwitchDefault) that read the switch value via dependency injection from the host. That coupling is why the case directives only work inside an [ngSwitch] host — and why the compiled @switch block is simpler.

The ng-container Core Directive

Objective: Use ng-container as an invisible grouping element to host a structural directive without adding an extra DOM node.

Structural directives need a host element. But sometimes a component has no single wrapping element, or you want to apply a directive across several sibling elements that no natural parent groups. Adding a throwaway <div> just to host the directive pollutes the DOM. ng-container solves this: it is a logical wrapper you can attach *ngIf, *ngFor, or *ngSwitchCase to, but it renders no element of its own, keeping the output DOM lean.

  • ng-container is a grouping element that produces no DOM output.
  • Use it as an anchor for a structural directive when there is no natural host.
  • It works with *ngIf, *ngFor, ngSwitch, and *ngSwitchCase alike.
  • It avoids extra wrapper <div>s and keeps the page lighter.

Example — branch 1-components, src/app/courses/course-card/course-card.component.html · reimplemented for clarity.

<div class="course-category" [ngSwitch]="course.category">
  <ng-container *ngSwitchCase="'BEGINNER'">
    <div class="category">Beginner</div>
    <span class="badge">New</span>
  </ng-container>
  <div class="category" *ngSwitchDefault>All Levels</div>
</div>
OutputBoth inner elements render for the beginner case, with no extra wrapper node in the DOM.
Common pitfallReaching for a wrapper <div> just to host a directive. That injects a real element that can break flexbox/grid layouts — ng-container hosts the directive invisibly.
Advancedng-container is also the idiomatic host for the *ngTemplateOutlet directive and for combining two structural directives (which cannot both sit on one element) — put one on a real element and the other on a surrounding ng-container.

Angular Built-In Pipes — Complete Catalog

Objective: Transform display values in templates with Angular's built-in pipes, applied via the | operator with optional arguments.

A pipe is a template mechanism that transforms data into a different presentation form. Apply one with a vertical bar and the pipe name; pass arguments after a colon, separating multiple arguments with further colons. Angular ships a rich catalog: date for formatting dates, number for numeric precision, currency and percent for money and ratios, the string pipes uppercase/lowercase/titlecase, slice for arrays, json for debugging, keyvalue for iterating object properties, and async for unwrapping observables and promises.

Three of these reward a closer look. The number pipe takes a format string shaped as minIntegerDigits.minFractionDigits-maxFractionDigits, so '3.3-5' pads the whole-number part to at least three digits and shows between three and five decimals, rounding as needed. The currency pipe defaults to US dollars ($) — pass an ISO code such as 'EUR' to change it. And keyvalue exists because a plain JavaScript object is not iterable: @for and *ngFor cannot loop over it directly. The pipe converts the object into an array of { key, value } pairs the loop can walk, sorting the keys alphabetically by default.

  • date:'MM/dd/yyyy' — format a Date; more Ms spell out the month name.
  • number:'3.3-5' — min integer digits, then min-max fraction digits.
  • currency:'EUR' and percent — money and percentage formatting.
  • uppercase / lowercase / titlecase — string casing.
  • slice:0:2 — subset an array; the end index is exclusive.
  • json — dump a value for debugging; keyvalue — loop an object's entries.
  • async — subscribe to and unwrap an observable/promise in the template.

Example — branch ng17-recordings, src/app/app.component.html · reimplemented for clarity.

export class AppComponent {
  startDate = new Date(2000, 0, 1); // Jan 1, 2000 (months are zero-based)
  price = 9.99;
  rate = 0.67;
  title = 'Angular Core Deepdive';
  courses = COURSES;
  course = COURSES[0];
}
<p>{{ startDate | date }}</p>
<p>{{ startDate | date:'MM/dd/yy' }}</p>
<p>{{ startDate | date:'MMMM d, yyyy' }}</p>

<p>{{ title | uppercase }}</p>
<p>{{ title | titlecase }}</p>

<p>{{ price | number:'3.3-5' }}</p>
<p>{{ price | currency:'EUR' }}</p>
<p>{{ rate | percent }}</p>

<p>{{ (courses | slice:0:2).length }} shown</p>
<pre>{{ courses | json }}</pre>

@for (pair of course | keyvalue; track pair.key) {
  <div>{{ pair.key }}: {{ pair.value }}</div>
}
OutputJan 1, 2000 → "01/01/00" and "January 1, 2000"; title → "ANGULAR CORE DEEPDIVE" / "Angular Core Deepdive"; 9.99 → "009.990" and "€9.99"; 0.67 → "67%"; slice keeps only indices 0–1; json dumps the array; keyvalue lists each Course property.
Common pitfallPassing a raw string date to the date pipe. It expects a Date, a timestamp number, or an ISO-8601 string — non-standard date strings throw or render nothing.
AdvancedMost built-in pipes are pure: they recompute only when their input reference changes, which is why mutating an array in place (without a new reference) can leave a piped view stale. async is an impure pipe that also manages subscription lifecycle — it unsubscribes automatically when the component is destroyed, preventing memory leaks.
Section cheat sheet
  • Component: @Component({ selector, standalone, templateUrl }) defines a reusable custom element.
  • @Input: parent → child, [course]="c"; add { required: true } for compile-time safety.
  • @Output: child → parent, @Output() sel = new EventEmitter<Course>(); parent binds (sel)="on($event)". Custom events do not bubble.
  • Loop: new @for (c of list; track c.id) {} (track mandatory) vs legacy *ngFor="let c of list; trackBy: fn".
  • Loop context: $index $count $first $last $even $odd and @empty (block) ≈ index first last even odd aliases (directive).
  • Condition: new @if / @else if / @else vs legacy *ngIf="x; else ref" + <ng-template #ref>. Both remove from the DOM.
  • Switch: new @switch/@case/@default vs legacy [ngSwitch] + *ngSwitchCase/*ngSwitchDefault.
  • Styling: [ngClass] for conditional state classes, [ngStyle] for dynamic properties; static styling stays on class.
  • Grouping: ng-container hosts a directive with no DOM output.
  • Pipes: date number currency percent uppercase lowercase titlecase slice json keyvalue async via value | pipe:arg1:arg2.
  • Migrate: ng generate @angular/core:control-flow upgrades *ngIf/*ngFor/*ngSwitch to block syntax.

Quick check

  1. Which decorator lets a child component emit a custom event that a parent can handle?

    • A. @Input() on an EventEmitter
    • B. @Output() on an EventEmitter
    • C. @ViewChild() on a method
    • D. @HostListener() on the class
    AnswerB. An @Output()-decorated EventEmitter exposes a bindable custom event; without the decorator the parent binding never fires.

  2. What happens if you write a @for block without a track expression?

    • A. It works but re-renders the whole list every change
    • B. It silently tracks by object identity
    • C. It produces a compile-time error
    • D. It falls back to tracking by $index
    AnswerC. Unlike *ngFor's optional trackBy, @for requires track and fails to compile without it — a deliberate safeguard against silent performance problems.

  3. When should you reach for ng-container?

    • A. To apply global CSS styles to a component
    • B. To host a structural directive without adding an extra DOM element
    • C. To lazy-load a feature module
    • D. To register a component in the imports array
    AnswerB. ng-container is an invisible grouping element — it hosts directives like *ngIf or *ngSwitchCase while emitting no DOM node of its own.

Angular Local Template Querying In Depth

A component template can wire up interactions on its own through template reference variables, but sooner or later the component class itself needs a direct, programmatic handle on one of those template elements. This section covers the two decorators that grant that handle — @ViewChild for a single match and @ViewChildren for a collection — plus the query configuration options, the timing rules enforced by the AfterViewInit lifecycle hook, the reactive QueryList returned for collections, and the modern signal-based equivalents.

How the @ViewChild Decorator Works

Objective: Understand what a view query is, why a component class sometimes needs a reference to a template element, and how @ViewChild resolves that reference.

Template reference variables are enough when the interaction stays inside the markup — one element reacts to another directly in the template. Often, though, the TypeScript class behind the template needs its own reference to a rendered element or child component: to call a method on it, read a property, or drive some imperative behaviour. A view query is the mechanism that pulls a template element up into the class. The simplest form is the @ViewChild decorator: you declare a class field, annotate it with @ViewChild, and pass a locator describing what to grab. When you pass a component class as the locator, the field is populated with the matching component instance.

  • Template reference variables interact within the markup; view queries expose elements to the component class.
  • @ViewChild(SomeComponent) resolves to the matching component instance, not its DOM node.
  • If several elements match the same query, @ViewChild keeps only the first match in document order.
  • The decorated field is not ready in the constructor — it is filled after the view is built (see lecture 3).

Source: branch ng17-recordings · reimplemented for modern standalone Angular.

import { Component, ViewChild } from '@angular/core';
import { CourseCardComponent } from './course-card/course-card.component';
import { COURSES } from './db-data';

@Component({
  selector: 'app-root',
  standalone: true,
  imports: [CourseCardComponent],
  template: `
    <app-course-card [course]="courses[0]" />
    <button (click)="onViewClicked()">View card</button>
  `,
})
export class AppComponent {
  courses = COURSES;

  // Locator = component class → resolves to the instance
  @ViewChild(CourseCardComponent)
  card!: CourseCardComponent;

  onViewClicked() {
    console.log('card instance:', this.card);
  }
}
Console — after clicking "View card"card instance: CourseCardComponent {
  course: { id: 1, description: 'Angular Core Deep Dive', ... },
  cardIndex: ''
}

Notice cardIndex logs as an empty string rather than a real value: that is the child's own @Input() default kicking in because the parent never bound [cardIndex]. The query hands back the instance exactly as Angular constructed it — defaults and all. And logging the reference is only the demonstration; the reason to hold the instance is to drive it imperatively. Because this.card is the live CourseCardComponent, the class can read its state or call its methods directly:

onViewClicked() {
  // imperative use — the whole point of grabbing the instance
  console.log(this.card.course.description); // read a property
  this.card.highlight();                     // call a method on the child
}
Console — after clicking "View card"Angular Core Deep Dive
// highlight() runs on the live child instance
Pitfall Reading the field too early returns undefined. In the constructor the view has not been created yet, so this.card is not populated. Also remember the "first match only" rule: if two <app-course-card> elements are present, a class-based @ViewChild ignores the second one entirely.
Advanced: the signal-based viewChild()

Modern Angular offers a reactive alternative that returns a signal instead of relying on a decorated field. It reads more predictably and plays well with computed() and effect().

import { Component, viewChild } from '@angular/core';

export class AppComponent {
  // Signal query — call it to read the value
  card = viewChild(CourseCardComponent);
  // required variant errors if nothing matches:
  // card = viewChild.required(CourseCardComponent);

  onViewClicked() {
    console.log(this.card()); // invoke the signal
  }
}

View Query Configuration Options: locators and read

Objective: Learn the different ways to locate a query target — by component class, by template reference variable, or a plain DOM element — and how the read option changes what you get back.

Beyond the component-class locator, @ViewChild accepts a template reference variable name (as a string). This is invaluable when the page has several elements of the same type and you want to target one specifically. The decorator can also grab ordinary DOM elements: query a reference on a plain <div> and you receive an ElementRef, whose nativeElement property is the actual DOM node. The distinction matters: querying a component gives the component instance by default, while querying a native element gives the element wrapper. When you query a component but actually want its host DOM element, pass the read: ElementRef option to override the default.

  • Locator by class: @ViewChild(CourseCardComponent). Locator by reference: @ViewChild('cardRef').
  • A query on a plain element yields an ElementRef; access the node via .nativeElement.
  • Querying a component returns its instance by default; { read: ElementRef } returns the component's host element instead.
  • Each element has its own reference name, so multiple similar elements can be queried independently.

Source: branch ng17-recordings · reimplemented for modern standalone Angular.

<app-course-card #cardOne [course]="courses[0]" />
<app-course-card #cardTwo [course]="courses[1]" />

<div #container class="courses">
  <!-- plain DOM element to query -->
</div>
import { Component, ViewChild, ElementRef } from '@angular/core';

export class AppComponent {
  // by reference name → component instances
  @ViewChild('cardOne') cardOne!: CourseCardComponent;
  @ViewChild('cardTwo') cardTwo!: CourseCardComponent;

  // plain element → ElementRef
  @ViewChild('container') containerDiv!: ElementRef;

  // component, but read its host element instead of the instance
  @ViewChild(CourseCardComponent, { read: ElementRef })
  cardEl!: ElementRef;
}
ngAfterViewInit() {
  console.log(this.cardOne.course.description); // first course
  console.log(this.cardTwo.course.description); // second course
  console.log(this.containerDiv.nativeElement); // real <div>
  console.log(this.cardEl.nativeElement);       // <app-course-card> host
}
ConsoleAngular Core Deep Dive
Angular for Beginners
<div class="courses">…</div>
<app-course-card>…</app-course-card>
Pitfall The query string is the template reference variable name, not the class field name. Renaming your TypeScript field does not change the locator — @ViewChild('container') matches #container in the template regardless of what the field is called. Also, a component's host element (<app-course-card>) is a real DOM node, distinct from the component instance; choose read: ElementRef deliberately when you need the node.
Advanced: other read tokens

read is not limited to ElementRef. You can read any injectable exposed at the element's node — a TemplateRef on an <ng-template>, a ViewContainerRef, or a directive instance applied to the same element. This lets one query target and extract a specific provider rather than the default resolution.

@ViewChild('tpl', { read: TemplateRef }) tpl!: TemplateRef<unknown>;
@ViewChild('slot', { read: ViewContainerRef }) slot!: ViewContainerRef;

The AfterViewInit Hook and How Deep @ViewChild Queries

Objective: Determine the earliest moment a view query is populated, why ngAfterViewInit is the right place for element initialisation, and the strict scope limits of local template querying.

View queries are not resolved when the constructor runs — at construction time the view has not been built, so the fields are still undefined. The framework fills them once the component's view is fully created, and it signals that moment by calling ngAfterViewInit. Implement the AfterViewInit interface and this method becomes the earliest safe place to touch queried elements and run initialisation that depends on them. Angular calls it for you; it is a lifecycle hook, never something you invoke yourself. One strict boundary applies to all of this: view queries are local. They see only the elements declared in the current component's own template. They cannot reach down into a child component's template, and they cannot look outward at the parent that hosts the component.

  • Queried fields are undefined in the constructor; they are ready in ngAfterViewInit.
  • ngAfterViewInit fires after the view is built and is the natural spot for element-dependent setup.
  • Scope is strictly local: no querying into child templates, no querying the parent template.
  • Do not synchronously mutate rendered data inside the hook — it breaks one-way data flow.

Source: branch ng17-recordings · reimplemented for modern standalone Angular.

import { Component, ViewChild, ElementRef, AfterViewInit } from '@angular/core';

export class AppComponent implements AfterViewInit {
  @ViewChild('container') containerDiv!: ElementRef;

  constructor() {
    // too early — the view does not exist yet
    console.log('ctor:', this.containerDiv); // undefined
  }

  ngAfterViewInit() {
    // ready: safe to read and initialise
    console.log('afterViewInit:', this.containerDiv.nativeElement);
  }
}
Consolector: undefined
afterViewInit: <div class="courses">…</div>
// Trying to query DEEPER than the local template fails.
// #courseImage lives inside CourseCardComponent's template,
// not inside AppComponent's template.
@ViewChild('courseImage') courseImage!: ElementRef;

ngAfterViewInit() {
  console.log(this.courseImage); // out of scope
}
Consoleundefined
// child-component internals are invisible to the parent's query
Pitfall: ExpressionChangedAfterItHasBeenCheckedError Mutating data that the view already rendered — synchronously, inside ngAfterViewInit — throws ExpressionChangedAfterItHasBeenCheckedError. Angular has already rendered the view against one value and now, in the same change-detection pass, sees a different one; it cannot decide which to honour — the value it painted, or the value you just mutated. Worse, re-rendering to reconcile could trigger yet another synchronous mutation, spiralling into a continuous re-render loop. Rather than risk that, Angular refuses, preserving its guarantee of unidirectional (one-way) data flow: a change-detection pass moves in a single direction and never loops back on itself within the same turn. The check runs only in development mode — it is compiled out of production builds — but a violation almost always points at a genuine design problem. If you must change bound data from this hook, defer it to a later turn (for example with an async operation) so it lands in a fresh change-detection cycle. 
ngAfterViewInit() {
  // ❌ throws — mutates already-rendered data in the same turn
  // this.courses[0].description = 'changed';

  // ✅ safe — runs in a later VM turn
  setTimeout(() => { this.courses[0].description = 'changed'; });
}
Advanced: querying a component's own content vs. its view

@ViewChild targets elements a component renders itself (its view). Elements projected into a component through <ng-content> belong to a different query family — @ContentChild / @ContentChildren, resolved in ngAfterContentInit. Signal queries mirror both: viewChild()/viewChildren() for the rendered view and contentChild()/contentChildren() for projected content. Keeping the two straight explains why a "missing" query is often just aimed at the wrong tree.

The @ViewChildren Decorator and QueryList

Objective: Query a collection of matching elements with @ViewChildren, work with the returned QueryList, and react to collection changes through its changes observable.

Where @ViewChild returns a single match, @ViewChildren returns them all. The result is not a plain array but a QueryList — a specialised, iterable container filled at ngAfterViewInit. It exposes familiar array-like members (first, last, length, map, forEach) plus something an array does not have: a changes observable. For many learners this is the first RxJS Observable they meet in the Angular API — a stream you subscribe() to, that emits values over time rather than returning one synchronously. Subscribing to changes lets you react whenever the matched set is modified — for example when @for adds or removes items. It stays quiet until an actual modification occurs, then emits the updated QueryList. Because it is a real subscription, in a long-lived component you would eventually unsubscribe (or lean on the signal-based API below, which manages that for you). As with @ViewChild, the read option applies: read ElementRef to get a list of DOM nodes instead of component instances.

  • @ViewChildren(CourseCardComponent) yields a QueryList<CourseCardComponent>, not an array.
  • QueryList provides first, last, length, map, filter, find, reduce, some, forEach, get, toArray(), and the changes observable.
  • changes emits only on collection modification, re-emitting the updated QueryList.
  • { read: ElementRef } turns the results into native DOM element references.

Source: branch ng17-recordings · reimplemented for modern standalone Angular.

import { Component, ViewChildren, QueryList, AfterViewInit } from '@angular/core';

@Component({
  selector: 'app-root',
  standalone: true,
  imports: [CourseCardComponent],
  template: `
