    <button (click)="onCoursesEdited()">Edit courses</button>
    @for (course of courses; track course.id) {
      <app-course-card [course]="course" />
    }
  `,
})
export class AppComponent implements AfterViewInit {
  courses = [...COURSES];

  @ViewChildren(CourseCardComponent)
  cards!: QueryList<CourseCardComponent>;

  ngAfterViewInit() {
    console.log('first:', this.cards.first.course.description);
    console.log('count:', this.cards.length);

    this.cards.changes.subscribe(list =>
      console.log('collection changed → length', list.length));
  }

  onCoursesEdited() {
    // push a new item → triggers changes.
    // Give it a FRESH id: the template uses `track course.id`, so copying
    // courses[0] verbatim would repeat id 1 and throw a duplicate-key
    // error (NG0955). A unique id keeps the @for keys distinct.
    this.courses.push({ ...this.courses[0], id: this.courses.length + 1 });
  }
}
Consolefirst: Angular Core Deep Dive
count: 10
// (silence until the collection is modified)

// after clicking "Edit courses":
collection changed → length 11
// Read DOM elements instead of component instances
@ViewChildren(CourseCardComponent, { read: ElementRef })
cardEls!: QueryList<ElementRef>;

ngAfterViewInit() {
  console.log(this.cardEls.first); // ElementRef, not CourseCardComponent
  this.cardEls.forEach(el => console.log(el.nativeElement.tagName));
}
ConsoleElementRef { nativeElement: <app-course-card> }
APP-COURSE-CARD
APP-COURSE-CARD
…

A QueryList is iterable but not a real Array (Array.isArray(this.cards) is false). It is not limited to map/forEach, though — it defines its own filter, find, reduce, some, get, and toArray methods, so filter/reduce run directly on it. Reach for toArray() only when you need a genuine Array — to hand to code that expects one, or to call methods QueryList lacks (slice, sort, indexOf, flatMap, …):

ngAfterViewInit() {
  // filter/reduce run directly on the QueryList -- no toArray() needed
  const rest = this.cards.filter(c => c.course.id > 1);   // CourseCardComponent[]
  const count = this.cards.reduce((n, _c) => n + 1, 0);   // 10

  // toArray() only when you need a genuine Array method QueryList lacks
  const sorted = this.cards.toArray().sort((a, b) => a.course.id - b.course.id);
  console.log('total:', count, 'filtered:', rest.length);
}
Consoletotal: 10 filtered: 9
Pitfall changes does not fire for the initial population — the list already exists by ngAfterViewInit, so your first read must be direct, not via the subscription. Also, a QueryList is not an Array: Array.isArray(this.cards) is false. It does define its own map, filter, find, reduce, some, forEach, get, and toArray, so those run directly on it — only methods it lacks (slice, sort, indexOf, …) need this.cards.toArray() first.
Advanced: the signal-based viewChildren()

The reactive counterpart returns a signal of a read-only array, so change detection tracks it automatically and you no longer subscribe to changes manually. Read it by calling the signal, and derive from it with computed().

import { Component, viewChildren, computed } from '@angular/core';

export class AppComponent {
  cards = viewChildren(CourseCardComponent);         // Signal<readonly CourseCardComponent[]>
  cardCount = computed(() => this.cards().length);    // recomputes reactively

  logFirst() {
    console.log(this.cards()[0]);
  }
}

Signal queries also drop the static flag entirely: they are always resolved reactively, so there is no decorator-era { static: true } decision to make.

Section cheat sheet — view queries at a glance
  • Single match: @ViewChild(locator, options?) → first match only.
  • Many matches: @ViewChildren(locator, options?) → QueryList<T>.
  • Locators: component class, template reference name (string), or a directive/token.
  • read: ElementRef for the DOM node, TemplateRef/ViewContainerRef/directive for other providers.
  • static: { static: true } is resolved in the first change-detection pass, so the value is available in ngOnInit (only for non-conditional, single elements); default false resolves in ngAfterViewInit.
  • Timing: constructor = too early; ngAfterViewInit = ready.
  • QueryList: first, last, length, map, filter, find, reduce, some, forEach, get, toArray(), changes observable.
  • Scope: local only — never into child templates, never up to the parent.
  • Signals: viewChild() / viewChild.required() / viewChildren() — reactive, no static flag.
  • Don't: mutate rendered data synchronously in ngAfterViewInit (ExpressionChanged… error).

Quick check — 3 questions

1. A component declares @ViewChild(CardComponent) card, and the template renders two <app-card> elements. What does card hold?

Show answer
  1. An array of both card instances
  2. The first matching card instance only
  3. The last matching card instance
  4. undefined, because there is more than one match

Answer: b. @ViewChild resolves to a single value — the first match in document order. Use @ViewChildren to capture the whole set.

2. You query a component instance but need its host DOM element instead. Which option achieves that?

Show answer
  1. { static: true }
  2. { descendants: true }
  3. { read: ElementRef }
  4. { host: true }

Answer: c. { read: ElementRef } overrides the default resolution and returns the element wrapper; its nativeElement is the actual node.

3. When is a @ViewChildren QueryList first safely readable, and when does its changes observable emit?

Show answer
  1. In the constructor; changes emits once immediately on init
  2. In ngAfterViewInit; changes emits only when the collection is later modified
  3. In ngOnInit; changes emits on every change-detection run
  4. Never automatically; you must call resolve() yourself

Answer: b. The list is populated by ngAfterViewInit (read it directly there), and changes stays silent until an actual add/remove modifies the matched set.

Angular Content Projection In Depth

A component's template is normally fixed: it decides exactly what markup it renders. Content projection flips that by letting a parent pass arbitrary markup into a child, which the child then splices into its own template through <ng-content>. This section builds up from a single default slot, to multiple named slots selected by CSS, and then to querying that projected content programmatically with @ContentChild and @ContentChildren (fired at the right time by AfterContentInit). Throughout, keep one distinction in mind — a view child lives in a component's own template, whereas a content child is markup handed in from outside and dropped into a projection slot.

1 · Content Projection with ng-content

Objective: Make a component configurable from the outside by projecting caller-supplied markup into it with <ng-content> — first a single default slot, then multiple named slots chosen with select.

A component whose template is hard-coded can only ever render what its author baked in. Consider a course-card component that always shows a fixed <img>. What if one caller wants a plain thumbnail, another wants an interactive element with buttons, and a third wants no image at all? Rewriting the template with a pile of @if branches does not scale. The cleaner answer is to let the caller supply that fragment and have the card simply host it.

To do that, the card removes the fixed markup from its own template and instead declares a landing spot: <ng-content></ng-content>. Whatever the parent writes between the card's opening and closing tags becomes the card's projected content, and Angular transplants it into that slot. This is the origin of the name — content authored in the parent is projected down into the child's template.

A single bare <ng-content> is greedy: it captures everything between the tags. Often you want finer control — route the image to one region and the description to another. The select attribute accepts a CSS selector (element name, .class, or attribute) and restricts a slot to matching top-level nodes. You can declare several selective slots plus one selector-less slot; the plain slot acts as a catch-all that receives any projected node not claimed by a select.

Aside (a warm-up from the same lecture, unrelated to projection): before wiring up the slots, the instructor aliases a single course for cleaner markup with *ngIf="courses[0] as course" — this binds courses[0] to a local template variable course so the surrounding markup can read course.iconUrl and course.description without repeating the index. The modern control-flow spelling is @if (courses[0]; as course) { … }.

  • Purpose: content projection builds configurable, reusable components whose look is customised by the caller.
  • Default slot: a bare <ng-content> projects all caller markup into one place.
  • Named slots: <ng-content select=".foo"> projects only nodes matching the selector; supports element, class, and attribute selectors.
  • Catch-all: one selector-less slot mops up whatever no other slot matched.
  • Containers project too: a wrapping <div class="foo"> with children inside is projected whole when its class matches.

Source: paraphrased from lecture "Angular Content Projection with ng-content In Detail" (branch ng17-recordings); examples reimplemented, not copied.

<!-- course-card.component.html : declares the slots -->
<div class="card">
  <ng-content select=".course-image"></ng-content>
  <ng-content select=".course-description"></ng-content>
  <!-- catch-all: anything the two above did not match -->
  <ng-content></ng-content>
  <h5>{{ course.lessonsCount }} lessons</h5>
</div>

<!-- app.component.html : the caller fills the slots -->
<course-card [course]="course">
  <img class="course-image" [src]="course.iconUrl">
  <p class="course-description">{{ course.description }}</p>
  <textarea>edit description…</textarea>
</course-card>
Rendered result[ image ] → .course-image slot [ description ] → .course-description slot [ textarea ] → default catch-all slot "10 lessons" (from card's own template) A second card with no <img> simply leaves the image slot empty — no error.

Selectors are not limited to classes. The lecture first projects by element type — <ng-content select="img"> claims any top-level <img> the caller writes, with no class needed — and then projects a whole container: wrap the image in <div class="course-image"> and the entire div (image and all) lands in the slot as one unit when its class matches. A selective slot is exact, though — an <h5> total-lessons heading written alongside is not swept up by select=".course-image"; it falls through to a catch-all, or is dropped if none exists.

<!-- child template: an element slot and a container slot -->
<div class="card">
  <ng-content select="img"></ng-content>              <!-- (a) by element type -->
  <ng-content select=".course-image"></ng-content>   <!-- (b) whole container -->
  <ng-content></ng-content>                          <!-- catch-all -->
</div>

<!-- caller for (b): the whole div is projected as a single unit -->
<course-card>
  <div class="course-image">
    <img [src]="course.iconUrl">
  </div>
  <h5>{{ course.lessonsCount }} total lessons</h5>
</course-card>
Where each node landsselect="img" → a bare top-level <img> (the img above is NESTED, so it does not match this slot) select=".course-image" → the whole <div> (its img rides along) <h5> total lessons → not matched by .course-image → caught by the selector-less slot

Improved, self-contained example: a reusable panel with header / body / footer regions. Because slots are matched by attribute, callers can use semantic HTML in any order.

import { Component } from '@angular/core';

@Component({
  selector: 'ui-panel',
  standalone: true,
  template: `
    <section class="panel">
      <header><ng-content select="[panel-title]"></ng-content></header>
      <div class="panel-body"><ng-content></ng-content></div>
      <footer><ng-content select="[panel-actions]"></ng-content></footer>
    </section>`,
  styles: `.panel{border:1px solid #ccc;border-radius:8px}
           header{font-weight:700;padding:8px}
           .panel-body{padding:8px} footer{padding:8px;text-align:right}`
})
export class PanelComponent {}

// usage:
// <ui-panel>
//   <span panel-title>Billing</span>
//   <p>Your plan renews on the 1st.</p>
//   <button panel-actions>Manage</button>
// </ui-panel>
Console / DOM┌─────────────────────────┐ │ Billing │ ← [panel-title] ├─────────────────────────┤ │ Your plan renews… │ ← default slot ├─────────────────────────┤ │ [Manage] │ ← [panel-actions] └─────────────────────────┘
Pitfall select only matches top-level projected nodes, and each projected node lands in exactly one slot. If two <ng-content> tags share the same selector, only the last one renders the content — the earlier duplicate stays empty. Also, projected content is not duplicated across slots: you cannot show the same node in two places.

Advanced: projection is not the same as *ngIf-hiding

Projected nodes are created and owned by the parent, not the child. Even when a child places <ng-content> inside an @if that is false, the projected content is still instantiated once (its lifecycle hooks run) — Angular just does not attach it to the DOM. That has consequences for expensive projected components; prefer @defer or passing an ng-template (covered in the Templates section) when you need truly lazy creation. Also note Angular projects by static selector match at compile time; you cannot switch a node's target slot at runtime by changing its class.

2 · The @ContentChild Decorator In Depth

Objective: Obtain a programmatic reference from the component class to a single projected node using @ContentChild, and understand why @ViewChild cannot see projected content.

@ContentChild is the projection-aware sibling of @ViewChild. Both let a component class hold a direct reference to an element, but they query different regions. @ViewChild searches the component's own template. @ContentChild searches only the projected content — the markup passed in between the component's tags — and nothing else.

The lecture demonstrates the boundary concretely. Put a template reference such as #courseImage on the projected <img> and try to grab it with @ViewChild('courseImage'). In ngAfterViewInit the value is undefined: from the card's perspective that image is not part of its view, it lives inside <ng-content>. (In the recording the log prints undefined twice, because two course cards sit on the page and each runs its own query; removing the second card is what gives the clean single log.) Swap the decorator to @ContentChild('courseImage') and the same reference resolves correctly — a reference to the projected image's DOM element.

The scope cuts both ways. A @ContentChild query cannot reach up into the parent's surrounding template (e.g. a top menu), nor down into the card's own fixed template. It sees exactly the content sandwiched between the host component instance's opening and closing tags. Like @ViewChild, it can query by template reference name or by component type. With no read option, the query returns whatever it matched: a component/directive match yields that instance, whereas a plain element (e.g. an element template reference like #courseImage on an <img>) yields an ElementRef. Pass { read: ElementRef } to force the native element, or { read: SomeToken } to read a specific provider.

  • Scope: @ContentChild queries only projected content; @ViewChild queries only the component's own view. Neither crosses into the other's territory.
  • Query by: template reference string, or a component/directive class.
  • read option: with no read, the query returns what it matched (component/directive instance, or ElementRef for a plain element); { read: ElementRef } forces the native element.
  • Timing: read results in ngAfterContentInit (or later) — not in the constructor or ngOnInit.
  • Rule of thumb: only reach for @ContentChild when your component uses projection and genuinely needs a class-level handle on the projected node.

Source: paraphrased from lecture "Angular Content Child Decorator - In-Depth Explanation" (branch ng17-recordings); examples reimplemented, not copied.

The queries below all reference a small CourseImageComponent — the projected child the lecture scaffolds with ng generate component course-image. It is worth seeing on its own first, because it also demonstrates the input alias: the internal class field is named imageUrl, yet callers bind to it under the public name src.

import { Component, Input } from '@angular/core';

@Component({
  selector: 'course-image',
  standalone: true,
  template: `<img class="thumb" [src]="imageUrl">`
})
export class CourseImageComponent {
  // internal field name is imageUrl, but the PUBLIC input name is "src"
  @Input('src') imageUrl = '';
}

// usage — bind to the ALIAS, not the field name:
// <course-image [src]="course.iconUrl"></course-image>
// (writing [imageUrl]="…" would NOT bind — the public name is src)
Input aliasinternal field : imageUrl public name : src ← @Input('src') caller binds : [src]="course.iconUrl"
import {
  Component, ContentChild, AfterContentInit, ElementRef
} from '@angular/core';
import { CourseImageComponent } from './course-image.component';

@Component({ selector: 'course-card', standalone: true,
  template: `<div class="card"><ng-content></ng-content></div>` })
export class CourseCardComponent implements AfterContentInit {

  // by template reference — returns the projected DOM element
  @ContentChild('courseImage') imageEl!: ElementRef;

  // by component type — returns the projected component INSTANCE
  @ContentChild(CourseImageComponent) image!: CourseImageComponent;

  // by type, but read the native element instead of the instance
  @ContentChild(CourseImageComponent, { read: ElementRef })
  imageHost!: ElementRef;

  ngAfterContentInit() {
    console.log('ref  :', this.imageEl?.nativeElement);
    console.log('inst :', this.image);        // CourseImageComponent {}
    console.log('host :', this.imageHost?.nativeElement);
  }
}
Console@ViewChild('courseImage') → undefined (image is projected, not in the view) @ContentChild('courseImage') ref : <img class="thumb" …> inst : CourseImageComponent {} host : <course-image>…</course-image>

Improved example: a tabs container that reads its projected active tab component to drive a headline, showing type-based @ContentChild.

@Component({ selector: 'app-tab', standalone: true,
  template: `<ng-content></ng-content>` })
export class TabComponent { @Input() label = ''; active = false; }

@Component({ selector: 'app-tabs', standalone: true,
  template: `
    <h4>Current: {{ first?.label }}</h4>
    <ng-content></ng-content>` })
export class TabsComponent implements AfterContentInit {
  @ContentChild(TabComponent) first?: TabComponent;
  ngAfterContentInit() {
    if (this.first) this.first.active = true;   // activate the first tab
  }
}
// <app-tabs><app-tab label="Overview">…</app-tab>…</app-tabs>
RenderedCurrent: Overview (the first projected <app-tab> is marked active by the container, without the caller wiring anything up)
Pitfall Reading a @ContentChild too early yields undefined. The constructor and ngOnInit run before projected content is initialised — you must wait for ngAfterContentInit. And remember: @ContentChild returns undefined, not an error, when nothing matches, so a wrong selector fails silently.

Advanced: static content queries and signal-based queries

Classic @ContentChild accepts a { static: true } option, exactly like a view query — it is not inert for content. If the projected node you query sits in a static position (it is not wrapped in a structural directive such as @if or @for), a static: true content query resolves once before the first change-detection pass and is already readable in ngOnInit, just as a static view query is. The reason you rarely reach for it is pragmatic rather than mechanical: projected content is usually dynamic — a caller can wrap it in @if or generate it with @for — and any such query is then forced to defer to ngAfterContentInit. So ngAfterContentInit stays the safe default, but do not assume static: true has no effect at all. In modern Angular you can replace the decorator entirely with the signal query contentChild(CourseImageComponent), which returns a Signal you read reactively (no lifecycle hook needed) and which integrates cleanly with computed() and effect().

3 · @ContentChildren and the AfterContentInit Hook

Objective: Query a collection of projected nodes with @ContentChildren, read them safely in ngAfterContentInit, and cement the view-children vs content-children contrast.

Where @ContentChild grabs one projected node, @ContentChildren grabs all matching ones as a QueryList. If a caller projects three <course-image> components, a single-child query returns only the first; the plural decorator returns a QueryList<CourseImageComponent> of length three. A QueryList is an iterable, observable container — you can loop it, call toArray(), read .length, and subscribe to its changes stream when projected content is added or removed dynamically.

Timing matters more here. Content queries are guaranteed populated only from ngAfterContentInit onward — the first moment Angular promises that content-level queries (@ContentChild, @ContentChildren) have run. Reading them in ngAfterViewInit happens to work in simple cases but is conceptually the wrong hook; ngAfterContentInit is the earliest safe point. As with the singular form, { read: ElementRef } switches the collection from component instances to native DOM elements.

One subtle point from the lecture: a content query targets the content region, not the projection outcome. Even if the card's template has no <ng-content> at all — so nothing is actually displayed — @ContentChildren still reports the projected nodes, because it inspects what was passed between the tags, independent of whether the template chose to render it.

View children vs content children — the core contrast:

  • View children (@ViewChild/@ViewChildren): elements the component declares in its own template. Resolved in ngAfterViewInit.
  • Content children (@ContentChild/@ContentChildren): elements passed in by the parent and projected via <ng-content>. Resolved in ngAfterContentInit.
  • Ownership: the view belongs to the component; content belongs to the parent that supplied it.
  • When to use each: reach for a content query when your component uses projection and needs a handle on what was projected; reach for a view query for your own template's elements.

Source: paraphrased from lecture "Angular Content Children and the AfterContentInit Lifecycle Hook" (branch ng17-recordings); examples reimplemented, not copied.

import {
  Component, ContentChildren, QueryList,
  AfterContentInit, ElementRef
} from '@angular/core';
import { CourseImageComponent } from './course-image.component';

@Component({ selector: 'course-card', standalone: true,
  template: `<div class="card"><ng-content></ng-content></div>` })
export class CourseCardComponent implements AfterContentInit {

  @ContentChildren(CourseImageComponent)
  images!: QueryList<CourseImageComponent>;

  // native elements instead of instances:
  @ContentChildren(CourseImageComponent, { read: ElementRef })
  imageEls!: QueryList<ElementRef>;

  ngAfterContentInit() {
    console.log('count:', this.images.length);
    this.images.forEach(img => console.log(img));
    // react to later add/remove of projected nodes:
    this.images.changes.subscribe(list =>
      console.log('now', list.length, 'images'));
  }
}
Console — 3 projected imagescount: 3 CourseImageComponent {} CourseImageComponent {} CourseImageComponent {} // with { read: ElementRef }: <course-image>…</course-image> ×3 // still reported even if the card // template omits <ng-content>.

Improved example: an accordion that counts and coordinates its projected panels via @ContentChildren — a realistic use of the collection query.

@Component({ selector: 'acc-panel', standalone: true,
  template: `<ng-content></ng-content>` })
export class AccPanelComponent { open = false; @Input() title = ''; }

@Component({ selector: 'acc-group', standalone: true,
  template: `<p>{{ panels.length }} panels</p><ng-content></ng-content>` })
export class AccGroupComponent implements AfterContentInit {
  @ContentChildren(AccPanelComponent) panels!: QueryList<AccPanelComponent>;

  ngAfterContentInit() {
    // enforce "only one open at a time" by opening the first
    this.panels.first && (this.panels.first.open = true);
  }
  openOnly(target: AccPanelComponent) {
    this.panels.forEach(p => (p.open = p === target));
  }
}
// <acc-group><acc-panel title="A">…</acc-panel>
//            <acc-panel title="B">…</acc-panel></acc-group>
Rendered2 panels ▼ A (open — set by ngAfterContentInit) ▶ B (collapsed) openOnly(B) → closes A, opens B via the QueryList.forEach coordination.
Pitfall Do not read a QueryList in the constructor or ngOnInit — it is empty until ngAfterContentInit. Also, mutating projected component state inside ngAfterContentInit (like setting open = true) can trigger ExpressionChangedAfterItHasBeenCheckedError in dev mode if that state is bound in the parent's already-checked view; defer such writes with a microtask or move them to ngAfterContentChecked guarded against loops.

Advanced: descendants, dynamic changes, and signal queries

By default @ContentChildren only finds direct projected children. Pass { descendants: true } to include matches nested deeper in the projected tree. The QueryList.changes observable emits whenever the projected set changes (e.g. an @for in the parent adds items), which is how components stay in sync with dynamic content. The modern equivalent is the signal query contentChildren(AccPanelComponent), returning a Signal<readonly T[]> that you can feed straight into computed() — no QueryList or lifecycle hook required.

Content Projection cheat sheet
  • <ng-content> — default slot, projects all caller markup.
  • <ng-content select=".header"> — named slot; matches element / .class / [attr] selectors on top-level nodes.
  • One selector-less slot = catch-all for unmatched content. Each node lands in exactly one slot.
  • @ContentChild(ref|Type, {read}) — one projected node; @ContentChildren(...) — a QueryList of them.
  • { read: ElementRef } → native element; default → what was matched (component/directive instance, or ElementRef for a plain element).
  • @ContentChildren(..., { descendants: true }) to reach nested projected nodes; subscribe .changes for dynamic updates.
  • Timing: content queries ready in ngAfterContentInit; view queries ready in ngAfterViewInit.
  • Contrast: view child = component's own template; content child = markup projected in by the parent.
  • Modern signal forms: contentChild() / contentChildren() return signals — no lifecycle hook needed.

Quick check — 3 questions

1. A parent projects an <img class="hero"> into a child. Which child slot receives it?

  1. <ng-content select="img"> only
  2. Any of <ng-content select=".hero">, select="img", or the default slot — whichever the child declares that matches, and if several match, the first matching slot
  3. The default slot only, because class selectors are ignored
  4. Both a matching selective slot and the default slot (duplicated)
Answer

B. Class, element, and attribute selectors all work. A node lands in a single slot — never duplicated — and a selector-less slot catches only what nothing else matched.

2. You put #courseImage on a projected element and query it with @ViewChild('courseImage'). What do you get in ngAfterViewInit?

  1. The element reference
  2. A runtime error
  3. undefined — projected content is not part of the component's view; use @ContentChild
  4. The parent component instance
Answer

C. @ViewChild only sees the component's own template. Projected content is reachable only through @ContentChild/@ContentChildren, resolved in ngAfterContentInit.

3. Which lifecycle hook is the earliest safe place to read a @ContentChildren QueryList?

  1. constructor
  2. ngOnInit
  3. ngAfterViewInit
  4. ngAfterContentInit
Answer

D. Content queries are guaranteed populated from ngAfterContentInit. The constructor and ngOnInit run too early (empty list), and ngAfterViewInit, while later, is conceptually the wrong hook for content.

Angular Templates In Depth

Angular's <ng-template> lets you declare a block of markup that is defined but not drawn until you deliberately instantiate it. This section unpacks the three ideas that make templates a powerful reuse tool: declaring an isolated template with <ng-template>, stamping it out on demand with ngTemplateOutlet plus a context object, and handing a TemplateRef to a child component through an @Input so the parent controls how the child renders. Together they turn a rigid component into one whose look is customisable from the outside.

1 · Introducing <ng-template> — declared but not rendered

Objective: Understand what an <ng-template> is, why its contents never appear on screen by default, and which variables it can read from the surrounding component.

An <ng-template> marks off a fragment of HTML that Angular parses but deliberately leaves out of the rendered page. Think of it as a stored recipe rather than a served dish: the markup exists in the compiled component, but nothing is inserted into the DOM until you explicitly ask for it. This is exactly what happens behind the scenes with the else branch of an *ngIf — the alternative content lives inside a template that is only stamped out when the condition is false.

A common first encounter is providing fallback content. When a course has no cover image, we want to show a placeholder instead. We give the template a name with a template reference variable (#blankImage) and point the *ngIf's else at it. If you remove that reference, the template's markup vanishes entirely — inspecting the DOM shows nothing between the surrounding elements, because Angular keeps the fragment in reserve and only materialises it when referenced.

One detail worth flagging early: in the original lesson the real cover image is not a plain <img> at all — it is handed to the card through content projection (<ng-content>), markup supplied by the parent. So a single card can blend projected content with an in-reserve template, a pairing we return to in Lecture 3.

A key detail: a template can read the variables of the component it is embedded in. The placeholder can happily interpolate course.description even though it is not currently displayed, because it shares the surrounding lexical scope. In the next lecture we'll see how a template can also carry its own private variables.

  • <ng-template> defines an isolated fragment that is not rendered unless instantiated.
  • A template reference variable (#name) gives the fragment a handle you can pass around.
  • Remove the reference and the content simply never reaches the DOM — no error, just nothing.
  • A template can see the variables of the enclosing component's scope.
  • *ngIf ... else is itself powered by <ng-template>.

Source: reimplemented from the ng17-recordings branch of angular-university/angular-course (course-card component); example rewritten for this guide.

<!-- course-card.component.html -->
<h3>{{ course.category }} — {{ course.description }}</h3>

<img
  *ngIf="course.iconUrl; else blankImage"
  class="course-thumbnail"
  [src]="course.iconUrl">

<!-- Declared here, but Angular does NOT draw it
     unless the else branch (or something else) uses it -->
<ng-template #blankImage>
  <div class="warn">No image available yet</div>
  <img class="course-thumbnail"
       src="/assets/empty-image.png">
</ng-template>
Rendered outputWhen course.iconUrl is set:
  [ cover image shown ]

When course.iconUrl is empty:
  No image available yet
  [ empty-image.png placeholder ]

If #blankImage were NOT referenced by
any *ngIf else, the <div class="warn">
and placeholder image would appear
nowhere in the DOM.
<!-- Angular 17+ control flow — same idea, new syntax -->
@if (course.iconUrl) {
  <img class="course-thumbnail" [src]="course.iconUrl">
} @else {
  <!-- the @else body is compiled to an embedded view -->
  <div class="warn">No image available yet</div>
  <img class="course-thumbnail" src="/assets/empty-image.png">
}
Compiles toEach @if / @else branch body is compiled
into an embedded view held in reserve,
conceptually like an <ng-template>, stamped
out only when course.iconUrl is falsy.
But built-in control flow is a compiler
feature, NOT a structural directive: it does
not use the NgIf directive or ngTemplateOutlet,
and no directive does the stamping — the
compiler wires it for you.
Pitfall Declaring an <ng-template> and expecting to "just see it" on the page is the classic beginner surprise. Nothing renders until the template is instantiated — via an else clause, an ngTemplateOutlet, or a structural directive. If your placeholder is blank, first check that something actually references it.
Advanced: templates are the mechanism behind every structural directive

Syntactic sugar like *ngIf and *ngFor desugars into <ng-template> blocks that the directive (NgIf/NgForOf) stamps into a ViewContainerRef at runtime. The asterisk form <div *ngIf="x"> is shorthand for <ng-template [ngIf]="x"><div>...</div></ng-template>. Understanding <ng-template> therefore explains why structural directives can defer, repeat, or conditionally create DOM — they hold a template and decide when to instantiate it. The newer @if/@for control flow reaches the same end result — branch bodies compiled to embedded views instantiated on demand — but it is a first-class compiler feature, not a structural directive: there is no NgIf/NgFor directive and no ngTemplateOutlet involved.

2 · Instantiating templates with ngTemplateOutlet and a context

Objective: Learn how to render a template on demand with ngTemplateOutlet, and how to give each instance its own private variables through ngTemplateOutletContext — including the special $implicit value.

To make a template reusable, it needs more than access to the enclosing scope — it needs its own context: a private set of variables supplied per instance. You declare those inputs inside the template with the let- syntax. Writing let-courseName="description" creates a template-local variable courseName that is filled from the description key of whatever context object you pass in. The expression on the right is evaluated against the context object, not the component class. This is a genuine scope switch worth pausing on: an ordinary input binding such as [icon]="course.iconUrl" is resolved against the component class, whereas the right-hand side of a let- is resolved against whatever context object you pass at instantiation.

You render the template with the *ngTemplateOutlet structural directive, pointing it at the template reference and supplying ngTemplateOutletContext — an ordinary JavaScript object whose keys match the let- bindings. Reuse the same template with two different context objects and you get two independent instances, each showing different data. Because *ngTemplateOutlet only needs some host element to attach to, you could hang it on a throwaway <div> — but that leaves a wrapper element in the DOM you never wanted. Switch the host to <ng-container> and the template is instantiated with no extra element of its own, so you get the rendered content without the clutter.

The special key $implicit is the default: a variable declared as bare let-value (with no ="key") receives the context's $implicit property. This is convenient for the "one main value" case, while named keys cover any additional data.

A useful diagnostic to internalise while learning: instantiating a template with no context at all — or a blank {} — still renders it. The let- variables simply resolve to undefined and come out empty. Seeing empty fields (rather than nothing on screen) is the tell-tale that your outlet is wired correctly but the context is missing — which is exactly what motivates supplying the context object in the first place.

  • let-x binds to the context's $implicit; let-x="key" binds to a named context property.
  • *ngTemplateOutlet="tpl"; context: obj renders tpl with obj as its private scope.
  • Right-hand expressions in let- are resolved against the context object, not the component.
  • Reusing one template with different contexts yields independent instances.
  • Use <ng-container> as the outlet host to avoid an extra wrapper element.

Source: reimplemented from the ng17-recordings branch of angular-university/angular-course (app component templates lesson); example rewritten and extended for this guide.

<ng-template #blankImage let-courseName="description">
  <div class="warn">No image — {{ courseName }}</div>
</ng-template>

<!-- Step 1: instantiate with NO context object -->
<ng-container *ngTemplateOutlet="blankImage"></ng-container>

<!-- Step 2: same outlet, now WITH a context -->
<ng-container
  *ngTemplateOutlet="blankImage;
    context: { description: course.description }">
</ng-container>
Rendered outputStep 1 (no context):
  No image —            <- courseName is
                           undefined, blank
Step 2 (with context):
  No image — Angular Deep Dive

The template renders either way; the
context only fills in the let- variables.
<!-- app.component.html -->
<ng-template #blankImage let-courseName="description"
                          let-badge>
  <div class="warn">No image — {{ courseName }}</div>
  <small>{{ badge }}</small>
</ng-template>

<hr>
<!-- instance A: named + $implicit via context object -->
<ng-container
  *ngTemplateOutlet="blankImage;
    context: {
      description: course.description,
      $implicit: 'primary'
    }">
</ng-container>

<hr>
<!-- instance B: different data -->
<ng-container
  *ngTemplateOutlet="blankImage;
    context: {
      description: courses[0].description,
      $implicit: 'archived'
    }">
</ng-container>
Rendered output────────────────────
No image — Angular Deep Dive
primary
────────────────────
No image — RxJs In Practice
archived
────────────────────

let-badge (no ="key") receives the
context's $implicit value.
let-courseName="description" receives
the context's description key.
Pitfall Forgetting the context object (or mis-spelling a key) does not raise an error — the corresponding let- variable is simply undefined and renders blank. Also remember the leading *: *ngTemplateOutlet is a structural directive, and omitting the asterisk means nothing is stamped out.
Advanced: the non-structural attribute form and type-safe contexts

The * form desugars to <ng-container [ngTemplateOutlet]="tpl" [ngTemplateOutletContext]="obj">. Using the attribute form directly is handy when you need to bind other inputs on the same element. For type safety, Angular provides a static ngTemplateContextGuard pattern on directives so the template type checker can validate let- bindings against a declared context interface — useful when authoring reusable directives that expose templates.

interface RowContext<T> {
  $implicit: T;      // the row item
  index: number;     // named context var
}
// Consumers write: <ng-template let-item let-i="index">

3 · Passing a TemplateRef into a component as an @Input

Objective: Make a child component's rendering customisable from the outside by accepting a TemplateRef as an @Input and instantiating it internally with ngTemplateOutlet and a context.

The real payoff of templates is passing one into a component so the parent decides how a slice of the child renders. The parent declares an <ng-template>, gives it a reference, and binds that reference to a child @Input. The child types the input as TemplateRef<any> and, wherever it wants the custom content, stamps the template out with ngTemplateOutlet, supplying a context of its own data.

In the course-card example, the parent supplies a "no image" template; the card receives it as @Input() noImageTemplate: TemplateRef<any> and, in its own *ngIf ... else, instantiates that template through an <ng-container> outlet — passing the course object as context so the parent's template can display the course name. Nothing is printed until the context is filled, so remember to hand over the data the template's let- bindings expect.

This is a powerful composition pattern: a single component can accept a template through an input and receive other markup through content projection, making it highly customisable while keeping its internal logic intact. It is the same principle behind reusable table, list, and card libraries where you supply the cell/row templates.

  • Type the input as TemplateRef<any> (or a typed context) and bind a parent #ref to it.
  • Instantiate it inside the child with *ngTemplateOutlet plus a context object.
  • The parent's template runs with data provided by the child — inversion of rendering control.
  • Combine template inputs with content projection for maximum flexibility.

Source: reimplemented from the ng17-recordings branch of angular-university/angular-course (customisable course-card lesson); example rewritten for this guide.

// course-card.component.ts
import { Component, Input, TemplateRef } from '@angular/core';

@Component({
  selector: 'course-card',
  standalone: true,
  imports: [NgIf, NgTemplateOutlet],
  templateUrl: './course-card.component.html',
})
export class CourseCardComponent {
  @Input() course!: Course;
  // Parent-supplied template that overrides the fallback UI
  @Input() noImageTemplate!: TemplateRef<any>;
}
DOM result<course-card> renders:
  when course has an icon → its image
  when it does not → the PARENT's
     "no image" template, showing the
     course name supplied via context

The child owns the *when*; the parent
owns the *what*.
<!-- course-card.component.html (child) -->
<img *ngIf="course.iconUrl; else noImage"
     [src]="course.iconUrl">

<ng-template #noImage>
  <ng-container
    *ngTemplateOutlet="noImageTemplate;
      context: { $implicit: course }">
  </ng-container>
</ng-template>

<!-- app.component.html (parent) -->
<course-card
  [course]="course"
  [noImageTemplate]="customPlaceholder">
</course-card>

<ng-template #customPlaceholder let-c>
  <div class="warn">Missing cover for {{ c.description }}</div>
</ng-template>
Rendered outputCourse with no icon renders:

  Missing cover for Angular Deep Dive

The parent decided the placeholder
markup; the child injected the course
object through context ($implicit → c).
Pitfall The @Input() must be a TemplateRef, not the rendered content — bind the reference variable ([noImageTemplate]="customPlaceholder"), never call or interpolate it. And the child must supply a context; if the parent template reads c.description but the child forgets context: { $implicit: course }, c is undefined and the output silently blanks.
Advanced: @ContentChild(TemplateRef) as an alternative to an explicit input

Instead of an @Input, a child can pick up a projected template with @ContentChild(TemplateRef) or by matching a directive marker. Libraries often expose a structural directive (e.g. *appCardImage) whose TemplateRef the component reads via @ContentChild(CardImageDirective). This lets consumers write ergonomic template markup inside the component's tags while the component still controls instantiation and context — the same rendering-inversion idea, surfaced through content projection rather than a named input.

Section cheat-sheet 
<!-- 1. Declare (not rendered) -->
<ng-template #tpl let-item let-i="index">
  {{ i }}: {{ item.name }}
</ng-template>

<!-- 2. Instantiate with a context -->
<ng-container
  *ngTemplateOutlet="tpl;
    context: { $implicit: row, index: 3 }">
</ng-container>

<!-- 3. Pass a template into a child -->
<my-list [rowTemplate]="tpl"></my-list>
<!-- child: @Input() rowTemplate!: TemplateRef<any>
     child html:
     <ng-container *ngTemplateOutlet="rowTemplate;
        context:{ $implicit:item, index:i }"></ng-container> -->
  • let-x → $implicit; let-x="k" → named key k.
  • *ngTemplateOutlet needs a host — prefer <ng-container>.
  • Import NgTemplateOutlet in standalone components' imports.
  • Missing/blank context = silent undefined, no error.

Quick check

1. You declare an <ng-template #box> with visible markup inside but nothing shows on the page. Why?

Show answer
  1. The template has a syntax error
  2. A template is not rendered until it is instantiated (via else, ngTemplateOutlet, or a structural directive)
  3. You must add display:block CSS
  4. <ng-template> is deprecated
Correct: b. Angular keeps the fragment in reserve and only materialises it when explicitly referenced.

2. In <ng-template let-value let-name="title">, where does each variable get its data from?

Show answer
  1. Both from the component class
  2. value from the context's $implicit property; name from the context's title property
  3. Both from $implicit
  4. From @Input decorators
Correct: b. A bare let-x maps to $implicit; let-x="key" maps to that named context key.

3. To let a parent customise how a child renders a fallback, the child should:

Show answer
  1. Accept an @Input() of type TemplateRef and stamp it with ngTemplateOutlet
  2. Copy the parent's HTML string and use innerHTML
  3. Emit an @Output event asking for markup
  4. Read the parent's DOM directly
Correct: a. The child types the input as TemplateRef<any>, then instantiates it internally with a context object it controls.

Angular Directives In Depth

A directive is a class that Angular attaches to an element to change how it looks or behaves. Components are just directives that happen to carry a template. This section unpacks the two remaining families: attribute directives, which decorate an existing element (a native <div> or a custom component) and mutate its properties, attributes, styles and event handling; and structural directives, the ones with the leading star (*ngIf, *ngFor) that add or remove elements by wrapping them in a template. Along the way we build a reusable highlight directive with @HostBinding, @HostListener, custom outputs and exportAs, then desugar the star syntax and hand-build an *appUnless directive from TemplateRef and ViewContainerRef.

1 · Introduction to Angular Attribute Directives

Objective: Understand what an attribute directive is, how it differs from a component and a structural directive, and scaffold your first custom directive with the Angular CLI.

Angular ships several kinds of directive. You have already used two of them: components (directives with a template) and structural directives such as *ngFor and *ngIf that reshape the DOM. The third kind is the attribute directive — an attribute you drop onto an element to alter its appearance, its behaviour, or both, without owning a template of its own.

Plain HTML already works this way. Marking an <input> as disabled greys it out and blocks typing; marking it required changes validation styling. Those attributes attach behaviour to an element that was not there before. A custom Angular directive does the same, letting you extend the browser's vocabulary just as a component lets you invent new tags.

Generate one with the CLI. The command below creates a highlighted directive inside a directives folder. The result is an ordinary TypeScript class decorated with @Directive. Unlike a component's element selector, a directive normally uses an attribute selector — the square brackets around the name mean "apply this to any element carrying this attribute". Each host element the directive lands on produces one instance of the class (verify it by logging in the constructor).

  • Components are directives with a template; attribute directives have no template.
  • Attribute selector [appHighlight] matches every element bearing that attribute.
  • One directive instance is created per matching host element.
  • The host may be a native element or another component — directives are element-agnostic.

Source: branch 2-directives, file src/app/courses/directives/highlighted.directive.ts — reimplemented below as a standalone directive for modern Angular.

# scaffold an attribute directive into src/app/courses/directives
ng generate directive courses/directives/highlighted
# shorthand: ng g d courses/directives/highlighted
CLI outputCREATE .../directives/highlighted.directive.ts
CREATE .../directives/highlighted.directive.spec.ts
import { Directive } from '@angular/core';

@Directive({
  selector: '[appHighlight]',   // attribute selector — note the brackets
  standalone: true
})
export class HighlightDirective {
  constructor() {
    console.log('HighlightDirective instantiated');
  }
}
Console (two host elements)HighlightDirective instantiated
HighlightDirective instantiated

The standalone: true flag opts the directive out of NgModules. It is required in Angular 17–18, but from Angular 19 onward standalone is the default for directives, components and pipes — the flag becomes redundant and the linter even flags it as unnecessary. The samples in this section keep it explicit so they read correctly across the whole v17–22 range.

<!-- one instance per element carrying the attribute -->
<course-card appHighlight [course]="course1"></course-card>
<course-card appHighlight [course]="course2"></course-card>

<!-- remove the attribute and the directive is never created -->
<course-card [course]="course3"></course-card>
ResultDirective attached to the first two cards only.
The third card produces no directive instance.
Pitfall Forgetting the square brackets in the selector (selector: 'appHighlight') turns it into an element selector, so <div appHighlight> silently matches nothing. Attribute directives must use [appHighlight].
Advanced: prefixes and multiple selectors

A directive selector can combine conditions, e.g. selector: 'a[appHighlight]' restricts it to anchor tags only, and selector: '[appHighlight]:not([disabled])' excludes disabled hosts. Teams prefix custom directives (app, or a library prefix such as ngx) so a reader instantly sees the attribute is not part of Angular core. Set the default prefix in angular.json under schematics so ng g d applies it automatically.

2 · Host Binding — DOM Properties vs Attributes

Objective: Use @HostBinding to write CSS classes, styles, DOM properties and DOM attributes on the host element, and understand why properties and attributes are not the same thing.

The element a directive is applied to is its host element. The most direct way to affect it is to write to the host's DOM properties. In the console you could do $0.className = 'highlighted' to add a class by hand — but a directive should not reach into the DOM imperatively. The @HostBinding decorator does it declaratively: place it on a getter and Angular keeps the named host property in sync with the getter's return value.

Because adding and removing CSS classes is so common, @HostBinding offers a shorthand: bind class.name to a boolean. Return true and the class is present; return false and it is removed. You can bind individual styles the same way with style.property. Crucially, when you bind a plain property name like className or style.border, that name must be a real DOM property — binding to something the element does not have throws at runtime.

DOM properties and DOM attributes are related but distinct. Attributes are the strings in the HTML markup; properties are the live JavaScript fields on the element object. The browser keeps some of them in sync and others not. To write an attribute rather than a property, use the attr. prefix. Finally, drive the whole thing from an @Input so callers decide when the element is highlighted.

  • @HostBinding('class.highlighted') toggles a class from a boolean getter.
  • @HostBinding('style.border') writes an inline style property.
  • Plain property bindings require a known DOM property or Angular throws.
  • @HostBinding('attr.disabled') writes a DOM attribute, not a property.
  • An @Input whose alias equals the selector lets you pass [appHighlight]="expr".

Source: branch 2-directives, file src/app/courses/directives/highlighted.directive.ts.

It helps to see the imperative version first. Inspect the host in DevTools, then in the console assign its class by hand — it works, but a directive that pokes at the DOM this way is fragile and untestable. @HostBinding is the declarative equivalent: you describe the value with a getter and Angular writes it for you.

// imperative — typed straight into the DevTools console
// ($0 is the element you selected in the Elements panel)
$0.className = 'highlighted';   // sets the class by hand
Declarative equivalent (@HostBinding)@HostBinding('class.highlighted')
get cssClass() { return this.isHighlighted; }
// same effect — no direct DOM access, driven by state
import { Directive, HostBinding, Input } from '@angular/core';

@Directive({ selector: '[appHighlight]', standalone: true })
export class HighlightDirective {
  // input alias == selector: <el [appHighlight]="true">
  @Input('appHighlight') isHighlighted = false;

  // shorthand: add/remove a CSS class by boolean
  @HostBinding('class.highlighted')
  get cssClass(): boolean { return this.isHighlighted; }

  // write an inline style property
  @HostBinding('style.border')
  get border(): string | null {
    return this.isHighlighted ? '2px solid red' : null;
  }

  // write a DOM ATTRIBUTE (note the attr. prefix)
  @HostBinding('attr.data-highlighted')
  get flag(): string { return String(this.isHighlighted); }
}
Rendered host element<course-card
   class="highlighted"
   style="border: 2px solid red;"
   data-highlighted="true">
</course-card>
/* the class the directive toggles */
.highlighted {
  border: 2px solid #c0392b;
  box-shadow: 0 0 0 3px rgba(192, 57, 43, .25);
}
EffectWhen [appHighlight]="true" the card gains a
red border; when false the class is removed.
Pitfall @HostBinding only writes known properties. Binding @HostBinding('doesNotExist') crashes with "property does not exist". If the target is an HTML attribute with no matching property, you must use the attr. form — e.g. attr.disabled, not disabled, on a non-form element.
Advanced: property vs attribute divergence

Set an <input>'s value attribute and it defines the initial value; the value property tracks the current user-typed value. After typing, the two differ — the attribute still shows the original, the property shows what the user entered. This is why Angular template bindings default to properties ([value]) and offer [attr.x] only when you genuinely need the markup attribute (SVG, ARIA, colspan, custom data-*).

3 · Host Listener — Handling Events in Directives

Objective: React to native DOM events on the host with @HostListener, capture the event object, and emit your own custom events from a directive with @Output.

Beyond writing properties, a directive can listen to events raised by its host. The @HostListener decorator takes the DOM event name as a string; the decorated method runs whenever that event fires on the host. To make the highlight follow the pointer, listen for mouseover to switch it on and mouseleave to switch it off. Any native event works — dblclick, focus, keydown, and so on.

When the handler needs the event object, pass a second argument: an array of expressions to inject. The special token '$event' resolves to the native event, so @HostListener('mouseover', ['$event']) hands your method the MouseEvent.

Directives can also be event sources. Exactly like a component, a directive may expose an @Output backed by an EventEmitter and emit a value. Here we surface a toggleHighlight output carrying the current on/off state, so a parent can react. Because none of this is tied to a specific component, the same directive works on a card, a menu bar, or a bare <div> — it is fully reusable.

  • @HostListener('mouseover') binds a method to a native host event.
  • Inject the event with @HostListener('mouseover', ['$event']).
  • Directives emit custom events via @Output + EventEmitter, just like components.
  • The behaviour is element-independent and reusable across the whole app.

Source: branch 2-directives, file src/app/courses/directives/highlighted.directive.ts.

import {
  Directive, HostBinding, HostListener, Output, EventEmitter
} from '@angular/core';

@Directive({ selector: '[appHighlight]', standalone: true })
export class HighlightDirective {
  private on = false;

  @Output() toggleHighlight = new EventEmitter<boolean>();

  @HostBinding('class.highlighted')
  get cssClass(): boolean { return this.on; }

  @HostListener('mouseover', ['$event'])
  onMouseOver(evt: MouseEvent) {
    this.on = true;
    this.toggleHighlight.emit(this.on);
  }

  @HostListener('mouseleave')
  onMouseLeave() {
    this.on = false;
    this.toggleHighlight.emit(this.on);
  }
}
Console — pointer enters then leavestrue
false
<!-- reusable: works on a component... -->
<course-card appHighlight
             (toggleHighlight)="onToggle($event)">
</course-card>

<!-- ...and equally on a plain element -->
<div appHighlight>Hover me too</div>
BehaviourHovering either element adds .highlighted
and logs true; leaving removes it and logs false.
Pitfall Use mouseleave, not mouseout. mouseout bubbles and fires again every time the pointer crosses a child element, so the highlight flickers off while still inside the host. mouseleave fires only when the pointer truly exits the host.
Advanced: global targets and passive listeners

@HostListener can target objects beyond the host: @HostListener('window:resize', ['$event']) or @HostListener('document:keydown.escape') attach to window/document. These stay active for the directive's lifetime, so keep the handlers cheap. @HostListener always runs its handler inside Angular's zone, so every fire triggers change detection. For hot events (scroll, touchmove) you can avoid that by registering the listener yourself with ngZone.runOutsideAngular(() => el.addEventListener('scroll', fn, { passive: true })) and only re-entering the zone when you actually need to update the view. Renderer2.listen does not help here — its listeners are zone-patched exactly like @HostListener, so they still run inside NgZone and still trigger change detection. The { passive: true } flag is a separate optimization: it lets the browser scroll without waiting on your handler, but it does not change the change-detection behaviour.

4 · The exportAs Syntax — Referencing a Directive Instance

Objective: Expose a directive's public API to the template with exportAs and a template reference variable, and query the same instance from the component class with @ViewChild.

So far the directive works transparently. Sometimes you want to call its public API directly — say a toggle() method — from the template or component that hosts it. To do that you need a handle on the directive instance. The exportAs option in the @Directive decorator gives the directive a name that a template reference variable can bind to.

Declare exportAs: 'appHighlight', then in the template write #hl="appHighlight". The variable hl now points at that specific directive instance, and you can invoke any public method on it — for example, toggling the highlight on double-click. If the component class also needs the instance, query it with @ViewChild(HighlightDirective); the reference is available from ngAfterViewInit onward. When one host carries several directives, disambiguate with the read option: @ViewChild(CourseCardComponent, { read: HighlightDirective }) pulls the directive rather than the component from the same element.

  • exportAs: 'name' makes the directive instance bindable as a template ref var.
  • #hl="appHighlight" captures that instance for use in the template.
  • Call any public method through the ref, e.g. (dblclick)="hl.toggle()".
  • @ViewChild(Directive) injects it into the class; { read: ... } selects which directive on a shared host.

Source: branch 2-directives, file src/app/courses/directives/highlighted.directive.ts.

import { Directive, HostBinding } from '@angular/core';

@Directive({
  selector: '[appHighlight]',
  exportAs: 'appHighlight',   // name for template reference variables
  standalone: true
})
export class HighlightDirective {
  private on = false;

  @HostBinding('class.highlighted')
  get cssClass(): boolean { return this.on; }

  toggle() { this.on = !this.on; }   // public API
}
Console — @ViewChild after view initHighlightDirective {on: false, ...}
<course-card appHighlight
             #hl="appHighlight"
             (dblclick)="hl.toggle()">
</course-card>
BehaviourEach double-click flips the highlight
on and off via the directive's toggle().
import { AfterViewInit, Component, ViewChild } from '@angular/core';

@Component({ /* ... */ })
export class AppComponent implements AfterViewInit {
  // direct reference to the directive instance
  @ViewChild(HighlightDirective) hl!: HighlightDirective;

  // when the host also has a component, pick the directive explicitly
  @ViewChild(CourseCardComponent, { read: HighlightDirective })
  hlOnCard!: HighlightDirective;

  ngAfterViewInit() {
    console.log(this.hl);       // HighlightDirective instance
    this.hlOnCard.toggle();     // drive it from the class
  }
}
ResultBoth queries resolve to the same
HighlightDirective attached to the card.
Pitfall The exportAs string is not the selector — you choose it independently, and the template ref must match it exactly (#hl="appHighlight"). A typo yields the confusing error "There is no directive with exportAs set to ...". Also, a @ViewChild-resolved directive is undefined until ngAfterViewInit; reading it in the constructor returns nothing.
Advanced: why read exists

A single element can host a component, several directives, a TemplateRef and a ViewContainerRef all at once. @ViewChild(ref) alone returns the component by default. The { read } option lets you ask for a specific token from that element's injector — a particular directive, the ElementRef, or the ViewContainerRef — which is exactly how structural directives (next lectures) obtain their template and container.

5 · Structural Directives — Understanding the Star Syntax

Objective: See that a structural directive is an attribute directive over a template, and that the leading star is pure syntactic sugar that Angular desugars into an <ng-template>.

Structural directives — *ngIf, *ngFor — add and remove elements from the page. The goal here is to build a custom one that is the logical inverse of *ngIf: an *appUnless that renders its element only when the condition is false. The app prefix is deliberate: a custom prefix (your app's app, or a library prefix such as ngx) signals to anyone reading the template that the directive is not part of Angular core — it is one you or a library added, unlike the built-in ngIf. That is exactly why we call ours appUnless and not a bare unless. Before implementing it, the key insight: a structural directive's selector is just an ordinary attribute selector. The magic is the star.

The * tells Angular the directive applies on top of a template. When Angular sees *ngIf="cond", it desugars the markup: it wraps the host element in an implicit <ng-template> and moves the directive onto that template as a plain attribute binding, [ngIf]="cond". The directive then decides, via its input, when to instantiate the template into the view — much like manually stamping a template through ngTemplateOutlet. So the concise starred form and the verbose <ng-template> form are two spellings of the same thing.

Two syntax notes when writing the expanded form by hand: the as keyword is not available there, and any template reference variable you declare (for example a loop variable) is only visible inside the generated template. A variable you declare with let-x but give no value is implicitly bound to the template's default ($implicit) value that the directive supplies — that is how let-c below receives something without an explicit assignment. Removing the directive from the expanded template renders nothing — proof that it is the directive controlling instantiation.

  • A structural directive is an attribute directive that can create a template.
  • *ngIf="c" desugars to <ng-template [ngIf]="c">...</ng-template>.
  • The star is only sugar — the expanded form behaves identically.
  • Template reference variables are scoped to the generated template.

Source: branch 2-directives, file src/app/courses/directives/ngx-unless.directive.ts — the desugaring below mirrors what the CLI-scaffolded directive will consume.

<!-- concise, starred form -->
<div *ngIf="course as c">{{ c.title }}</div>

<!-- exact equivalent Angular generates -->
<ng-template [ngIf]="course" let-c="ngIf">
  <div>{{ c.title }}</div>
</ng-template>
ResultBoth render identically. Remove the
directive from the template and nothing is stamped.

Precise point on that as alias: it desugars to let-c="ngIf", binding c to the directive's ngIf context key (the value NgIf publishes for the alias). You will often see it written as a bare let-c, which binds the context's $implicit value instead — for NgIf the two happen to be equal (both are the truthy condition), so the rendered result is the same. The let-c="ngIf" form is what the compiler actually generates.

Pitfall You cannot stack two structural directives on one element — <div *ngIf="x" *ngFor="..."> is a compile error, because each star wants to own the element's template. Wrap one in an <ng-container>, or in Angular 17+ use the block @if/@for control flow instead.
Advanced: the microsyntax parser

The string after the star (let item of items; trackBy: fn; index as i) is a microsyntax. Angular parses it into separate bindings on the generated template: an input for the directive (ngForOf), context variables via let-, and helper inputs like ngForTrackBy. Understanding this is why the input setter name matters in a custom structural directive — Angular derives the binding names from the selector plus the microsyntax keywords.

6 · Structural Directives — Step-by-Step Implementation

Objective: Build the *appUnless structural directive from scratch using TemplateRef and ViewContainerRef, driven by an input setter, and guard against redundant re-renders.

Now we implement the inverse-of-ngIf directive. A structural directive needs two collaborators, both injected in the constructor. TemplateRef is a programmatic handle on the template the star produced — the block of DOM to stamp. ViewContainerRef is the slot where that template gets rendered; its createEmbeddedView(templateRef) instantiates the template on demand, and clear() tears it down.

The condition arrives through an @Input. Rather than a plain field, we use a setter whose name matches the selector exactly (set appUnless) — Angular calls it with the already-evaluated boolean. Inside the setter lies the whole logic: when the condition is false, create the embedded view; when true, clear the container. That is the inversion of ngIf.

One subtlety: Angular may call the setter repeatedly, sometimes when the view is already in its desired state. Creating the view twice or clearing an empty container is wasteful, so we keep a visible flag and only act on a genuine transition. With that guard, the directive is complete and reusable — a natural fit for showing or hiding elements based on permissions or feature flags.

  • Inject TemplateRef (the template) and ViewContainerRef (where to stamp it).
  • Receive the condition through an @Input setter named like the selector.
  • createEmbeddedView(tpl) renders; clear() removes.
  • Track a visible flag so repeated setter calls don't re-create or double-clear.

Source: branch 2-directives, file src/app/courses/directives/ngx-unless.directive.ts — reimplemented below as a standalone appUnless directive.

import {
  Directive, Input, TemplateRef, ViewContainerRef
} from '@angular/core';

@Directive({ selector: '[appUnless]', standalone: true })
export class UnlessDirective {
  private visible = false;

  constructor(
    private tpl: TemplateRef<any>,        // the wrapped template
    private vcr: ViewContainerRef         // where to render it
  ) {}

  // setter name MUST equal the selector
  @Input()
  set appUnless(condition: boolean) {
    if (!condition && !this.visible) {
      this.vcr.createEmbeddedView(this.tpl);  // show
      this.visible = true;
    } else if (condition && this.visible) {
      this.vcr.clear();                        // hide
      this.visible = false;
    }
  }
}
Behaviourcondition = false  →  element rendered
condition = true   →  element removed
(opposite of *ngIf)
<!-- show the thumbnail UNLESS the icon is missing -->
<img class="course-thumbnail"
     *appUnless="!course.iconUrl"
     [src]="course.iconUrl">

<!-- flag true hides it, false shows it -->
<p *appUnless="user.isAdmin">Standard user view</p>
ResultThumbnail appears when iconUrl is set.
Paragraph shows for non-admins only.

The original course app uses the directive on projected content: a <course-image> passed into a <course-card>, shown only when the course actually has an icon.

<!-- course-image is projected INTO course-card via ng-content -->
<course-card [course]="course">
  <course-image *appUnless="!course.icon"
                [src]="course.icon"></course-image>
</course-card>
Resultcourse-image renders only when course.icon
is set, and stays projected inside course-card.
Pitfall The setter name must match the selector character-for-character (set appUnless for selector [appUnless]) or Angular never routes the bound value to it, and the template stays permanently hidden. Also mark the setter with @Input(), not the class field — decorating the wrong member breaks the binding silently.
Advanced: why keep the star over the hand-expanded form

It is tempting to rewrite *appUnless as an explicit <ng-template [appUnless]="…"> wrapping the <course-image>. Don't — it can break content projection. course-card locates its image with a content query (@ContentChild(CourseImageComponent)), which only matches elements directly projected into the card. Under the star form the course-image stays a projected child, so the query finds it; hand-expanding buries it inside a <ng-template> that the directive stamps later, and the content query resolves to undefined. The star form is not just terser here — it preserves the projected shape the parent depends on.

Advanced: injecting via inject() and context data

In modern Angular you can drop the constructor parameters and use the functional API: private tpl = inject(TemplateRef); private vcr = inject(ViewContainerRef);. To expose data back to the template (like ngFor's index), pass a context object: this.vcr.createEmbeddedView(this.tpl, { $implicit: value, count: n }), then consume it in markup with let-value and let-c="count". Defining a static ngTemplateContextGuard gives the template strict typing for those variables.

Directives cheat-sheet
  • Scaffold: ng g d courses/directives/highlighted → class with @Directive and an attribute selector [appHighlight].
  • Write to host: @HostBinding('class.x') (class), @HostBinding('style.p') (style), @HostBinding('attr.a') (attribute). Plain names must be real DOM properties.
  • Listen on host: @HostListener('mouseover', ['$event']); use mouseleave not mouseout.
  • Emit: directives can have @Output() e = new EventEmitter() just like components.
  • Expose API: exportAs: 'name' + #ref="name" in the template; @ViewChild(Dir, { read }) in the class.
  • Structural = star: *x="c" desugars to <ng-template [x]="c">. Inject TemplateRef + ViewContainerRef; input setter named like the selector; createEmbeddedView / clear; guard with a visible flag.

Quick check — 3 questions

1. Why must a custom attribute directive's selector be written as [appHighlight] rather than appHighlight?

Show answer
  1. Brackets make it a structural directive
  2. Brackets make it an attribute selector, matching elements that carry that attribute; without them it becomes an element selector and matches nothing
  3. Brackets are required by TypeScript decorators
  4. There is no difference

Correct: b. An attribute directive is applied via an attribute, so it needs an attribute selector.

2. You need to set an element's colspan — an HTML attribute with no matching DOM property. Which host binding is correct?

Show answer
  1. @HostBinding('colspan')
  2. @HostBinding('class.colspan')
  3. @HostBinding('attr.colspan')
  4. @HostListener('colspan')

Correct: c. Binding a plain unknown property throws; the attr. prefix writes the DOM attribute directly.

3. In a custom structural directive, what does *appUnless="cond" desugar into?

Show answer
  1. <div appUnless (cond)>
  2. <ng-template [appUnless]="cond">...</ng-template> wrapping the host element
  3. A component with an inline template
  4. Nothing — the star is a runtime directive, not sugar

Correct: b. The star wraps the element in an implicit <ng-template> and rebinds the directive as a property binding, which the directive stamps via ViewContainerRef.createEmbeddedView.

Angular View Encapsulation In Depth

Component styles in Angular are scoped by default: CSS written in a component's stylesheet only affects that component's own template, never the rest of the page. This section opens the hood on how that isolation is achieved — the generated _nghost/_ngcontent attributes the compiler rewrites your selectors around — then walks through the special selectors Angular gives you to work with (and around) that boundary: :host to reach the component's own element, ::ng-deep to break out of the scope (now deprecated), and :host-context for ancestor-driven theming. It closes by comparing the default Emulated strategy against native ShadowDom and None.

Angular View Encapsulation — Under the Hood

Objective: Understand why CSS placed in a component's styleUrls only styles that component, and how Angular rewrites selectors using generated content attributes to make it happen.

A style rule such as a shadow on a .course-card behaves differently depending on where you put it. Dropped into the global styles.css, it hits every matching element on the page. Moved into the component's own stylesheet (styleUrls: ['./course-card.component.css']), the same rule still styles the card, but a sibling element outside the component with the identical class is now untouched. That containment is called view encapsulation, and by default Angular delivers it without Shadow DOM — purely by rewriting your CSS and stamping attributes onto the DOM.

When Angular instantiates a component's view under the default Emulated mode, it assigns that component definition a unique id and adds a matching _ngcontent-* attribute to every element the template renders. The stylesheet's selectors are then rewritten to require that same attribute, so a rule can only apply to elements the component itself produced. The id belongs to the component class, not to each rendered instance: drop ten course-cards onto the page and every one of them stamps the same _ngcontent-ng-c1 on its elements, all sharing the one scoped rule.

Different components in the tree get different ids. The root app-root owns ng-c0; each course-card it renders owns ng-c1; and a course-image nested inside the card owns ng-c2 — with the host element of each carrying _nghost-<id> and everything its template renders carrying _ngcontent-<id>. Because the ids differ, a rule scoped to ng-c1 cannot touch the child image's ng-c2 elements or content projected in from the parent — those belong to a different scope.

  • Emulated encapsulation is the default; it needs no browser Shadow DOM support.
  • Every template element receives a _ngcontent-<id> attribute; every selector is rewritten to require it.
  • The id is per component definition, not per instance — every rendered course-card shares one ng-c1; a different component (a child, or the parent that projected content in) gets a different id, which is what isolates their styles.
  • Projected (ng-content) nodes keep the content attribute of the component that authored them, not the one that displays them.

Course branch master · reimplemented for modern standalone Angular.

import { Component } from '@angular/core';

@Component({
  selector: 'course-card',
  standalone: true,
  template: `
    <div class="course-card">
      <h2 class="title">{{ title }}</h2>
    </div>`,
  styleUrls: ['./course-card.component.css'],
  // encapsulation: ViewEncapsulation.Emulated  // ← the default
})
export class CourseCardComponent {
  title = 'Angular Core Deep Dive';
}

/* course-card.component.css */
/* .course-card { box-shadow: 0 0 10px #0002; } */
Simulated compiled DOM + scoped CSS<course-card _nghost-ng-c1>
  <div class="course-card" _ngcontent-ng-c1>
    <h2 class="title" _ngcontent-ng-c1>Angular Core Deep Dive</h2>
  </div>
</course-card>

/* selector rewritten by the compiler: */
.course-card[_ngcontent-ng-c1] {
  box-shadow: 0 0 10px #0002;
}
/* a .course-card WITHOUT [_ngcontent-ng-c1]
   elsewhere on the page is not matched */

The quickest way to feel the boundary is to stand a plain look-alike next to a real component. Give the app a <course-card> and a bare <div class="course-card test-card">. With the box-shadow rule sitting in the global styles.css, both light up. Move that exact rule into course-card.component.css and only the real card keeps its shadow — the plain outer div now falls outside the rewritten [_ngcontent-ng-c1] selector.

<!-- app.component.html: a real component and a plain look-alike -->
<course-card></course-card>
<div class="course-card test-card">just a div, same class</div>
The same rule, two homes/* BEFORE — rule lives in the GLOBAL styles.css */
.course-card { box-shadow: 0 0 10px #0002; }
→ BOTH the <course-card> and the plain test-card div get the shadow

/* AFTER — the exact same rule moved into course-card.component.css */
.course-card { box-shadow: 0 0 10px #0002; }
compiles to → .course-card[_ngcontent-ng-c1] { box-shadow: 0 0 10px #0002; }
→ only the real card keeps the shadow; the test-card div is untouched
Pitfall The generated ids (ng-c1) are build-time artifacts — never hard-code them in a selector or a test. They change between builds and Angular versions. Rely on the encapsulation behavior, not the attribute names.
Advanced: why the attributes, and not just Shadow DOM?

Emulated mode predates broad browser Shadow DOM support and still has advantages: rewritten attribute selectors work everywhere, keep a single flat DOM (easier to inspect and to reach with global tools), and let global stylesheets and resets still cascade into components. The trade-off is that isolation is one-directional — it stops your rules from leaking out, but global rules from styles.css can still reach in. True Shadow DOM (covered later) blocks both directions.

The :host Selector

Objective: Style the component's own host element from within its stylesheet using the special :host pseudo-class, instead of referencing the element by tag name.

Most component CSS targets elements inside the template. But some rules need to style the component's own wrapper element — for example making course-card a display: block box. You cannot simply write course-card { ... } in the component stylesheet: encapsulation rewrites that into course-card[_ngcontent-ng-c1], and the host element does not carry a content attribute, so the rule silently fails to apply.

The fix is the :host pseudo-class selector, part of the encapsulation vocabulary. It targets the host element specifically. At runtime Angular scopes it through the host's own marker — _nghost-<id> — which is present only on the component element itself, not on any of its children.
