Like content attributes, each component gets a unique host id, so :host rules stay isolated to that component's element.

  • :host selects the component's own element; a bare tag-name selector will not, because the host has no content attribute.
  • It compiles to a [_nghost-<id>] attribute selector applied only to the host element.
  • :host(.selected) conditionally styles the host when it also matches the parenthesized selector (e.g. a class the parent set).

Course branch master · reimplemented for modern standalone Angular.

/* course-card.component.css */

/* Styles the HOST element (the <course-card> tag) */
:host {
  display: block;
  border: 1px solid #ddd;
  border-radius: 8px;
}

/* Conditional: only when the host has .selected */
:host(.selected) {
  border-color: #0b63ce;
}

/* Contrast: this targets a child INSIDE the template */
.title {
  font-size: 1.2rem;
}
Simulated scoped output/* :host  →  matches only the host element */
[_nghost-ng-c1] {
  display: block;
  border: 1px solid #ddd;
  border-radius: 8px;
}

[_nghost-ng-c1].selected {
  border-color: #0b63ce;
}

.title[_ngcontent-ng-c1] {   /* child, content-scoped */
  font-size: 1.2rem;
}

DOM:
<course-card _nghost-ng-c1 class="selected">…</course-card>
Pitfall Writing the component's tag name (course-card { display:block }) inside its own encapsulated stylesheet does nothing — the rewritten selector demands a content attribute the host lacks. Always use :host for the wrapper.
Advanced: :host with descendant selectors

:host can lead a descendant selector — :host(.active) .title { … } styles a template child, but only when the host carries the active class. Mind the parentheses: the compound form :host.active looks equivalent and happens to work under Emulated (it is rewritten to an attribute selector), yet it does not match a real host under ShadowDom, where the host is featureless and only the functional :host(…) form matches it. Prefer :host(.active) everywhere — the same portable form the keypoints above already use. This is a clean way to expose state-driven variants (open/closed, valid/invalid) that a parent toggles via a class binding on the element, without adding an @Input purely for styling.

Bypassing View Encapsulation with ::ng-deep

Objective: Style elements that were handed to the component through content projection — nodes that carry a different component's content attribute — by piercing the encapsulation boundary with ::ng-deep, and understand why it is deprecated.

Content projected into a component with ng-content is authored by the parent, so those nodes keep the parent's content attribute (e.g. ng-c0), not the projecting component's (ng-c1). A normal rule in the child's stylesheet — rewritten to require [_ngcontent-ng-c1] — therefore cannot reach them. If you move the rule up to the parent's stylesheet it works, but now a card-specific style lives in the wrong file, defeating the whole point of colocating styles with their component.

::ng-deep solves this by disabling encapsulation for the portion of the selector that follows it: everything after ::ng-deep is emitted without a content attribute, so it can match projected (or otherwise descendant) elements regardless of which component created them. Anything before ::ng-deep (typically :host) stays scoped, which is what keeps the rule from becoming fully global. The catch: ::ng-deep is officially deprecated. It is retained only because no standard replacement yet exists for piercing shadow boundaries from a parent, and it will eventually be removed.

  • Projected nodes belong to the parent's scope, so a child's ordinary rules miss them.
  • ::ng-deep strips the content-attribute requirement from the rest of the selector, letting it reach those nodes.
  • Always anchor it with :host (:host ::ng-deep .foo) so the escape stays contained to this component's subtree.
  • It is deprecated — prefer CSS custom properties, or moving the markup so it is genuinely part of the component.

Course branch master · reimplemented for modern standalone Angular.

First, the markup that creates the mismatch. The card's template exposes an <ng-content> slot, and the parent projects a .course-description through it. In the compiled DOM the description sits inside the card, yet it still carries the parent's _ngcontent-ng-c0 — because app-root, not the card, authored it:

<!-- course-card.component.html (this component owns ng-c1) -->
<div class="course-card">
  <ng-content></ng-content>   <!-- projection slot -->
</div>

<!-- app.component.html (owns ng-c0) projects a description IN -->
<course-card>
  <div class="course-description">Master change detection…</div>
</course-card>
Compiled DOM — who owns which attribute<course-card _nghost-ng-c1>
  <div class="course-card" _ngcontent-ng-c1>
    <!-- projected node keeps the PARENT's attribute: -->
    <div class="course-description" _ngcontent-ng-c0>Master…</div>
  </div>
</course-card>
/* the card stamped ng-c1 on everything IT rendered, but the
   description was authored by app-root, so it stays ng-c0 */
/* course-card.component.css */

/* Fails: projected .course-description carries the
   PARENT's content attribute, not this component's */
.course-description {
  max-width: 500px;
}

/* Works: pierce the boundary, but stay anchored to :host
   so it only affects THIS card's projected content */
:host ::ng-deep .course-description {
  max-width: 500px;
}
Simulated scoped output/* the failing rule compiles to: */
.course-description[_ngcontent-ng-c1] { max-width: 500px; }
/* but the projected node is:
   <div class="course-description" _ngcontent-ng-c0>
   → attribute mismatch, rule ignored */

/* ::ng-deep drops the attribute after :host: */
[_nghost-ng-c1]   .course-description { max-width: 500px; }
/* host stays scoped; the descendant part is unscoped,
   so it now matches the ng-c0 projected node */
Pitfall A bare ::ng-deep .foo with no leading :host is emitted with zero scoping — it leaks to every .foo on the page, exactly like putting the rule in the global stylesheet. If you are going to write global CSS, do it deliberately in styles.css; do not disguise it as component CSS.
Advanced: the modern alternative — CSS custom properties

CSS custom properties pierce Shadow DOM and encapsulation boundaries by design and are not deprecated. Have the child expose theming hooks — .course-description { max-width: var(--card-desc-width, 500px); } — and let any ancestor set --card-desc-width. The child stays in control of which properties are themeable, the parent controls the values, and nothing relies on a deprecated selector.

The :host-context Selector — Theming Use Case

Objective: Style a component based on a condition set on one of its ancestors (such as a theme class), using :host-context — while keeping the rule scoped to this component.

Sometimes a component should change appearance depending on context established outside it — a classic case is theming, where an ancestor element carries a class like salmon-theme and every card beneath it should adopt a themed background. You cannot express this with a plain rule in the component stylesheet, because encapsulation scopes the whole selector to this component and the theme class lives on an ancestor. Using ::ng-deep to escape works, but it over-reaches: the themed rule then applies to any matching element on the page, not just this component's instances.

:host-context(selector) is the precise tool. It matches the host element when the host itself or any ancestor matches the parenthesized selector, and — crucially — the part of the rule after :host-context stays scoped to this component. So :host-context(.salmon-theme) .course-card themes only the cards inside a salmon-themed ancestor, and leaves an unrelated sibling under the same ancestor untouched. That is the difference from ::ng-deep: the ancestor condition is generic, but the styled target remains component-specific.

  • :host-context(.x) checks the host element and its ancestors for .x; the styled selector after it stays scoped to the component.
  • Ideal for theming: an ancestor sets the theme class, components opt in without any @Input.
  • Unlike ::ng-deep, it does not leak the styled rule to unrelated elements sharing the same ancestor.

Course branch master · reimplemented for modern standalone Angular.

/* course-card.component.css */

/* When ANY ancestor has .salmon-theme,
   theme this component's card — and only this one */
:host-context(.salmon-theme) .course-card {
  background-color: lightsalmon;
}

/* Compare: ::ng-deep would also hit a plain .course-card
   sitting outside the component under the same ancestor */
Simulated scoped output/* :host-context compiles to an ancestor test,
   styled part still content-scoped: */
.salmon-theme .course-card[_ngcontent-ng-c1] {
  background-color: lightsalmon;
}

DOM:
<div class="salmon-theme">
  <course-card _nghost-ng-c1>
    <div class="course-card" _ngcontent-ng-c1>…</div>  [themed]
  </course-card>
  <div class="course-card test-card">…</div>         [NOT themed]
</div>
Pitfall Reaching for ::ng-deep (or the global stylesheet) to implement theming applies the rule to every matching element under the ancestor, not just your component's. When the intent is "style my component according to an outer condition," :host-context is the correct, still-supported selector.
Advanced: :host-context matches the host too, and combines conditions

:host-context tests the host element and all of its ancestors, so the theme class can sit on the host itself or any wrapper above it. You can also stack it with :host state — :host-context(.dark-theme):host(.selected) — to vary appearance by both outer theme and inner selection. For app-wide theming, one :host-context(.dark-theme) per component is a lightweight alternative to threading a theme @Input through the tree.

Angular View Encapsulation — Emulated vs ShadowDom

Objective: Know the three ViewEncapsulation modes — Emulated (default), ShadowDom (native), and None — how each isolates (or does not isolate) styles, and which to choose.

The encapsulation property of @Component selects the strategy. Emulated is the default and the recommended choice: it approximates isolation entirely through the generated content/host attributes seen so far, works in every browser, and lets global styles still cascade in. None turns encapsulation off — Angular collects every component's CSS, drops it verbatim into a single global stylesheet, and adds no attributes; :host and :host-context lose their meaning and rules apply page-wide, so a card's theme leaks onto unrelated elements again.

ShadowDom uses the browser's real Shadow DOM API. Angular attaches a shadow root to the host and renders the template and a <style> tag inside it. Because a shadow root is effectively a self-contained mini-document, the styles inside are natively confined — no rewritten attributes are needed, and the confinement is close to two-way: your rules cannot escape, and the cascade of non-inherited properties from the outer page is blocked. It is not a total wall, though. Inheritable properties (color, font-family, and the like) and CSS custom properties still cross the shadow boundary — which is exactly what lets the custom-property theming from the ::ng-deep lecture keep working under this mode. The real behavioral difference is that a class-based ancestor theme applied with plain CSS (say .salmon-theme .course-card) no longer bleeds in. (An older Native option existed for this and is deprecated; use ShadowDom.) Emulated remains the general recommendation; if the Shadow DOM standard becomes universal, it is plausible the default shifts there in future.

  • ViewEncapsulation.Emulated — default; attribute-based scoping, no Shadow DOM, one-way isolation. Recommended.
  • ViewEncapsulation.None — no scoping; all component CSS becomes global; :host/:host-context stop working meaningfully.
  • ViewEncapsulation.ShadowDom — real shadow root; near two-way isolation: the cascade of non-inherited outer styles is blocked, though inherited properties and CSS custom properties still cross.
  • The legacy Native mode is deprecated — use ShadowDom.

Course branch master · reimplemented for modern standalone Angular.

import { Component, ViewEncapsulation } from '@angular/core';

@Component({
  selector: 'course-card',
  standalone: true,
  template: `<div class="card">{{ title }}</div>`,
  styles: [`.card { padding: 16px; box-shadow: 0 0 10px #0002; }`],
  encapsulation: ViewEncapsulation.ShadowDom,   // try each mode
})
export class CourseCardComponent {
  title = 'Deep Dive';
}
Simulated DOM per mode/* Emulated (default) — attributes + global <style> */
<course-card _nghost-ng-c1>
  <div class="card" _ngcontent-ng-c1>Deep Dive</div>
</course-card>

/* None — no attributes, CSS hoisted to page <head> */
<course-card><div class="card">Deep Dive</div></course-card>

/* ShadowDom — style lives INSIDE the shadow root */
<course-card>
  #shadow-root (open)
    <style>.card { padding:16px; box-shadow:0 0 10px #0002; }</style>
    <div class="card">Deep Dive</div>
</course-card>
Pitfall Switching to ShadowDom silently blocks your global stylesheet, resets, and third-party theme CSS from reaching the component — a layout that "just worked" in Emulated can break. And None quietly makes every component style global, reintroducing exactly the leakage encapsulation was meant to prevent. Change modes deliberately, not to fix a single stubborn selector.
Advanced: ShadowDom, projection, and ::part / ::slotted

Under ShadowDom, projected content lives in the light DOM and is rendered through native <slot> elements; style it from inside with the standard ::slotted() pseudo-element. To let a parent theme specific internals across the shadow boundary in a supported (non-deprecated) way, expose them with the part attribute and target them from outside via ::part(). Together with CSS custom properties, these give you controlled, forward-compatible theming hooks — the modern replacement for reaching in with ::ng-deep.

Cheat sheet — view encapsulation
  • Scoping mechanism (Emulated): every element gets _ngcontent-<id>, the host gets _nghost-<id>; selectors are rewritten to require the attribute.
  • :host — style the component's own element (compiles to [_nghost-<id>]). :host(.cls) for conditional host styling.
  • :host-context(.theme) .target — style .target only when an ancestor matches .theme; stays component-scoped. Best for theming.
  • :host ::ng-deep .target — pierce the boundary to reach projected/descendant nodes; deprecated. Always anchor with :host. Prefer CSS variables / ::part.
  • Modes: Emulated (default, recommended) · None (all CSS global) · ShadowDom (native two-way isolation) · Native (deprecated).
  • Golden rule: never hard-code generated attribute ids; rely on behavior, not ng-c1.

Quick check

1. In default Emulated encapsulation, why does a rule course-card { display:block } written inside the component's own stylesheet fail to style the host?

Show answer
  1. Angular rewrites it to course-card[_ngcontent-<id>], but the host element carries _nghost, not _ngcontent, so it never matches. Use :host instead.

2. You need a card to turn salmon only when it sits inside an ancestor with class salmon-theme, without affecting unrelated siblings under that same ancestor. Which selector?

Show answer
  1. :host-context(.salmon-theme) .card — the ancestor test is generic but the styled target stays scoped to this component, unlike ::ng-deep which would leak to siblings.

3. What is the key behavioral difference between Emulated and ShadowDom isolation?

Show answer
  1. Emulated is one-way — it stops component styles leaking out but global page styles still cascade in. ShadowDom is near two-way native isolation — the outer cascade of non-inherited properties is blocked (though inherited properties and CSS custom properties still cross the shadow boundary).

Angular Injectable Services In Depth

Up to now the course has lived in the view layer — how a tree of components renders data on screen. This section moves down a level to the service layer: the code that talks to a backend, fetches and mutates data, and hands it to components to display. You will use Angular's built-in HttpClient to issue GET and PUT requests (with query parameters and custom headers), stream the results into the template with the async pipe, and then extract that HTTP logic into your own reusable @Injectable() service wired up through Angular's dependency-injection system. Examples target modern standalone Angular: provideHttpClient() in the app config and inject() inside classes.

Section Introduction & Project Setup

Objective: Check out the starting branch, run both the Angular app and a small REST backend, and confirm the API is reachable before writing any service code.

This section starts from a fresh branch that ships a companion HTTP backend. After checking the branch out and installing dependencies, you run the frontend with npm start (not a bare ng serve, because the npm script also configures the proxy) and, in a second terminal, launch a tiny REST server with npm run server. That server listens on port 9000 and exposes a courses API. Hitting the server root returns a 404 because no root route is configured, so you verify it by requesting the actual endpoint, /api/courses, which returns a JSON list of courses. At this point the app still renders a hard-coded array from a local data file — the goal of the section is to replace that with data fetched live from this backend.

  • Start the frontend with npm start; start the backend with npm run server in a separate terminal.
  • The REST API lives at http://localhost:9000/api/courses; the app proxies /api to it.
  • A 404 at the server root is expected — always test a real route to confirm the backend is up.
  • The starting point still uses a hard-coded courses array; the section swaps it for real HTTP data.

Reimplemented from branch 3-services (file src/app/courses/courses.service.ts) of the course repo; commands paraphrased for a modern standalone setup.

# grab the section's starting code
git checkout -B 3-services origin/3-services
npm install

# terminal 1 — Angular dev server (uses the proxy config)
npm start          # → http://localhost:4200

# terminal 2 — small REST backend
npm run server     # → http://localhost:9000
GET http://localhost:9000/api/courses[ { "id": 1, "description": "Angular Core Deep Dive", "iconUrl": "..." }, { "id": 2, "description": "RxJs In Practice", "iconUrl": "..." } /* ...10 courses total; the endpoint returns a plain array... */ ]
Pitfall Running ng serve directly skips the proxy that the npm start script wires in. Requests to /api/courses then hit the dev server (port 4200) instead of the backend (port 9000) and fail. Use the npm scripts the project provides.
Advanced: the proxy config

The npm script passes --proxy-config proxy.json so browser calls to /api are forwarded to the backend during development, sidestepping CORS. In production you would instead configure the real API origin.

// proxy.json
{
  "/api": { "target": "http://localhost:9000", "secure": false }
}

The Angular HttpClient — GET Calls with Request Parameters

Objective: Inject HttpClient, fire a GET in ngOnInit, subscribe to the returned Observable, and attach query parameters with HttpParams.

Angular ships a built-in HTTP service, HttpClient. You obtain it through dependency injection — historically via a constructor parameter, and in modern Angular via the inject() function. The right place to trigger a data-loading request is the ngOnInit lifecycle hook: Angular calls it after the constructor has run and dependencies are available, so it is the recommended home for initialization logic like a backend call. Crucially, http.get() returns an Observable and is lazy — nothing is sent until you subscribe(). In the success callback you receive the response body, which you then assign to a component field so the template can render it. That assignment is the step that actually feeds the view, and it is worth proving: leave the field empty and the screen stays blank even though the request returned 200; only when the emitted array is assigned inside the subscribe callback do the cards appear — the request fetches the data, but the assignment is what puts it on screen. To add query-string parameters, build an immutable HttpParams instance: each set() call returns a new instance, so you chain them, then pass the result under the params option of get(). Inspecting the network tab confirms the request URL carries ?pageNumber=1&pageSize=10.

  • HttpClient.get() returns a cold Observable — it only executes on subscribe().
  • ngOnInit runs after the constructor and is the recommended place to launch initial requests.
  • Type the call as get<Course[]>(...) so the response is strongly typed.
  • HttpParams is immutable; set() returns a new instance, so chain calls and pass the result as the params option.

Reimplemented from branch 3-services (file src/app/courses/courses.service.ts); modernized to standalone components with inject() and provideHttpClient().

import { Component, OnInit, inject } from '@angular/core';
import { HttpClient, HttpParams } from '@angular/common/http';
import { Course } from './model/course';

@Component({
  selector: 'app-root',
  standalone: true,
  templateUrl: './app.component.html'
})
export class AppComponent implements OnInit {
  private http = inject(HttpClient);
  courses: Course[] = [];

  ngOnInit() {
    const params = new HttpParams()
      .set('pageNumber', '1')
      .set('pageSize', '10');

    this.http
      .get<Course[]>('/api/courses', { params })
      .subscribe(courses => {
        this.courses = courses;      // feed the view
        console.log(courses);
      });
  }
}
Network request & consoleRequest URL: /api/courses?pageNumber=1&pageSize=10 Method: GET Status: 200 before assign (courses = []): view renders an empty list after this.courses = courses: 10 cards appear console: (10) [{id: 1, description: 'Angular…'}, …]
// app.config.ts — register HttpClient app-wide (standalone)
import { ApplicationConfig } from '@angular/core';
import { provideHttpClient } from '@angular/common/http';

export const appConfig: ApplicationConfig = {
  providers: [ provideHttpClient() ]
};
Pitfall Forgetting to subscribe() means the request is never sent — the Observable stays cold and nothing happens. Equally, HttpParams is immutable: writing params.set('x','1') without capturing the return value discards the parameter.
Advanced: fromObject and array params

You can build params from an object in one step, and repeat a key with append() to send array-style values.

const params = new HttpParams({ fromObject: { pageNumber: '1', pageSize: '10' } })
  .append('category', 'BEGINNER')
  .append('category', 'ADVANCED'); // ?category=BEGINNER&category=ADVANCED

The Async Pipe — a Better Way to Pass Observable Data to the View

Objective: Replace manual subscription with the async pipe so the template subscribes implicitly and unsubscribes automatically when the component is destroyed.

Manually subscribing and assigning to a mutable field works, but it invites bugs — someone may reassign the plain courses array by hand — and it leaves you responsible for cleanup. The async pipe offers a more reactive approach. Instead of a plain array, expose an Observable field, conventionally suffixed with $ (for example courses$: Observable<Course[]>), and assign the typed GET result to it without subscribing in the class. In the template you subscribe implicitly by piping it: courses$ | async. The pipe subscribes, waits for the emitted value, and swaps the expression for that value once the data arrives; it also works with Promises. A common refinement is to unwrap the Observable once with @if (or *ngIf) and an alias — @if (courses$ | async; as courses) — then iterate the local courses inside, avoiding multiple subscriptions. The decisive benefit: when the component is destroyed, the async pipe unsubscribes for you, preventing memory leaks.

  • Expose an Observable field (courses$) rather than assigning to a mutable array.
  • observable$ | async subscribes in the template and renders the latest emitted value (works with Promises too).
  • Use @if (courses$ | async; as courses) to unwrap once and reuse the alias — avoids re-subscribing per usage.
  • The pipe auto-unsubscribes on destroy, so you write no cleanup code and avoid leaks.

Reimplemented from branch 3-services (file src/app/courses/courses.service.ts); template uses Angular 17 control-flow (@if/@for).

import { Component, OnInit, inject } from '@angular/core';
import { HttpClient } from '@angular/common/http';
import { Observable } from 'rxjs';
import { Course } from './model/course';

@Component({ selector: 'app-root', standalone: true, /* ... */ })
export class AppComponent implements OnInit {
  private http = inject(HttpClient);
  courses$!: Observable<Course[]>;   // note the $ suffix

  ngOnInit() {
    // no subscribe() here — the template handles it
    this.courses$ = this.http.get<Course[]>('/api/courses');
  }
}
Rendered list• Angular Core Deep Dive • RxJs In Practice • NgRx In Depth • Angular Forms In Depth … (10 cards rendered)
<!-- unwrap once with an alias, then loop -->
@if (courses$ | async; as courses) {
  <div class="courses">
    @for (course of courses; track course.id) {
      <course-card [course]="course"></course-card>
    }
  </div>
}
Pitfall Piping the same Observable in several places (courses$ | async here, again there) creates a new subscription — and often a new HTTP request — each time. Unwrap it once with the as alias, or share the stream with shareReplay().
Advanced: sharing a cold HTTP stream

Each subscription to a raw http.get() re-fires the request. If a stream is consumed multiple times, cache the response with shareReplay.

import { shareReplay } from 'rxjs';
this.courses$ = this.http.get<Course[]>('/api/courses')
  .pipe(shareReplay(1));   // one request, replayed to all subscribers

Custom Services — The @Injectable Decorator

Objective: Generate a custom service, understand @Injectable({ providedIn: 'root' }), and confirm that dependency injection hands every consumer the same singleton instance.

Beyond built-in services, you write your own. The CLI scaffolds one with ng generate service courses/services/courses, producing a plain TypeScript class annotated with @Injectable(). That decorator makes the class participate in Angular's dependency-injection system — it can both be injected into components and receive its own dependencies (like HttpClient) through injection. The class never news up its collaborators; it declares them and Angular supplies them. This matters because the class does not know or care where HttpClient came from — whether it is the real client or a test double — which keeps it decoupled and testable. The providedIn: 'root' option in the decorator registers the service at the root injector, meaning Angular creates exactly one instance for the whole application: a singleton. Injecting the service into the app component and into every course-card component proves it, and the console makes the proof visible: put a log in the service constructor and another at each injection site. Because the page renders many course cards, the injection log prints once per card — many times over — while the constructor log prints exactly once. That contrast, many injection points resolving to a single construction, is the visible signature of an app-wide singleton.

  • @Injectable() lets a class be injected and lets it receive injected dependencies of its own.
  • A class declares its dependencies (via inject() or the constructor); Angular constructs and supplies them — that is dependency injection.
  • providedIn: 'root' registers the service on the root injector → one shared singleton per application.
  • Constructor logging fires once no matter how many components inject the service, confirming the singleton.

Reimplemented from branch 3-services (file src/app/courses/courses.service.ts); DI via inject() in place of constructor parameters.

ng generate service courses/services/courses
# CREATE src/app/courses/services/courses.service.ts
# CREATE src/app/courses/services/courses.service.spec.ts
Console (one page load)Courses service created <-- constructor: runs on the FIRST injection, EXACTLY ONCE injecting CoursesService… <-- AppComponent (the first injection) injecting CoursesService… <-- CourseCard #1 injecting CoursesService… <-- CourseCard #2 injecting CoursesService… <-- CourseCard #3 … (once per card, many times)
import { Injectable, inject } from '@angular/core';
import { HttpClient } from '@angular/common/http';

@Injectable({
  providedIn: 'root'        // one singleton for the whole app
})
export class CoursesService {
  private http = inject(HttpClient);   // DI supplies the client

  constructor() {
    console.log('Courses service created');
  }
}
Pitfall Also listing a providedIn: 'root' service in a component's own providers: [] array creates a second, component-scoped instance — the singleton assumption silently breaks. Provide it in exactly one place unless a separate instance is intentional.
Advanced: tree-shakable providers

providedIn: 'root' is also tree-shakable: if no code injects the service, the bundler can drop it from the build entirely — an advantage over registering services in a module's providers array, where they are always retained.

Custom Service — Fetching Data

Objective: Move the HTTP GET out of the component and into a reusable loadCourses() method on the service, returning a typed Observable.

A service groups commonly used, domain-related operations so they can be reused across the app. The course-loading logic currently sitting in the component is a prime candidate: any component might need the same list. Rather than duplicate the HTTP call, you relocate it into CoursesService as a method — loadCourses() — whose return type is Observable<Course[]>, matching what http.get() yields. The service injects HttpClient directly, so the request logic lives in one place; the method simply returns the Observable to its caller. The component now depends only on the service, not on HttpClient, and obtains its stream by calling coursesService.loadCourses(). This isolates the HTTP details behind a descriptive API: the component knows only that data arrives via an Observable, not whether it came from a network call, a cache, or anything else. The payoff is readability (loadCourses() says more than a raw HTTP call) and reuse (the same operation is one call away anywhere).

  • Concentrate HTTP logic in the service; components call intent-revealing methods like loadCourses().
  • The method returns Observable<Course[]> — the component subscribes (or pipes with async).
  • The component depends on CoursesService only, no longer on HttpClient — a cleaner abstraction.
  • Callers are shielded from the data source (backend, cache, mock); they see only the Observable contract.

Reimplemented from branch 3-services (file src/app/courses/courses.service.ts).

// courses.service.ts
@Injectable({ providedIn: 'root' })
export class CoursesService {
  private http = inject(HttpClient);

  loadCourses(): Observable<Course[]> {
    return this.http.get<Course[]>('/api/courses');
  }
}

// app.component.ts — now depends only on the service
export class AppComponent implements OnInit {
  private coursesService = inject(CoursesService);
  courses$!: Observable<Course[]>;

  ngOnInit() {
    this.courses$ = this.coursesService.loadCourses();
  }
}
Rendered resultApp still renders 10 course cards. Component no longer imports HttpClient — HTTP detail is hidden inside the service.
Pitfall Do not subscribe inside loadCourses() and return the array or a stored field. That forces the timing on every caller and breaks composition. Return the Observable and let each caller decide how to consume it.
Advanced: unwrap the API payload with map

Real backends often wrap results in an envelope (e.g. { payload: [...] }). Normalize it inside the service so components receive a clean array.

import { map } from 'rxjs';
loadCourses(): Observable<Course[]> {
  return this.http
    .get<{ payload: Course[] }>('/api/courses')
    .pipe(map(res => res.payload));
}

Custom Service — Data Modification with an HTTP PUT

Objective: Add a saveCourse() method that issues an HTTP PUT with a request body and a custom header, wired to a course-card edit event.

Services modify data, not just read it. Here the card exposes an editable title input and emits a courseChanged event carrying a modified copy of the course — built with the object spread operator so the original is not mutated, replacing only the description. The app component listens for that event ((courseChanged)="save($event)") and forwards the course to coursesService.saveCourse(). The service calls http.put(), whose first argument is the target URL including the course id (/api/courses/{id}) and whose second argument is the request body — PUT requires a body, so the code will not compile without it. As with GET, PUT returns a cold Observable, so you must subscribe() to actually send it; without a subscription nothing is saved. After saving, reloading the app shows the persisted new title, confirming the round trip. To attach a custom header — say an authentication token — build an immutable HttpHeaders instance (same set()-returns-new-instance pattern as HttpParams), conventionally prefixing custom names with X-, and pass it under the headers option in the call's third argument. The network tab then shows the header on the outgoing request.

  • http.put(url, body, options) — the body is mandatory; the URL includes the resource id.
  • PUT returns a cold Observable; you must subscribe() for the request to fire.
  • Emit an edited copy via object spread ({ ...course, description: newTitle }) instead of mutating the original.
  • HttpHeaders is immutable like HttpParams; pass built headers under the headers option (third argument).

Reimplemented from branch 3-services (file src/app/courses/courses.service.ts).

// courses.service.ts
import { HttpClient, HttpHeaders } from '@angular/common/http';

saveCourse(course: Course): Observable<any> {
  const headers = new HttpHeaders()
    .set('X-Auth', 'my-auth-cookie');   // custom header

  return this.http.put(
    `/api/courses/${course.id}`,   // URL with id
    course,                         // request body (required)
    { headers }                     // options: headers/params
  );
}

// app.component.ts
save(course: Course) {
  this.coursesService.saveCourse(course)
    .subscribe(() => console.log('Course saved'));   // must subscribe
}
Network + backend logPUT /api/courses/1 → 200 OK Request Headers: X-Auth: my-auth-cookie server: saving course 1 ... console: Course saved (reload → new title persists)
// course-card.component.ts — where the edited copy is built and emitted
import { Component, Input, Output, EventEmitter } from '@angular/core';
import { Course } from './model/course';

@Component({ selector: 'course-card', standalone: true, /* ... */ })
export class CourseCardComponent {
  @Input() course!: Course;
  @Output() courseChanged = new EventEmitter<Course>();

  saveClick(newTitle: string) {
    // spread a copy so the original course object is never mutated
    this.courseChanged.emit({ ...this.course, description: newTitle });
  }
}
<!-- course-card emits an edited copy; app reacts -->
<course-card
  [course]="course"
  (courseChanged)="save($event)">
</course-card>
Pitfall A put() that is never subscribed silently does nothing — the UI may look fine while no data reaches the server. Also, put(url) with no second argument is a compile error (the body parameter is required) — but put(url, optionsObject) is not: since body is typed any, an options object slipped into the body slot compiles cleanly and is silently sent as the request payload. Always pass the body 2nd and options 3rd.
Advanced: the full HttpClient verb set

HttpClient exposes get, post, put, patch, delete and head, all returning Observables and all accepting an options object for params, headers, responseType, and observe (use observe: 'response' to read the full HttpResponse including status and headers).

this.http.delete(`/api/courses/${id}`).subscribe();
this.http.patch(`/api/courses/${id}`, { description }).subscribe();
Section cheat sheet 
// Register once (standalone)
providers: [ provideHttpClient() ]           // app.config.ts

// Inject anywhere
private http = inject(HttpClient);

// GET with params (HttpParams is immutable — chain set())
const params = new HttpParams().set('pageNumber','1').set('pageSize','10');
this.http.get<Course[]>('/api/courses', { params });   // cold Observable

// Async pipe in template (auto-subscribe + auto-unsubscribe)
// @if (courses$ | async; as courses) { @for (c of courses; track c.id) {...} }

// Custom singleton service
@Injectable({ providedIn: 'root' })          // one instance app-wide

// Mutate: PUT needs a body; subscribe to send; custom header
const headers = new HttpHeaders().set('X-Auth','token');
this.http.put(`/api/courses/${id}`, course, { headers }).subscribe();

Quick check

1. Why does calling this.http.get('/api/courses') without .subscribe() send no request?

Show answer

HttpClient methods return cold Observables. The HTTP request is only executed when something subscribes — either an explicit .subscribe() in code or implicitly through the async pipe in the template. With no subscriber, the Observable never runs.

2. What is the main advantage of the async pipe over manually subscribing in the component?

Show answer

The pipe subscribes and unwraps the latest value in the template, and — critically — automatically unsubscribes when the component is destroyed, preventing memory leaks. It also encourages exposing an Observable field (courses$) instead of a mutable array that others might reassign by hand.

3. With @Injectable({ providedIn: 'root' }), how many instances of the service exist when it is injected into ten different components?

Show answer

Exactly one. providedIn: 'root' registers the service on the root injector, so Angular creates a single singleton shared across the whole application — its constructor runs only once regardless of how many components inject it.

Angular Dependency Injection In Depth

Dependency injection (DI) is the quiet machinery that hands a class the collaborators it needs without that class ever having to construct them. This section peels back the abstraction: we rebuild a provider by hand to see what Angular normally generates for us, meet the injection token that uniquely names every dependency, learn the four ways a provider can produce a value (useClass, useValue, useFactory, useExisting), and follow a request as it climbs the hierarchical injector tree. We finish with tree-shakeable providers and the resolution modifiers @Optional, @Self, @SkipSelf, and @Host. Modern examples favour the inject() function over constructor parameters.

1 · Introduction to the Angular Dependency Injection System

Objective: Understand what dependency injection solves, how constructor injection works, and why removing a provider yields the classic NullInjectorError: No provider for ….

A component or directive never builds its own collaborators. Instead it declares what it needs and Angular supplies it. When a class lists CoursesService as a constructor parameter (or reads it via inject()), the class stays ignorant of how that service is created — whether it talks to a real backend or is a mock swapped in for a test. That indirection is the first big win: testability. The second is that a service needed deep inside a nested component tree can be injected directly at the leaf, instead of being threaded down through layer after layer of @Input bindings (the "prop-drilling" problem that grows unbearable in large UIs).

Angular obtains the instance through a provider — a recipe that tells the injector how to manufacture the dependency. Under the hood a provider is essentially a factory function the DI system calls the first time the value is requested. If no provider is registered for a requested type, Angular cannot build it and throws No provider for CoursesService. That error is the clearest proof that the friendly providedIn: 'root' we usually write is really registering a provider on our behalf. A dependency, moreover, can declare dependencies of its own — CoursesService needs HttpClient — so the injector resolves an entire chain of collaborators, not merely the single class the component named.

  • DI decouples a class from the construction of its dependencies — enabling substitution and easy testing.
  • It removes the need to pass shared services down a component tree as inputs.
  • A provider is the recipe (a factory) that produces the dependency on demand.
  • No provider for X means the injector was asked for X but has no registered recipe for it.

Adapted from branch 3-services · reimplemented for modern standalone Angular.

// A dependency can itself declare dependencies — DI resolves the WHOLE
// graph of collaborators, not just the one class the component asked for.
@Injectable({ providedIn: 'root' })
export class CoursesService {
  private http = inject(HttpClient);          // CoursesService's OWN dependency
  loadAllCourses(): Observable<Course[]> {
    return this.http.get<Course[]>('/api/courses');
  }
}

// Angular injects CoursesService — the component never calls "new".
@Component({ selector: 'app-root', standalone: true, templateUrl: './app.component.html' })
export class AppComponent {
  // Modern style: the inject() function, no constructor needed.
  private coursesService = inject(CoursesService);
  courses$ = this.coursesService.loadAllCourses();
}

// If CoursesService has NO provider anywhere, bootstrap fails:
//   remove providedIn from @Injectable and the app crashes on load.
Console (no provider registered)ERROR NullInjectorError:
  R3InjectorError(Standalone[AppComponent])[
    CoursesService -> CoursesService]:
  NullInjectorError: No provider for CoursesService!
PitfallInjecting a class that was never provided is the single most common DI error. Before assuming a bug in your logic, read the message literally: No provider for X means register a provider for X, not fix X's code.
Advanced — why DI beats manual instantiation

Calling new CoursesService(httpClient, logger, cache) yourself hard-wires every transitive dependency into the caller. DI inverts that: the injector resolves the whole graph, so a change to CoursesService's own constructor never ripples into the components that consume it. This is the Inversion-of-Control principle applied to object graphs.

2 · Understanding Providers and Injection Tokens

Objective: Hand-write a provider to reveal its two ingredients — a unique injection token and a factory (with its deps) — and see how a consumer requests a value by token with @Inject / inject().

We normally never author providers manually; Angular generates them. Writing one by hand is purely to expose the moving parts. A provider needs to answer two questions: which dependency is this, and how is it built. The "which" is answered by an injection token — a unique key created with new InjectionToken<T>('description'). The "how" is a factory function supplied through useFactory, together with a deps array naming the tokens Angular must resolve and pass into that factory (Angular cannot read a plain function's parameters to infer them). You cannot simply drop a bare factory function into the providers array either — that will not even compile; a lone function is not a valid provider and must be wrapped in the { provide, useFactory, deps } configuration object.

Once registered, the token is the only way to ask for the value. A consumer must request it explicitly — @Inject(COURSES_TOKEN) on a constructor parameter, or inject(COURSES_TOKEN) in field form. Nothing is shared ambiently: every dependency resolves through this same token-and-provider pairing, so each consumer must re-state the token at its own injection site — the lecture applies the request independently in both AppComponent and CourseCardComponent. Forgetting the token binding reproduces the very No provider error we started with, because Angular has no way to connect a bare CoursesService request to the token we configured.

  • Every dependency has an injection token — a unique identity in the injector.
  • Nothing resolves ambiently — each consumer re-requests the value by the same token at its own injection site.
  • useFactory supplies the build function; deps lists the tokens fed into it.
  • Consumers retrieve a token-keyed value with @Inject(token) or inject(token).
  • An InjectionToken<T> is needed when the token is not itself a class.

Adapted from branch 3-services · reimplemented for modern standalone Angular.

export const COURSES_TOKEN = new InjectionToken<CoursesService>('COURSES_TOKEN');

// A hand-written provider: token + factory + its dependencies.
@Component({
  selector: 'app-root',
  standalone: true,
  providers: [
    {
      provide: COURSES_TOKEN,
      useFactory: (http: HttpClient) => new CoursesService(http),
      deps: [HttpClient],   // tokens Angular resolves and passes to the factory
    },
  ],
})
export class AppComponent {
  // Must request by the SAME token the provider registered.
  private courses = inject(COURSES_TOKEN);
}
BehaviourFactory runs once on first request.
Angular resolves HttpClient, calls
useFactory(http), stores the result,
and injects it wherever COURSES_TOKEN
is requested. Omitting deps → factory
runs with no args (http = undefined);
app breaks later when http is used.
PitfallA useFactory whose deps array is missing does not error at injection time — Angular cannot introspect the factory's parameters, so it simply calls the factory with no arguments (each parameter becomes undefined). The app then breaks later, when the missing dependency is first used (e.g. Cannot read properties of undefined). Because Angular does not read the factory's signature, the order of deps must exactly match the factory's parameters. (The Can't resolve all parameters message is a different error — it is raised for reflective class instantiation, i.e. useClass/type-based injection lacking constructor metadata, not for a factory with omitted deps.)
Advanced — an interface cannot be a token

You might wish to write provide: CoursesApi where CoursesApi is an interface. It won't compile as a value, because interfaces are erased at compile time and don't exist at runtime. Tokens must be runtime values — a class reference or an InjectionToken instance. This is exactly why InjectionToken exists.

3 · Simplified Provider Configuration

Objective: Collapse the verbose hand-written provider into Angular's shorthands — a class as its own token, useClass, and the one-element form [CoursesService].

Angular accepts a class name as an injection token, because at runtime a class exists as a constructor function — a perfectly good unique key. (An interface, being compile-time only, cannot serve this role.) So instead of a custom InjectionToken we can simply provide: CoursesService. Next, rather than writing our own factory, useClass: CoursesService tells Angular to call new on that class and resolve its constructor dependencies automatically — no manual deps needed. When the token and the class are identical, even that object is redundant: the array shorthand providers: [CoursesService] expands internally to { provide: CoursesService, useClass: CoursesService }.

These providers were declared on a component's providers array, which scopes the service to that component. That is not how application-wide singletons are normally created — those belong at the application/root level. What "scoped to a component" actually means is the subject of the next lecture: hierarchical DI.

  • A class doubles as its own token; an interface cannot (it has no runtime existence).
  • useClass lets Angular instantiate and wire the class's own dependencies.
  • [CoursesService] is sugar for { provide: CoursesService, useClass: CoursesService }.
  • Component-level providers scope a service to that component subtree, not the whole app.

Adapted from branch 3-services · reimplemented for modern standalone Angular.

// All three forms register the same provider — from most explicit to shortest.

// 1) Fully explicit
providers: [{ provide: CoursesService, useFactory: (h: HttpClient) => new CoursesService(h), deps: [HttpClient] }]

// 2) Let Angular new the class and resolve its deps
providers: [{ provide: CoursesService, useClass: CoursesService }]

// 3) Shorthand when token === class
providers: [CoursesService]

// useExisting: alias one token to another (share ONE instance, no new copy)
providers: [
  CoursesService,
  { provide: COURSES_API, useExisting: CoursesService },
]

// ── Identity demo: useClass mints a NEW instance, useExisting shares one ──
let n = 0;
@Injectable() class Svc { id = ++n; }

// useExisting → COURSES_API is an ALIAS of Svc: one object, one id.
providers: [Svc, { provide: COURSES_API, useExisting: Svc }]
//   inject(Svc).id === inject(COURSES_API).id   → true

// useClass → COURSES_API builds its OWN Svc: two objects, two ids.
providers: [Svc, { provide: COURSES_API, useClass: Svc }]
//   inject(Svc).id === inject(COURSES_API).id   → false
ResultForms 1–3 are equivalent; the app
behaves identically.

Identity demo (ids logged):
  useExisting → Svc #1, COURSES_API #1  (one shared object)
  useClass    → Svc #1, COURSES_API #2  (two separate instances)
PitfalluseExisting and useClass look similar but differ sharply: useClass creates a new instance for that token, while useExisting aliases to an already-provided one. Using useClass where you meant useExisting silently produces two separate instances.

Advanced — the four "use" recipes at a glance
  • useClass — instantiate a class (optionally a different implementation than the token).
  • useValue — inject a ready-made object/primitive as-is.
  • useFactory — run a function (with deps) to compute the value.
  • useExisting — alias the token to another existing provider (one shared instance).

4 · Understanding Hierarchical Dependency Injection

Objective: See that injectors form a tree mirroring the component tree, and that a request walks up that tree until a provider is found — producing either shared singletons or private per-component instances.

Register CoursesService as an app-wide singleton with @Injectable({ providedIn: 'root' }), give each course-card component its own providers: [CoursesService], and log a unique counter in the constructor. The result is telling: the root component declares no local provider, so it inherits the shared root singleton (#1), while each card — because that component-level providers array is itself a provider — mints a private instance of its own. Remove the card-level provider and every card falls back to the same root #1. This happens because Angular maintains a hierarchy of injectors that parallels the component hierarchy. When a component asks for a dependency, its own injector is searched first; if no matching provider is found there, the request bubbles up to the parent injector, then the parent's parent, all the way to the application root injector — and beyond it to the platform/null injector, where an unresolved request finally errors.

This gives you a design lever. A stateless service (like course loading, which just calls the backend and returns) is safe to share as a root singleton — multiple copies would be wasteful and interchangeable. A service holding state private to a subtree should be provided at that component's level so the instance is invisible elsewhere and is destroyed together with the component that owns it.

  • Injectors form a tree that mirrors the component tree.
  • Resolution searches the local injector, then parents, up to root, then the null injector (error).
  • A component-level provider produces a private instance tied to that component's lifecycle.
  • Stateless services → share as a root singleton; subtree-private state → provide at the component.

Adapted from branch 3-services · reimplemented for modern standalone Angular.

let counter = 0;

// providedIn: 'root' registers the app-wide singleton — instance #1.
@Injectable({ providedIn: 'root' })
export class CoursesService {
  id = ++counter;                       // unique per instance
  constructor() { console.log('created CoursesService', this.id); }
}

// Root component: NO local provider → inherits the root singleton (#1).
@Component({ selector: 'app-root', standalone: true, /* ... */ })
export class AppComponent {
  private courses = inject(CoursesService);
  ngOnInit() { console.log('root uses', this.courses.id); }
}

// Card component: its OWN component-level provider → private instance per card.
@Component({ selector: 'course-card', standalone: true, providers: [CoursesService] })
export class CourseCardComponent {
  private courses = inject(CoursesService);
  ngOnInit() { console.log('card uses', this.courses.id); }
}
Console (10 cards, card-level provider)created CoursesService 1   // root singleton, built on first inject
root uses 1
created CoursesService 2   // first card's own private copy
card uses 2
created CoursesService 3
card uses 3
...  (each card a distinct id)

// Remove the card provider → no #2, #3, …;
// every "card uses" then logs 1 (the root singleton).
PitfallPutting providers: [SomeService] on a repeated child component silently creates one instance per rendered instance. If you expected a shared singleton, state written by one card will be invisible to the others.
Advanced — lifecycle of a component-scoped service

Instances created by a component's own provider are destroyed when that component is destroyed — their memory is released with the component, and any ngOnDestroy on the service runs. Root singletons live for the entire application session. This makes component-level providers a natural home for per-view state that should not outlive the view.

5 · Angular Tree-Shakeable Providers

Objective: Register a provider from inside @Injectable using providedIn so unused services are dropped from the bundle (tree-shaking) — and know when to prefer it over component providers.

A provider listed in a component's or module's providers array is referenced from that decorator's metadata, so the bundler must keep the service's code even if nothing ever injects it. Multiply that across a third-party library exposing many services you don't use, and the bundle carries dead code. The DI system is already lazy at runtime — an un-requested provider's factory never runs — but the code still ships.

The fix is to configure the provider at the service itself via @Injectable({ providedIn: 'root', … }). Because the registration lives on the class, the bundler can reason that if the class is never imported/injected anywhere, both the class and its provider are safe to eliminate — a tree-shakeable provider. The providedIn field also accepts the same useClass / useFactory (+ deps) / useValue recipes, but providedIn is mandatory: it names the injector level ('root' for an app-wide singleton). Prefer this form for stateless singletons; reserve component providers for services whose state must be scoped to a component.

  • Component/module providers pin the service into the bundle even when unused.
  • providedIn: 'root' on @Injectable makes the provider tree-shakeable and app-wide.
  • The same useFactory/useClass/useValue options apply; providedIn is required.
  • Default to tree-shakeable providers; use component providers only for component-scoped state.

Adapted from branch 3-services · reimplemented for modern standalone Angular.

// Simplest: class-as-token singleton, tree-shakeable.
@Injectable({ providedIn: 'root' })
export class CoursesService {
  private http = inject(HttpClient);
  loadAllCourses(): Observable<Course[]> {
    return this.http.get<Course[]>('/api/courses');
  }
}

// Explicit factory form — still tree-shakeable, provided in root:
@Injectable({
  providedIn: 'root',
  useFactory: (http: HttpClient) => new CoursesService(http),
  deps: [HttpClient],
})
export class CoursesService { /* ... */ }
Bundle effectIf nothing injects CoursesService,
the bundler removes its code entirely.
If injected anywhere, it is included
and a single root instance is created
lazily on first request.
PitfallRegistering the same service both with providedIn: 'root' and again in a component's providers array creates two separate instances. The component-level one shadows the root singleton for that subtree — rarely what you intend.
Advanced — other providedIn scopes

Beyond 'root', providedIn accepts 'platform' (shared across multiple Angular apps on one page) and 'any' (a distinct instance per lazy-loaded injector). Passing a specific NgModule class ties the singleton to that module's injector. For most apps, 'root' is the right and default choice.

6 · Injection Tokens In Detail — injecting non-class values

Objective: Inject a plain configuration object (not a class) by defining an InjectionToken<T>, supply it with useValue or useFactory, and make it tree-shakeable via the token's factory.

Not everything worth injecting is a class instance — application-wide configuration is typically a plain object. Since a type/interface has no runtime identity, it cannot be a token; we create an explicit InjectionToken<AppConfig>('description') instead. Registering it with a useFactory that returns the object works, but the simpler useValue injects the object directly. Either way, consumers must ask for it by token: inject(APP_CONFIG) (or @Inject(APP_CONFIG) on a constructor parameter), because the object has no class Angular could infer from.

A provider declared in a component's providers array is not tree-shakeable — inspect the bundle and the config object is present even when unused. To make it tree-shakeable, drop the array registration and move the recipe onto the token itself: new InjectionToken<AppConfig>('APP_CONFIG', { providedIn: 'root', factory: () => APP_CONFIG }). Now the config is bundled only if something injects it. (Note: the config's type never appears in the bundle regardless — it is a compile-time construct.)

  • Use InjectionToken<T> to inject anything that isn't a class (objects, primitives, functions).
  • useValue injects a ready object; useFactory computes one (with optional deps).
  • Consumers must request the token explicitly via inject(TOKEN) / @Inject(TOKEN).
  • A token with a { providedIn, factory } second argument is tree-shakeable.

Adapted from branch 3-services · reimplemented for modern standalone Angular.

// config.ts
export type AppConfig = { apiUrl: string; courseCacheSize: number };
export const APP_CONFIG: AppConfig = { apiUrl: 'http://localhost:9000', courseCacheSize: 10 };

// Tree-shakeable token: recipe lives on the token, not in a providers array.
export const CONFIG_TOKEN = new InjectionToken<AppConfig>('CONFIG_TOKEN', {
  providedIn: 'root',
  factory: () => APP_CONFIG,
});

@Component({ selector: 'app-root', standalone: true, /* no providers needed */ })
export class AppComponent {
  private config = inject(CONFIG_TOKEN);
  constructor() { console.log('config', this.config); }
}

// Non-tree-shakeable alternative (object stays in bundle even if unused):
//   providers: [{ provide: CONFIG_TOKEN, useValue: APP_CONFIG }]
Consoleconfig { apiUrl: 'http://localhost:9000',
         courseCacheSize: 10 }

// Bundle: with the factory token, APP_CONFIG
// is included ONLY when injected. The type
// AppConfig never appears (compile-time only).
PitfallTrying to inject(AppConfig) — the type — fails with Can't resolve all parameters. Types/interfaces vanish at runtime; you must inject the InjectionToken value, never the type.
Advanced — useValue vs a factory token for config

useValue is fine for a static object, but a factory token can compute config lazily and pull in other dependencies through inject() inside the factory (Angular runs the factory in an injection context). That lets configuration depend on, say, the current environment or another injected service — impossible with a frozen useValue literal.

7 · DI Decorators — @Optional, @Self, @SkipSelf

Objective: Control resolution with three modifiers: tolerate a missing dependency (@Optional), forbid climbing the tree (@Self), or force climbing past the local injector (@SkipSelf).

By default a constructor dependency is mandatory — absent a provider, the app crashes. @Optional relaxes this: instead of throwing, Angular injects null, and it becomes your job to guard against it before use (otherwise you swap No provider for a Cannot read property of null). @Self restricts resolution to the component's own injector — if there's no local provider it errors rather than inheriting a parent's instance; use it to guarantee a truly private copy. @SkipSelf is the mirror image: it ignores the local injector entirely and starts the search at the parent, guaranteeing the dependency comes from further up the tree. These are advanced tools — the default hierarchical behaviour is usually exactly right, so reach for them only in the rare cases that need it.

With inject(), the same intent is expressed through an options object rather than parameter decorators, which reads more cleanly: inject(CoursesService, { optional: true }), { self: true }, or { skipSelf: true }.

  • @Optional → inject null instead of throwing when no provider exists (guard the null).
  • @Self → resolve only from the local injector; error if absent (no inheritance).
  • @SkipSelf → skip the local injector; resolve from parents upward.
  • inject() options: { optional }, { self }, { skipSelf } replace the decorators.

Adapted from branch 3-services · reimplemented for modern standalone Angular.

@Component({ selector: 'course-card', standalone: true, providers: [CoursesService] })
export class CourseCardComponent {
  // Optional: may be null → must guard.
  private maybeLogger = inject(LoggerService, { optional: true });

  // Self: only this component's own provider counts.
  private local = inject(CoursesService, { self: true });

  // SkipSelf: ignore the local provider, take a parent's instance.
  private parentCourses = inject(CoursesService, { skipSelf: true });

  ngOnInit() {
    this.maybeLogger?.log('safe even when no provider');   // guarded
    console.log('local id', this.local.id, 'parent id', this.parentCourses.id);
  }
}

// Classic decorator equivalents (constructor form):
// constructor(@Optional() logger: LoggerService,
//             @Self() local: CoursesService,
//             @SkipSelf() parent: CoursesService) {}
Consolelocal id 5      // this card's own instance
parent id 1     // inherited from root

// @Self with no local provider  → No provider for CoursesService!
// @SkipSelf but no parent provider → No provider for CoursesService!
// @Optional with nothing to inject → value is null (no crash)
Pitfall@Optional turns a loud startup error into a silent null. Every optional injection needs a null check (?. or an if) at the point of use, or you merely defer the crash to the first method call.
Advanced — combining modifiers

Modifiers compose: inject(X, { self: true, optional: true }) means "give me the local instance, or null if this component doesn't provide it" — never inheriting from a parent. { skipSelf: true, optional: true } means "give me an ancestor's instance, or null". These pairings are common when a directive wants to optionally coordinate with a host-provided service without demanding it.

8 · The @Host Decorator & Section Conclusion

Objective: Use @Host so a directive resolves a dependency only up to its host element, and consolidate when each DI modifier is warranted.

A directive applied to a component treats that component as its host element. Normally, injecting a service into the directive resolves the same way as anywhere — climbing the injector tree until it finds a provider, often landing on the root singleton (every instance of the directive then shares service #1). @Host caps that upward search at the host element's injector: the dependency must be provided by the host component (or the directive itself), and resolution will not travel higher. If the host component has no such provider, injection errors — precisely because @Host refuses to reach past it to the root. This is the tool for a directive that is tightly coupled to its host and wants a service the host owns.

Conclusion. These modifiers are rarely needed. The overwhelming default — a stateless service as a tree-shakeable providedIn: 'root' singleton, injected without any modifier — covers most real applications. Reach for @Self when you need a component's own local instance, @SkipSelf to force an ancestor's instance, @Optional to tolerate a missing provider, and @Host when a directive must draw a dependency from its host element. Next the course moves on to change detection — default strategy, OnPush, and custom detection.

  • @Host limits resolution to the host element's injector (host component or the directive itself).
  • Without a host-level provider, @Host injection errors instead of climbing to root.
  • It suits directives tightly coupled to the component they decorate.
  • Default to modifier-free root singletons; modifiers are exceptions, not the norm.

Adapted from branch 3-services · reimplemented for modern standalone Angular.

@Directive({ selector: '[highlighted]', standalone: true })
export class HighlightedDirective {
  // Resolve ONLY from the host element's injector — no climbing to root.
  private courses = inject(CoursesService, { host: true });
  constructor() { console.log('directive got CoursesService', this.courses.id); }
}

// Host component MUST provide the service, or @Host injection fails.
@Component({
  selector: 'course-card',
  standalone: true,
  imports: [HighlightedDirective],
  template: `<div highlighted>{{ title }}</div>`,
  providers: [CoursesService],   // required for the host-scoped injection
})
export class CourseCardComponent { title = 'RxJS'; }

// Constructor-decorator equivalent:
// constructor(@Host() private courses: CoursesService) {}
Console// With providers on the host card:
directive got CoursesService 2
directive got CoursesService 3
...        // each card's private instance

// Remove the host provider →
ERROR NullInjectorError: No provider
for CoursesService! (found by @Host)
Pitfall@Host deliberately will not fall back to the root singleton. A directive using it on a host that lacks the provider throws No provider — even though the service exists at root. Pair @Host with a host-level provider, or make it { host: true, optional: true }.
Advanced — @Host beyond directives

@Host also matters for content projection: a projected child component can use @Host to grab a service provided by the component into which it is projected, without accidentally resolving a same-typed service higher in the tree. This is how form-control directives bind to the NgForm/ControlContainer of their immediate host form rather than an outer one.

Cheat sheet — Angular DI
  • Provider recipes: useClass (new instance), useValue (ready object), useFactory + deps (computed), useExisting (alias to one existing instance).
  • Register a singleton: @Injectable({ providedIn: 'root' }) — tree-shakeable, app-wide, lazy.
  • Component-scoped: providers: [Svc] on the component → private instance, dies with the component. Not tree-shakeable.
  • Shorthand: [Svc] ≡ { provide: Svc, useClass: Svc }.
  • Non-class value: new InjectionToken<T>('name', { providedIn:'root', factory:() => value }) → tree-shakeable; consume with inject(TOKEN).
  • Resolution: local injector → parents → root → null injector (error).
  • Modifiers (as inject() options): { optional } null-if-absent · { self } local only · { skipSelf } parents only · { host } up to host element.
  • Rule of thumb: stateless → root singleton, no modifier. Reach for modifiers only in rare, specific cases.

Quick check

1. What does NullInjectorError: No provider for CoursesService most directly indicate?

Show answer
  1. The service constructor threw an exception.
  2. No provider (recipe) for that token is registered in any reachable injector.
  3. The HTTP backend is unreachable.
  4. The component template has a syntax error.

b — the injector was asked for the token but has no way to build it. Register a provider (e.g. providedIn: 'root' or a providers entry).

2. Why is @Injectable({ providedIn: 'root' }) preferred over listing the service in a component's providers array for a stateless singleton?

Show answer
  1. It runs the constructor eagerly at startup.
  2. It allows injecting interfaces as tokens.
  3. It is tree-shakeable — the service is dropped from the bundle if never injected.
  4. It creates a new instance per component.

c — registration lives on the class, so the bundler can eliminate unused services. Component providers keep the code in the bundle regardless of use.

3. A directive uses inject(CoursesService, { host: true }) but its host component has no provider for it, even though the service is providedIn: 'root'. What happens?

Show answer
  1. It resolves to the root singleton.
  2. It throws No provider — @Host refuses to climb past the host element.
  3. It injects null automatically.
  4. It creates a fresh instance in the directive.

b — @Host caps resolution at the host element's injector and will not reach the root. Add a host-level provider, or use { host: true, optional: true } to get null instead.

Angular Change Detection In Depth

Change detection is the machinery that keeps the rendered view in sync with your component data. This section works through the two built-in strategies — the Default strategy that is active out of the box, and the opt-in OnPush strategy that trades convenience for speed — then shows how OnPush cooperates with observable streams and the async pipe. It closes with two lower-level tools: the @Attribute decorator for values that never change, and ChangeDetectorRef for the rare cases where you must drive detection by hand.

1. Default Change Detection — How It Works

Objective: Understand what triggers Angular's default change detection and why it can safely track mutable objects.

Angular's default strategy rebuilds the affected portion of the view automatically whenever the model changes — you never wire it up yourself. After the template is compiled, Angular records every binding expression it contains. Then, following any asynchronous browser activity, it re-evaluates each of those expressions and compares the new result against the value from the previous pass. When an expression's value differs, the corresponding piece of DOM is updated; when nothing changed, the component is left untouched.

The set of triggers is broad on purpose: DOM events (a click, a keyup), custom @Output emissions, HTTP responses, and timers such as setTimeout or setInterval all cause a full sweep. Rather than memorise the list, hold onto the underlying rule: any async event might have mutated the data behind a plain JavaScript object, so the only way Angular can guarantee correctness is to re-check the whole component tree top-down. Under the hood, Zone.js is what notifies Angular that one of these async events fired. This is extremely reliable but, on very large trees, checking every expression after every event carries a cost — though most applications never feel it.

  • Detection runs top-down over the entire component tree after each async event.
  • Each binding's current value is compared to its previous value; a difference forces a DOM update.
  • Zone.js patches browser APIs (events, timers, XHR) to tell Angular when to run a pass.
  • Because it re-reads every expression, it tolerates direct mutation of objects and arrays.

Adapted from the course example on branch master · reimplemented for modern standalone Angular.

The playground for this lecture feeds its list from a hardcoded courses array in a local db-data file rather than a live backend, which keeps the focus on detection itself. The component also carries no providers array, because CoursesService registers itself through a tree-shakeable providedIn: 'root' injector — so it is available everywhere without being listed locally.

Here a keystroke handler mutates the bound course object in place. Default detection still picks up the edited title because it re-evaluates the interpolation on every keyup.

import { Component, Input } from '@angular/core';

@Component({
  selector: 'course-card',
  standalone: true,
  template: `
    <h2>{{ course.description }}</h2>
    <input #titleInput
           (keyup)="onTitleChanged(titleInput.value)"
           placeholder="Edit title">
  `
})
export class CourseCardComponent {
  @Input() course!: { id: number; description: string };

  onTitleChanged(newTitle: string) {
    // Direct mutation — allowed under the default strategy
    this.course.description = newTitle;
  }
}
Rendered behaviourTyping "Angular Core" into the box
updates the <h2> live, character by
character. Each keyup triggers a pass;
the interpolation is re-read and the
DOM heading is patched.
Pitfall Bind to keyup, not keydown, when you need the latest typed value. On keydown the input's value has not yet incorporated the newly pressed key, so you read a stale string.
Advanced — why Zone.js is the trigger, and life without it

Zone.js monkey-patches asynchronous browser APIs so that when a patched callback finishes, Angular is told to run change detection. That is why a bare setTimeout callback that mutates data still repaints the view without any manual call. Modern Angular can also run zoneless (via provideZonelessChangeDetection()), in which case signals and explicit notifications replace Zone-driven passes — a direction that makes OnPush-style thinking the default.

2. OnPush Change Detection — How It Works and What Triggers It

Objective: Learn how OnPush skips subtrees for performance, and the precise conditions that still cause an OnPush component to be checked.

OnPush is faster than the default strategy but demands more discipline — a deliberate trade-off. An OnPush component is not re-checked on every event throughout the app. Instead Angular checks it only when one of a small number of conditions holds: one of its @Input references changes (compared by identity, not by deep contents), an event handler fires inside the component or its own template, an observable it consumes through the async pipe emits, or — in newer Angular — a signal it reads changes. When none of these occur, Angular walks past the component and its entire subtree without inspecting a single expression.

The win is about CPU, not memory. Every component — OnPush or not — stores the previous value of each of its own template bindings in its internal view data (the LView) so it can diff them when it is checked. OnPush does not drop that per-binding cache: when an OnPush component actually is checked (a changed input reference, an internal event, an async-pipe emission, or a signal it reads), it re-diffs its bindings exactly like a default component. What OnPush saves is work — when a whole subtree's inputs are unchanged, Angular skips checking it at all, so those comparisons never run. Fewer components checked, not fewer values stored.

The critical detail is the identity comparison on inputs. If a parent mutates a nested property of an object it already passed down (course.description = '...'), the object reference is unchanged, so an OnPush child sees no new input and does not re-render — a common source of "why won't my view update?" bugs. Replacing the object with a fresh copy ({ ...course, description }) changes the reference and the OnPush child renders correctly. This is exactly why immutable data pairs so naturally with OnPush.

  • OnPush checks a component only on: input reference change, an event within the component, an async-pipe emission, or a signal read change.
  • Inputs are compared by reference — mutating a nested field is invisible to OnPush.
  • Skipping a component skips its whole subtree, which is where the performance win comes from.
  • The saving is CPU, not memory: every component caches its bindings' previous values to diff when checked; OnPush's win is skipping the check of untriggered subtrees, not omitting that cache.
  • Prefer immutable updates (object spread, Object.freeze, Immer/Immutable.js) to stay safe.

Adapted from the course example on branch master · reimplemented for modern standalone Angular.

Below, a parent button mutates a nested field versus replacing the whole object. Only the reference swap reaches the OnPush child.

import { ChangeDetectionStrategy, Component, Input } from '@angular/core';

@Component({
  selector: 'course-card',
  standalone: true,
  changeDetection: ChangeDetectionStrategy.OnPush,   // OnPush belongs on the child that renders the input
  template: `<h2>{{ course.description }}</h2>`
})
export class CourseCardComponent {
  @Input() course!: { id: number; description: string };
}

@Component({
  selector: 'app-root',
  standalone: true,
  template: `
    <button (click)="mutate()">Mutate in place</button>
    <button (click)="replace()">Replace reference</button>
    <course-card [course]="courses[0]"></course-card>
  `
})
export class AppComponent {
  courses = [{ id: 1, description: 'Angular Basics' }];

  mutate() {              // reference unchanged -> OnPush child NOT updated
    this.courses[0].description = 'Edited';
  }

  replace() {             // new reference -> OnPush child IS updated
    this.courses[0] = { ...this.courses[0], description: 'Edited' };
  }
}
Console / viewClick "Mutate in place":
  card still shows "Angular Basics"
  (OnPush ignored the nested change)

Click "Replace reference":
  card now shows "Edited"
  (new object reference detected)
Pitfall Under OnPush, mutating a property of an existing input object leaves the view stale. Always produce a new object/array reference for data flowing into OnPush children, or the update silently disappears.
Advanced — OnPush and signals

When an OnPush template reads a signal, Angular records a dependency. Updating that signal marks the component for check automatically — no reference juggling required. This makes signals the ergonomic modern answer to OnPush's "how do I notify the view?" question, effectively giving you OnPush-level performance with default-level convenience.

3. OnPush with Observable Data Streams

Objective: See why the async pipe is essential for feeding observable data into OnPush components.

With OnPush there are two supported ways to push data into a component: through input properties, and through observables bound with the async pipe. The async pipe is a first-class OnPush trigger — whenever its source observable emits, Angular marks the component for check and re-renders it. So a template using @if (courses$ | async; as courses) continues to display fresh backend data even under OnPush.

The trap appears when you subscribe manually and assign the emitted value to a plain field. Manually assigning this.courses = value inside a subscribe callback is neither an input change, an in-component event, nor an async-pipe emission — so an OnPush component has no idea it should re-render, and the list never appears. Switching that same code back to the async pipe fixes it instantly. As a bonus, the async pipe also unsubscribes automatically when the component is destroyed, preventing leaks.
