  • OnPush data arrives two ways: @Input references, or observables via the async pipe.
  • Each async-pipe emission marks the OnPush component for check — no manual work needed.
  • A manual subscribe + field assignment does not trigger OnPush and leaves the view empty.
  • The async pipe also handles unsubscription on destroy.

Adapted from the course example on branch master · reimplemented for modern standalone Angular.

First, the trap in full — an OnPush component that subscribes by hand and stores the result in a field. It compiles, the HTTP call succeeds, and the list still renders blank, because none of OnPush's triggers ever fired.

@Component({
  selector: 'app-root',
  standalone: true,
  changeDetection: ChangeDetectionStrategy.OnPush,
  template: `
    <!-- BROKEN under OnPush: nothing marks the component for check -->
    @for (c of courses; track c.id) {
      <course-card [course]="c"></course-card>
    }
  `
})
export class AppComponent implements OnInit {
  courses: Course[] = [];

  constructor(private coursesService: CoursesService) {}

  ngOnInit() {
    this.coursesService.loadCourses()
      .subscribe(list => {
        // Assigning a field is NOT an OnPush trigger
        this.courses = list;
      });
  }
}
Result — brokenHTTP request succeeds and `courses`
holds 10 items in memory...
but the @for renders NOTHING.

The subscribe callback is not an input
change, an in-component event, or an
async-pipe emission — so OnPush never
schedules a pass and the view is blank.

Now the fix: hand the same observable to the template through the async pipe. Each emission is a first-class OnPush trigger, so the list appears the moment the response lands.

@Component({
  selector: 'app-root',
  standalone: true,
  imports: [AsyncPipe],
  changeDetection: ChangeDetectionStrategy.OnPush,
  template: `
    <!-- WORKS under OnPush: async pipe marks for check on emit -->
    @if (courses$ | async; as courses) {
      @for (c of courses; track c.id) {
        <course-card [course]="c"></course-card>
      }
    }
  `
})
export class AppComponent implements OnInit {
  courses$!: Observable<Course[]>;

  constructor(private coursesService: CoursesService) {}

  ngOnInit() {
    this.courses$ = this.coursesService.loadCourses();
  }
}
Result — fixedList renders as soon as the HTTP
response arrives. Every later emission
repaints it too, and the pipe
unsubscribes automatically on destroy.

Only change from the broken version:
a field + subscribe became an
observable + `| async`.
Pitfall Do not hand-roll subscribe and assign to a field in an OnPush component. Prefer | async; if you truly must subscribe manually, you have to call markForCheck() yourself (next lecture).
Advanced — combining streams cleanly

When a template needs several observables, either apply async to each, or merge them upstream with RxJS combineLatest and expose one view-model observable. A single @if (vm$ | async; as vm) keeps the template flat and preserves the OnPush guarantee that every emission repaints the view.

4. The @Attribute Decorator — When to Use It

Objective: Use @Attribute to read a fixed, one-time value that Angular should never re-check.

Every @Input becomes a binding that Angular evaluates on each change-detection pass. For a value that is set once at creation and will never change — a static "kind" or "variant" string, for example — that continual checking is wasted effort. The @Attribute decorator reads the attribute a single time when the component is constructed and then forgets about it, so it contributes nothing to subsequent detection passes.

You inject it as a constructor parameter: @Attribute('type') private type: string. Note the distinction from the shorthand template syntax: writing type="beginner" without brackets can also feed a normal @Input — the bracket-free form is just how you pass a literal string to an input, and it does not by itself imply @Attribute. The decorator is specifically the performance optimisation that makes the read one-time.

  • @Attribute reads a host attribute once, at construction — never re-checked.
  • Best for values that are constant for the component's lifetime.
  • Bracket-free attr="value" alone still targets a normal @Input; @Attribute is the opt-out from re-checking.
  • The value is always a string (or null if the attribute is absent).

Adapted from the course example on branch master · reimplemented for modern standalone Angular.

import { Attribute, Component } from '@angular/core';

@Component({
  selector: 'course-card',
  standalone: true,
  template: `<div class="card {{ type }}">...</div>`
})
export class CourseCardComponent {
  constructor(@Attribute('type') public type: string) {
    // Read exactly once when the component is created
    console.log('card type =', this.type);
  }
}

// Usage — a literal that never changes over time:
// <course-card type="beginner"></course-card>
ConsoleReload with ten <course-card>s on screen:

card type = beginner
card type = beginner
card type = beginner
... (exactly 10 lines — one per instance,
     each logged from its constructor)

Then click, type, trigger timers — the
count stays at 10. The 'type' value is
NEVER revisited on later change-detection
passes, so it costs nothing per event.

To feel the difference, put a plain @Input next to an @Attribute and log from each. The input logs from ngDoCheck, which runs on every pass, so it keeps printing; the attribute logs from the constructor, which runs once. Watching the two counters diverge is the concrete version of the claim that @Attribute is excused from every subsequent pass.

import { Attribute, Component, Input, DoCheck } from '@angular/core';

// A — @Input: re-evaluated on EVERY change-detection pass
@Component({
  selector: 'input-card',
  standalone: true,
  template: `<div class="card {{ type }}">input</div>`
})
export class InputCardComponent implements DoCheck {
  @Input() type = '';
  ngDoCheck() {
    console.log('[@Input] type =', this.type); // logs each pass
  }
}

// B — @Attribute: read ONCE at construction, never re-checked
@Component({
  selector: 'attr-card',
  standalone: true,
  template: `<div class="card {{ type }}">attr</div>`
})
export class AttrCardComponent {
  constructor(@Attribute('type') public type: string) {
    console.log('[@Attribute] type =', this.type); // logs once
  }
}
ConsoleInitial render (one of each):
  [@Attribute] type = beginner   (constructor, once)
  [@Input] type = beginner       (first ngDoCheck)

After ANY later event (click / keyup / timer):
  [@Input] type = beginner       (logged AGAIN, every pass)
  [@Input] type = beginner
  ...                            (@Attribute never logs again)
Pitfall @Attribute captures the value only at construction. If you expect the value to change over time and update the view, use a normal @Input — an attribute read will never see the later value.
Advanced — is it worth it?

@Attribute is a micro-optimisation. Its clearest benefit is on components rendered in large numbers (long lists) where trimming even one binding per instance adds up, and it also documents intent: this value is fixed. For everyday components the saving is negligible — reach for it only when profiling points at binding overhead.

5. Custom Change Detection with ChangeDetectorRef

Objective: Drive change detection manually with ChangeDetectorRef — markForCheck, detectChanges, detach, and reattach — for the rare cases the two strategies cannot cover.

Each component owns its own change detector, injectable as ChangeDetectorRef. It exposes an API for the exceptional situations where neither default nor OnPush detection does what you need. The most common member is markForCheck(): it flags the component (and its ancestors up to the root) so that the next detection pass includes it. This is what you call when you have manually subscribed inside an OnPush component and need to tell Angular that fresh data arrived.

The DoCheck lifecycle hook is the intended home for custom detection logic — it runs on every pass, so you can decide, per pass, whether to call markForCheck(). For instance you might render only the first backend emission and ignore later ones, guarded by a boolean flag. Beyond marking, the API also offers detectChanges() to run a pass on this component immediately and synchronously, and the pair detach() / reattach() to remove a subtree from automatic checking entirely and later restore it — useful when you want to throttle updates from a high-frequency data source to, say, once per second. Treat all of this as a last resort: default detection handles most cases, OnPush handles most performance needs, and reaching for the async pipe is usually simpler than manual marking.

  • markForCheck() — schedules this component (and ancestors) for the next pass; ideal after a manual subscribe under OnPush.
  • detectChanges() — runs a pass on this component now, synchronously.
  • detach() / reattach() — take a subtree off automatic detection and put it back; enables throttling.
  • DoCheck is the correct hook for custom, per-pass detection decisions.
  • Use sparingly — a last resort after Default, OnPush, and the async pipe.

Adapted from the course example on branch master · reimplemented for modern standalone Angular.

@Component({
  selector: 'app-root',
  standalone: true,
  changeDetection: ChangeDetectionStrategy.OnPush,
  template: `
    @for (c of courses; track c.id) {
      <course-card [course]="c"></course-card>
    }
  `
})
export class AppComponent implements OnInit, DoCheck {
  courses: Course[] = [];
  private loaded = false;

  constructor(
    private coursesService: CoursesService,
    private cd: ChangeDetectorRef
  ) {}

  ngOnInit() {
    // Manual subscribe under OnPush -> must notify Angular ourselves
    this.coursesService.loadCourses()
      .subscribe(list => {
        this.courses = list;
        this.loaded = true;
      });
  }

  ngDoCheck() {
    // Render only the FIRST batch, then stop marking
    console.log('ngDoCheck');
    if (this.loaded) {
      this.cd.markForCheck();
      console.log('cd.markForCheck()');
      this.loaded = false;
    }
  }
}
ConsolengDoCheck
ngDoCheck
ngDoCheck            (fires every pass)
cd.markForCheck()    (once, on first data)

View shows the initial list. Later
emissions are intentionally ignored
because the flag gates the mark call.
Pitfall Do not scatter markForCheck()/detectChanges() throughout a component as a fix for stale views. That usually signals a data-flow problem — in the example above, simply using courses$ | async removes the need for any manual detection at all.
Advanced — markForCheck vs detectChanges vs detach

markForCheck() does not run detection itself; it only sets a flag so the next scheduled pass includes the component and its ancestors — cooperative and safe. detectChanges() runs a pass on the current component subtree immediately and can be called even while detached, which is how you implement "update this branch on my own schedule". detach() fully removes a subtree from Angular's automatic checking; pair it with a timer plus detectChanges() to throttle a firehose stream, then reattach() to hand control back to Angular.

Section cheat sheet
  • Default: checks the whole tree top-down after every async event (Zone.js driven); tolerates mutation; safe and effortless.
  • OnPush: checks only on input reference change, in-component event, async-pipe emit, or signal read change; skips whole subtrees for speed.
  • OnPush + streams: always feed observables via | async; a manual subscribe + assignment will not trigger a re-render.
  • @Attribute('name'): one-time constructor read for fixed values; excused from every subsequent pass.
  • ChangeDetectorRef: markForCheck() (schedule next pass), detectChanges() (run now), detach()/reattach() (throttle a subtree); put custom logic in ngDoCheck. Last resort.
  • Rule of thumb: stay on Default; adopt OnPush only for measured performance problems; reach for manual detection almost never.

Quick check — 3 questions

1. An OnPush child receives a course object as an input. A parent does this.course.title = 'New'. Why does the child's view not update?

Show answer
  1. OnPush disables all input bindings.
  2. The object reference is unchanged, and OnPush compares inputs by identity, not by nested contents.
  3. Zone.js is not loaded.
  4. The parent must call detach() first.

B — OnPush only reacts to a new input reference; mutating a field of the same object is invisible. Assign a fresh copy instead.

2. Which of these does not trigger a check of an OnPush component?

Show answer
  1. An event handler firing inside the component's template.
  2. An observable bound with the async pipe emitting a value.
  3. An @Input receiving a new object reference.
  4. A manual subscribe callback assigning a value to a plain component field.

D — a hand-rolled subscription that mutates a field is none of OnPush's triggers, so the view stays stale unless you call markForCheck() or use the async pipe.

3. You need to render only the first emission from a high-frequency stream in an OnPush component and ignore the rest. Which approach fits best?

Show answer
  1. Switch the component to the default strategy.
  2. Implement ngDoCheck and call cd.markForCheck() once, gated by a boolean flag.
  3. Add @Attribute to the stream field.
  4. Call detach() and never reattach.

B — DoCheck runs each pass, so a flag lets you mark for check exactly once; @Attribute is unrelated, and permanently detaching would stop all rendering.

Angular Lifecycle Hooks In Depth

Every Angular component moves through a well-defined sequence of stages, from construction to destruction, and the framework offers a callback method for each stage. This section walks through those lifecycle hooks one by one — initialization (ngOnInit), input-change notification (ngOnChanges with its SimpleChanges payload), the per-cycle checks (ngAfterContentChecked and ngAfterViewChecked, including the notorious ExpressionChangedAfterItHasBeenCheckedError), and teardown (ngOnDestroy) — before closing with the complete ordering of all hooks and when to reach for each one.

ngOnInit and ngOnDestroy In Detail

Objective: Understand why ngOnInit exists alongside the constructor, and how ngOnDestroy gives a component a chance to release resources before it is removed.

Lifecycle hooks are methods the Angular framework calls at specific moments — you implement them, but you never invoke them yourself from application code. The first and most common is ngOnInit, declared through the OnInit interface. Logging inside it shows it fires exactly once per component, no matter how many events the component later handles.

The key difference from the constructor becomes clear when you log the value of an @Input() property in both places. In the constructor the input is still undefined, because Angular constructs the class first and only afterwards populates its bound inputs. By the time ngOnInit runs, any input bound synchronously in the parent template is set — but read that guarantee carefully. ngOnInit promises that the class is fully initialized and that statically bound inputs are present; it does not promise that asynchronously supplied data has arrived. If a parent fills the input from a backend call (an HTTP subscribe) or a setTimeout, the value is still undefined inside ngOnInit and only shows up later, through ngOnChanges. So ngOnInit is the right home for initialization that depends on synchronously bound inputs, while the constructor should do nothing beyond capturing injected dependencies into fields.

The counterpart hook is ngOnDestroy, from the OnDestroy interface. Angular calls it right before the component instance is torn down — for example when an @if condition wrapping the component evaluates to false and removes it from the DOM. In the lecture this is forced deliberately: the bound array is reassigned to one whose single element is undefined, so the @if guarding the card turns false and Angular removes (and therefore destroys) the component. It is the natural place to free long-lived resources such as manual subscriptions to observables. In practice it is rarely needed: passing observables through the async pipe lets Angular unsubscribe automatically, so most components use ngOnInit often and ngOnDestroy seldom.

  • Lifecycle hooks are called by the framework — never call them directly from your own code.
  • The constructor runs before inputs are set; @Input() values are still undefined there.
  • ngOnInit runs once, after inputs are populated — the right home for initialization logic.
  • Keep the constructor limited to storing injected dependencies; no real logic.
  • ngOnDestroy fires just before teardown — ideal for cleanup, but often replaced by the async pipe.
import { Component, Input, OnInit, OnDestroy } from '@angular/core';

@Component({
  selector: 'course-card',
  standalone: true,
  template: `<h2>{{ course?.title }}</h2>`
})
export class CourseCardComponent implements OnInit, OnDestroy {
  @Input() course: any;

  constructor() {
    console.log('constructor', this.course); // undefined
  }

  ngOnInit() {
    console.log('ngOnInit', this.course);    // input is available
  }

  ngOnDestroy() {
    console.log('ngOnDestroy — releasing resources');
  }
}
Outputconstructor undefined
ngOnInit { id: 1, title: 'Angular Core' }
// when the component is removed from the DOM:
ngOnDestroy — releasing resources

The caveat above is easiest to see by contrasting two parents that feed the same input. A synchronous binding has the value ready before ngOnInit; an asynchronous one (an HTTP call, a setTimeout) leaves the input undefined in ngOnInit and only fills it in later, which ngOnChanges then reports.

// (a) SYNCHRONOUS binding — value exists before ngOnInit runs:
//     <course-card [course]="courses[0]"></course-card>

// (b) ASYNCHRONOUS binding — value arrives AFTER ngOnInit runs:
@Component({ template: `<course-card [course]="course"></course-card>` })
export class ParentComponent implements OnInit {
  course: any;                            // starts undefined
  constructor(private http: HttpClient) {}
  ngOnInit() {
    this.http.get('/api/courses/1')
      .subscribe(c => this.course = c);   // resolves LATER
  }
}

// Inside CourseCardComponent (implements OnInit, OnChanges):
ngOnInit()    { console.log('ngOnInit', this.course); }
ngOnChanges() { console.log('ngOnChanges', this.course); }
Output// case (a) synchronous binding:
ngOnChanges { id: 1, title: 'Angular Core' } // firstChange: true
ngOnInit { id: 1, title: 'Angular Core' }
// case (b) asynchronous binding (HTTP resolves after init):
ngOnChanges undefined // firstChange: true
ngOnInit undefined
ngOnChanges { id: 1, title: 'Angular Core' } // firstChange: false — value arrives later

Setup mirrors branch master of angular-university/angular-course · reimplemented for clarity.

Common pitfallPutting input-dependent logic in the constructor. The input is still undefined at that point, so the code either crashes or silently uses stale data. Move it to ngOnInit.
AdvancedModern Angular offers cleaner teardown than manual ngOnDestroy unsubscription: inject DestroyRef and register callbacks with destroyRef.onDestroy(fn), or pipe observables through takeUntilDestroyed() (from @angular/core/rxjs-interop) so they complete automatically when the component is destroyed — no explicit hook or Subscription bookkeeping required.

ngOnChanges and the SimpleChanges Object

Objective: Learn how ngOnChanges notifies a component when its input properties change, and why only reference changes — not property mutations — trigger it.

When you need to react whenever an @Input() value changes — for example, comparing the previous value with the new one — implement the OnChanges interface. Its single method, ngOnChanges, receives a SimpleChanges object keyed by input name. Each entry is a SimpleChange holding previousValue, currentValue, and firstChange (a boolean flag for the very first invocation). When a component has several inputs, each one that changed appears as its own key in the same call: if both course and cardIndex change, changes carries a separate SimpleChange under changes['course'] and changes['cardIndex'] — so a single invocation can describe multiple inputs at once.

In the ordering, ngOnChanges is called first at construction time — before ngOnInit — once inputs are populated, and then again on every subsequent input change. Logging the same input in all three places makes the timing concrete: the constructor prints undefined, then the very first ngOnChanges already carries the value, and only afterwards does ngOnInit run with that value in place. A crucial subtlety: ngOnChanges fires only when the reference passed to an input changes, not when a property inside an existing input object is mutated. Editing course.description in place does nothing; replacing the array element with a brand-new object does trigger the hook.

  • Implement OnChanges to be notified when any bound input changes.
  • The SimpleChanges argument maps each input name to a SimpleChange with previousValue, currentValue, and firstChange.
  • It runs once during initialization (before ngOnInit) and again on each later input change.
  • Only a change of object reference triggers it — mutating a property of an existing input does not.
  • Use it for callback logic that compares old and new input values.
import { Component, Input, OnChanges, SimpleChanges } from '@angular/core';

@Component({ selector: 'course-card', standalone: true, template: '' })
export class CourseCardComponent implements OnChanges {
  @Input() course: any;

  ngOnChanges(changes: SimpleChanges) {
    const c = changes['course'];
    if (c) {
      console.log('first?', c.firstChange,
                  '| prev:', c.previousValue?.description,
                  '| curr:', c.currentValue?.description);
    }
  }
}

// Parent: this REPLACES the reference, so ngOnChanges fires:
editCourse() {
  const updated = { ...this.courses[0], description: 'ngOnChanges' };
  this.courses = [updated, ...this.courses.slice(1)];
}
Outputfirst? true | prev: undefined | curr: Original text
// after editCourse() replaces the object:
first? false | prev: Original text | curr: ngOnChanges

Setup mirrors branch master of angular-university/angular-course · reimplemented for clarity.

Common pitfallExpecting ngOnChanges to fire after mutating an existing input object (course.description = 'x'). It will not — Angular compares references. Assign a new object/array to trigger it.
AdvancedSignal-based inputs (input()) sidestep ngOnChanges entirely: read the value reactively and derive from it with computed(), or observe transitions with effect(). This gives fine-grained, per-input reactivity without a coarse "something changed" callback, and works regardless of reference-vs-mutation semantics for the derived state.

ngAfterContentChecked — Use Cases and Pitfalls

Objective: Understand when Angular runs ngAfterContentChecked, what "content" means, and why you may modify some data here but not the projected content itself.

Implemented via the AfterContentChecked interface, this hook runs every time Angular finishes checking the component's projected content — the markup a parent places between the component's opening and closing tags and that lands in an <ng-content> slot. Because it runs on every change-detection pass, it fires very frequently, so any code inside it must be lightweight; expensive work (backend calls, heavy computation) placed here will degrade UI performance badly.

This hook is a valid spot for last-second data tweaks before rendering — with one strict limitation. You may modify properties that are not used inside the projected content (for example the card's title or category), but if you modify a value that is consumed by the content that Angular just checked (such as an image URL bound inside the projected element), Angular throws ExpressionChangedAfterItHasBeenCheckedError. The rule protects change detection from modifying the very data it just verified, which could otherwise cause the view to fall out of sync or loop.

  • Implement AfterContentChecked; it runs after Angular checks the projected (ng-content) part of the component.
  • It executes on every change-detection cycle — keep the body cheap and side-effect-light.
  • Good for final data adjustments to properties that are not part of the projected content.
  • Modifying a value used inside the checked content throws ExpressionChangedAfterItHasBeenCheckedError (dev mode).
  • The restriction exists so change detection never mutates the data it just validated.
import { Component, Input, AfterContentChecked } from '@angular/core';

@Component({
  selector: 'course-card',
  standalone: true,
  template: `<ng-content></ng-content> <span>{{ course.category }}</span>`
})
export class CourseCardComponent implements AfterContentChecked {
  @Input() course: any;

  ngAfterContentChecked() {
    // OK: category is NOT inside the projected content
    this.course.category = 'ADVANCED';

    // ERROR if course.iconUrl is bound inside the projected <ng-content>:
    // this.course.iconUrl = '';
  }
}
OutputCard renders with category = ADVANCED, no error.
// Uncommenting the iconUrl line (used in projected content) logs:
ERROR ExpressionChangedAfterItHasBeenCheckedError: Expression has changed after it was checked. Previous value: 'https://…/icon.png'. Current value: ''.

The "content" vs "view" split is easiest to see from the parent's side. Whatever markup a parent nests between the component's tags is projected content; it drops into the card's <ng-content> slot. Everything the card declares in its own template around that slot is the view.

<!-- Parent template: markup BETWEEN the tags is projected content -->
<course-card [course]="course">
  <course-image [url]="course.iconUrl"></course-image>   <!-- CONTENT -->
</course-card>

<!-- course-card's OWN template (the VIEW) wraps that slot: -->
<!-- <ng-content></ng-content> <span>{{ course.category }}</span> -->
What is whatThe projected <course-image> (and the iconUrl it binds) is CONTENT — checked by ngAfterContentChecked. The card's own <span>{{ course.category }}</span> is VIEW — checked later, by ngAfterViewChecked. That is why you may retouch category in the content hook but not iconUrl.

Setup mirrors branch master of angular-university/angular-course · reimplemented for clarity.

Common pitfallDoing backend calls or heavy math in ngAfterContentChecked. Because it runs on every cycle, such work slows the app to a crawl. Reserve it for tiny, cheap adjustments.
AdvancedThe error only surfaces in development mode, where Angular runs a second verification pass to catch data that mutated after being checked. Production builds skip that pass, so the bug hides silently — one more reason to keep this hook free of data mutations that touch the checked content.

ngAfterViewChecked — Use Cases and Pitfalls

Objective: Learn how ngAfterViewChecked differs from the content hook, why it forbids all component-data changes, and when DOM operations like scrolling belong here.

Implemented through AfterViewChecked, this hook runs immediately after Angular finishes checking the component's own template (everything outside the projected content). In the ordering it always follows ngAfterContentChecked, and like it, runs on every change-detection cycle. The distinction is scope: by the time ngAfterViewChecked runs, both the content and the full view have been checked.

Consequently you cannot modify any data the component displays here — neither content nor template values. Attempting to change a bound value either silently fails to reflect in the view or, with default change detection, throws ExpressionChangedAfterItHasBeenCheckedError, because Angular has already finalized the view and does not know which value to keep. The right use for this hook is direct DOM work that requires the freshly rendered elements to exist — scrolling to the bottom of a newly grown list, moving focus to a just-added input, and similar. At this point Angular has committed the new elements to the DOM, so the operation actually takes effect.

  • ngAfterViewChecked runs after the component's own view (template) is checked, always after ngAfterContentChecked.
  • No data modification is allowed — changing displayed values throws ExpressionChangedAfterItHasBeenCheckedError or leaves the view out of sync.
  • Ideal for post-render DOM operations: scrolling, focusing, measuring — actions needing the new DOM in place.
  • Runs on every cycle, so the body must stay lightweight.
import { Component, ElementRef, AfterViewChecked } from '@angular/core';

@Component({
  selector: 'course-list',
  standalone: true,
  template: `<div #list class="scroll"><!-- cards --></div>`
})
export class CourseListComponent implements AfterViewChecked {
  constructor(private host: ElementRef) {}

  ngAfterViewChecked() {
    // DOM operation is safe here: the new elements already exist
    const list = this.host.nativeElement.querySelector('.scroll');
    list.scrollTop = list.scrollHeight; // scroll to the bottom

    // this.title = 'changed'; // would throw ExpressionChangedAfterItHasBeenCheckedError
  }
}
OutputAfter new courses are added, the list scrolls to the bottom.
// If a bound value were changed here, with default change detection:
ERROR ExpressionChangedAfterItHasBeenCheckedError: Expression has changed after it was checked.

Setup mirrors branch master of angular-university/angular-course · reimplemented for clarity.

Common pitfallAssigning to a displayed property inside ngAfterViewChecked to "fix up" the view. It either does nothing or throws the changed-after-checked error. Use it strictly for DOM reads/operations, never data writes.
AdvancedThe error only appears with default change detection because OnPush components skip the dev-mode re-check for that pass — the stale view can then go unnoticed. For DOM work that must run after render, prefer afterNextRender() / afterRender() (modern Angular), which are purpose-built for post-render DOM access and run outside the change-detection verification.

Angular Lifecycle Hooks — Complete Overview and Order

Objective: See every lifecycle hook, the exact order Angular calls them, and a typical use case for each.

With all hooks implemented in one component and logging added, the console reveals the full sequence. The component is first constructed, then Angular fills its inputs and calls ngOnChanges, followed by the one-time ngOnInit. Next comes ngDoCheck (custom change detection), then the content is initialized (ngAfterContentInit, once) and checked (ngAfterContentChecked), then the view is initialized (ngAfterViewInit, once) and checked (ngAfterViewChecked).

Three hooks run exactly once — ngOnInit, ngAfterContentInit, ngAfterViewInit — and are the correct homes for initialization that depends on inputs, on @ContentChild/@ContentChildren queries, and on @ViewChild/@ViewChildren queries respectively. The recurring hooks — ngOnChanges, ngDoCheck, ngAfterContentChecked, ngAfterViewChecked — fire on each change-detection pass. Finally, ngOnDestroy runs once when the component is removed. In everyday work most components need only ngOnInit; the checked hooks are rarely required and must stay cheap.

  • Order: constructor → ngOnChanges → ngOnInit → ngDoCheck → ngAfterContentInit → ngAfterContentChecked → ngAfterViewInit → ngAfterViewChecked → … → ngOnDestroy.
  • Run-once hooks: ngOnInit, ngAfterContentInit, ngAfterViewInit — for input-, content-query-, and view-query-dependent init.
  • Per-cycle hooks: ngOnChanges, ngDoCheck, ngAfterContentChecked, ngAfterViewChecked.
  • ngOnChanges only runs when the component has inputs and one changes by reference.
  • Most components need just ngOnInit; keep the "checked" hooks lightweight.
import {
  Component, OnChanges, OnInit, DoCheck,
  AfterContentInit, AfterContentChecked,
  AfterViewInit, AfterViewChecked, OnDestroy, SimpleChanges
} from '@angular/core';

@Component({ selector: 'course-card', standalone: true, template: '' })
export class CourseCardComponent implements
    OnChanges, OnInit, DoCheck, AfterContentInit, AfterContentChecked,
    AfterViewInit, AfterViewChecked, OnDestroy {
  constructor()                        { console.log('constructor'); }
  ngOnChanges(c: SimpleChanges)        { console.log('ngOnChanges'); }
  ngOnInit()                           { console.log('ngOnInit'); }
  ngDoCheck()                          { console.log('ngDoCheck'); }
  ngAfterContentInit()                 { console.log('ngAfterContentInit'); }
  ngAfterContentChecked()              { console.log('ngAfterContentChecked'); }
  ngAfterViewInit()                    { console.log('ngAfterViewInit'); }
  ngAfterViewChecked()                 { console.log('ngAfterViewChecked'); }
  ngOnDestroy()                        { console.log('ngOnDestroy'); }
}
Outputconstructor
ngOnChanges
ngOnInit
ngDoCheck
ngAfterContentInit
ngAfterContentChecked
ngAfterViewInit
ngAfterViewChecked
// on each later change-detection run:
ngOnChanges (if inputs changed)
ngDoCheck
ngAfterContentChecked
ngAfterViewChecked
// when removed from the DOM:
ngOnDestroy

Setup mirrors branch master of angular-university/angular-course · reimplemented for clarity.

Common pitfallAssuming the "init" hooks run repeatedly. ngOnInit, ngAfterContentInit, and ngAfterViewInit each run exactly once — put recurring logic in the matching "checked" hook instead, not the "init" one.
AdvancedThe ngDoCheck hook lets you implement custom change detection for values Angular's reference-based comparison would miss (e.g. mutated arrays). It runs on every cycle and can be paired with OnPush plus ChangeDetectorRef.markForCheck(), but because it fires so often it must stay extremely cheap — a common source of accidental performance regressions.
Cheat-sheet
// Execution order (per component):
constructor()            // capture injected deps only
ngOnChanges(changes)     // inputs set / changed by REFERENCE — SimpleChanges
ngOnInit()               // once: init using inputs
ngDoCheck()              // every cycle: custom change detection
ngAfterContentInit()     // once: @ContentChild(ren) ready
ngAfterContentChecked()  // every cycle: cheap tweaks (not to projected content)
ngAfterViewInit()        // once: @ViewChild(ren) ready
ngAfterViewChecked()     // every cycle: DOM ops (scroll/focus), no data writes
ngOnDestroy()            // once: cleanup — or use DestroyRef / takeUntilDestroyed()

Quick check

Why should input-dependent initialization go in ngOnInit rather than the constructor?

  1. The constructor cannot contain any code
  2. Bound @Input() values are still undefined when the constructor runs
  3. ngOnInit runs before the constructor
Answerb — Angular constructs the class first and only afterwards populates inputs, so they are available in ngOnInit but not the constructor.

What causes ngOnChanges to fire for an object input?

  1. Mutating a property of the existing object
  2. Assigning a new object reference to the input
  3. Any keypress in the template
Answerb — Angular compares references, so replacing the object triggers the hook; mutating a property of the same object does not.

Which hook is the correct place to scroll to the bottom of a list of newly rendered elements?

  1. ngAfterViewChecked, because the new DOM elements already exist
  2. ngOnChanges, because inputs just changed
  3. The constructor, before rendering
Answera — by ngAfterViewChecked the view is committed to the DOM, so DOM operations like scrolling take effect (but never modify displayed data here).

Angular Modules in Depth

This section explains the classic Angular module system built around the @NgModule decorator: how the root AppModule wires an application together, what each metadata property (declarations, imports, exports, providers, bootstrap) is for, and how to break a growing app into cohesive feature modules with a well-defined public surface. It closes with the modern reality — standalone components have largely replaced hand-written NgModules — and shows how a real app splits into root, feature, and shared modules.

Context — NgModules vs standaloneFrom Angular 14 onward, and by default in projects scaffolded with recent CLI versions, components are standalone: they declare their own imports and no longer need to be listed in an NgModule. NgModules are still fully supported and appear in countless existing codebases, so the concepts below remain essential reading — but for new work, prefer standalone components (covered in the Standalone Components section) over building NgModules by hand.

Introduction to Angular Modules

Objective: Understand what an NgModule is and what each of its five metadata properties — declarations, imports, exports, providers, and bootstrap — controls, using the root AppModule as the reference example.

An Angular module is an organizational unit that groups together closely related building blocks — components, directives, pipes, and services. When a set of these all serve one business concept (say, everything to do with "courses": a course-card component, an image component, a highlight directive, a courses service, and a formatting pipe), it can make sense to bundle them into a single module that could even be published and reused independently of the host application.

Every application already has at least one module: the root module, conventionally defined in app.module.ts and decorated with @NgModule. Its metadata object exposes several key properties:

  • declarations — the components, directives, and pipes that belong to this module. Each such class must be declared in exactly one module.
  • imports — other modules this module depends on. Importing HttpClientModule, for example, makes Angular's HttpClient service available for injection; omit it and you get a dependency-injection error.
  • providers — module-level service providers, an alternative to declaring a provider on a component or via providedIn.
  • bootstrap — the root component(s) Angular instantiates at startup. It is an array because an app can have more than one root component, though most have exactly one.
  • exports — (used by feature modules) the public subset visible to modules that import this one; covered in the next lecture.

The BrowserModule is imported by the root module because it bundles the services Angular needs to render in a browser plus the common structural directives; if the app were server-rendered it would use the server module instead. BrowserModule re-exports CommonModule transitively, which is why the root module gets *ngIf, *ngFor, and friends without importing CommonModule directly. Animations require you to also import BrowserAnimationsModule (from @angular/platform-browser/animations) in the root module.

The bootstrap array ties back to index.html: the custom tag there (e.g. <app-root>) matches the selector of the bootstrapped component. Adding a second root element would mean defining another component and listing it here as well.

import { NgModule } from '@angular/core';
import { BrowserModule } from '@angular/platform-browser';
import { HttpClientModule } from '@angular/common/http';

import { AppComponent } from './app.component';
import { CoursesService } from './courses.service';

@NgModule({
  declarations: [AppComponent],          // components/directives/pipes owned here
  imports: [BrowserModule, HttpClientModule], // dependency modules
  providers: [CoursesService],           // module-level DI providers
  bootstrap: [AppComponent]              // root component(s) started at launch
})
export class AppModule {}
OutputApp boots <app-root> from index.html.
HttpClient and CoursesService are injectable app-wide.
Removing HttpClientModule → NullInjectorError: No provider for HttpClient.
Currency — modern equivalentsAs of Angular 18 (2024), HttpClientModule and the other NgModule-based HTTP APIs are deprecated in favour of provideHttpClient(), supplied in the application's providers; likewise BrowserAnimationsModule is superseded by provideAnimations(). The NgModule wiring above still works and mirrors the course, but new (standalone) apps enable these features through the provider functions rather than by importing the modules.

The providers array is only one of several equivalent ways to register a service, and which route you take depends on how the service is written. A service marked @Injectable({ providedIn: 'root' }) is tree-shakable and needs no explicit registration at all. Strip that metadata down to a plain @Injectable() and the service becomes non-tree-shakable — you must then register it yourself. Listing it in a module's providers array and listing it on a component's own providers array are two equivalent routes to the same result; the module-level array simply widens the scope to every component the module reaches.

// Non-tree-shakable service: no providedIn metadata
@Injectable()
export class CoursesService { /* ... */ }

// Option A — register once at the module level
@NgModule({ providers: [CoursesService] })
export class AppModule {}

// Option B — register on a component (same effect, narrower scope)
@Component({
  selector: 'app-root',
  providers: [CoursesService],
  templateUrl: './app.component.html'
})
export class AppComponent {}
OutputBoth make CoursesService injectable. Option A shares one instance app-wide;
Option B creates a fresh instance scoped to AppComponent and its children.

Mirrors branch master of the Angular Core Deep Dive course · reimplemented for clarity.

Common pitfallDeclaring the same component in two different modules. A component may be declared in exactly one declarations array — sharing it means one module declares and exports it, and the others import that module.
AdvancedPrefer @Injectable({ providedIn: 'root' }) over listing a service in a module's providers array. Tree-shakable providers let the bundler drop services that are never injected, whereas a module-level provider is always retained once the module is loaded.

Angular Feature Modules in Detail

Objective: Build a feature module from scratch with the CLI, move related components, directives, and services into it, and expose a public API through the exports property so the root module can consume it.

A feature module isolates one area of functionality. Generate one with ng generate module courses; the CLI creates courses.module.ts (plus a courses.module.spec.ts test file) inside a courses/ folder. The generated module already imports CommonModule — every feature module needs it for the common directives, since (unlike the root module) it does not import BrowserModule.

Refactoring then means moving each related file into the feature folder and registering it: components and directives go in declarations, services go in providers, and the same items are removed from the root module. Finally the root module lists the feature module in its own imports.

Doing only this produces a template error such as "Can't bind to 'course' since it isn't a known property of 'course-card'." The cause: declaring a component inside a module keeps it private to that module. To make components usable by importers, list them in the module's exports property — the module's public API. Exporting is per-component, and the fix often surfaces one error at a time: adding CourseCardComponent to exports clears its error, but because the card's own template renders CourseImageComponent, the build then fails again on the image component until it too is exported. Every component consumed in an importing module's templates must appear in exports individually. Components meant only as internal implementation detail are deliberately left unexported.

  • ng generate module courses scaffolds a feature module (and its .spec.ts test) pre-wired with CommonModule.
  • Move related components/directives into declarations and services into providers, then delete them from the root module.
  • Declaring a class in a module makes it private; only classes in exports are visible to importing modules.
  • The root module gains the feature by adding it to its imports array.
  • A well-scoped feature module can be published and reused across applications.
  • A SharedModule is a special feature module that declares and exports widely-reused components, directives, and pipes, and re-exports common modules — so any feature module gets them by importing SharedModule alone.
ng generate module courses
OutputCREATE src/app/courses/courses.module.ts (191 bytes)
CREATE src/app/courses/courses.module.spec.ts (240 bytes)
import { NgModule } from '@angular/core';
import { CommonModule } from '@angular/common';

import { CourseCardComponent } from './course-card/course-card.component';
import { CourseImageComponent } from './course-image/course-image.component';
import { HighlightedDirective } from './highlighted.directive';
import { CoursesService } from './courses.service';

@NgModule({
  declarations: [
    CourseCardComponent,
    CourseImageComponent,
    HighlightedDirective
  ],
  imports: [CommonModule],
  providers: [CoursesService],
  exports: [CourseCardComponent, CourseImageComponent] // public API
})
export class CoursesModule {}
OutputWithout exports: "Can't bind to 'course' since it isn't a known property of 'course-card'."
After exporting the components: app compiles and renders correctly.

That feature module is only half of the extraction. The root module is the source the classes were pulled out of, so it must be trimmed to match: the moved components and directive leave its declarations, CoursesService leaves its providers, and CoursesModule joins its imports.

// Root AppModule AFTER the extraction
import { NgModule } from '@angular/core';
import { BrowserModule } from '@angular/platform-browser';

import { AppComponent } from './app.component';
import { CoursesModule } from './courses/courses.module';

@NgModule({
  declarations: [AppComponent],            // course components/directive removed
  imports: [BrowserModule, CoursesModule], // feature module pulled in here
  bootstrap: [AppComponent]                // CoursesService provider removed too
})
export class AppModule {}
OutputAppModule no longer declares the course components or provides CoursesService;
it gains them by importing CoursesModule, whose exports supply the public pieces.
// A SharedModule re-exports commonly needed pieces in one place
import { NgModule } from '@angular/core';
import { CommonModule } from '@angular/common';
import { ReactiveFormsModule } from '@angular/forms';
import { EllipsisDirective } from './ellipsis.directive';

@NgModule({
  declarations: [EllipsisDirective],
  imports: [CommonModule, ReactiveFormsModule],
  exports: [CommonModule, ReactiveFormsModule, EllipsisDirective]
})
export class SharedModule {}
OutputAny feature module that imports SharedModule now has CommonModule,
ReactiveFormsModule, and EllipsisDirective available.

Mirrors branch master of the Angular Core Deep Dive course · reimplemented for clarity.

Common pitfallForgetting to add a feature module's components to exports. They compile fine inside their own module but are invisible to any importing module, producing "not a known property" or "not a known element" errors at bootstrap.
AdvancedNever put services in a SharedModule's providers if that module is imported by lazy-loaded feature modules — each lazy module would receive its own instance. Keep stateful services out of shared modules (use providedIn: 'root') so the whole app shares a single instance.
Cheat-sheet
@NgModule({
  declarations: [...],  // components, directives, pipes owned by this module
  imports:      [...],  // other modules this one depends on
  exports:      [...],  // public API — visible to importers
  providers:    [...],  // module-level DI providers
  bootstrap:    [...]   // root component(s), root module only
})
// CLI:  ng generate module courses
// Root: import BrowserModule; Feature: import CommonModule
// A class must be declared in exactly ONE module.
// Standalone components (Angular 14+) replace most NgModule usage.

Quick check

Which @NgModule property lists the components, directives, and pipes that belong to the module?

  1. imports
  2. declarations
  3. providers
Answerb — declarations registers the module's own components, directives, and pipes; each may be declared in only one module.

A feature module's component renders fine internally but importing modules report "not a known element." What is missing?

  1. The component is not in the module's exports array
  2. The component is not in bootstrap
  3. The module forgot to import HttpClientModule
Answera — declaring a component makes it private; it must appear in exports to be usable by modules that import the feature module.

Why does the root module import BrowserModule while feature modules import CommonModule?

  1. They are interchangeable names for the same module
  2. BrowserModule adds browser-rendering services and re-exports CommonModule; feature modules only need the common directives
  3. Feature modules cannot use *ngIf or *ngFor
Answerb — BrowserModule should be imported once (in the root) and includes browser rendering plus a transitive CommonModule; feature modules import CommonModule directly for the structural directives.

Angular Pipes In Depth

This section closes the pipes topic by moving from consuming Angular's built-in pipes to authoring your own. You build a reusable filterByCategory pipe from scratch — a decorated class implementing PipeTransform — and then study how the pipe engine interacts with change detection. That leads to the crucial distinction between pure pipes (re-evaluated only when their inputs change by identity) and impure pipes (re-evaluated on every change-detection cycle), along with the performance trade-offs each choice carries.

Angular Custom Pipes — Step-by-Step Implementation

Objective: Build a custom pipe that filters an array of courses by category, wiring it end to end from template usage to the @Pipe class and module registration.

A pipe is just a class, exactly like a component or a directive, but annotated with the @Pipe decorator and declared in a module so the template compiler can find it. The goal here is a filterByCategory pipe that receives the full list of courses and returns only the ones matching a chosen category — for example, showing just the beginner courses or just the advanced ones.

Start from the template. Loop over the data with *ngFor, then apply the pipe inside the loop expression using the vertical-bar syntax. The name after the bar (filterByCategory) must match the name declared in the decorator, and the single argument after the colon is the category to keep. Wrapping the piped expression in parentheses makes the data flow easier to read: the array on the left is the pipe's primary input, and the string on the right is the extra argument.

The class itself implements the PipeTransform interface, which requires exactly one method — transform. Its first parameter is the pipe's main input (the courses array), and each subsequent parameter maps to an argument supplied after a colon in the template (here, the target category). Whatever transform returns becomes the value the template actually iterates over. The implementation simply calls Array.filter, comparing each course's category with the requested one.

Finally, register the pipe so the template compiler can resolve its name. In modern Angular a pipe is standalone — note the standalone: true flag in the decorator below — and you make it available simply by adding the pipe class to the imports array of whichever standalone component uses it, exactly as you would import a standalone directive or child component. (From Angular 19 onward, standalone is the default, so the explicit standalone: true is redundant and newer lint rules flag it; you can safely omit the flag on v19+.)

The older, NgModule-based path is the one demonstrated in the video: there the pipe lives inside a feature module (the courses module), so it is added to that module's declarations array to compile, and — critically — to its exports array as well. Skip the export and the pipe stays private to its own module, so the app's root module can never use it. Whichever path you take, once the pipe is registered, reloading shows only the courses of the selected category, and switching the argument from beginner to advanced immediately changes the rendered list.

  • A pipe is a decorated class: @Pipe({ name: '...' }) plus a class implementing PipeTransform.
  • The decorator's name is the identifier used after the | bar in templates.
  • transform(value, ...args) receives the piped value first, then one parameter per colon-separated argument.
  • The method's return value replaces the original expression in the view.
  • Register a standalone pipe by adding it to a component's imports; the legacy NgModule path uses declarations (plus exports to share it beyond its own module).
import { Pipe, PipeTransform } from '@angular/core';

export interface Course {
  id: number;
  title: string;
  category: string;
}

@Pipe({
  name: 'filterByCategory',
  standalone: true   // default from Angular 19+, so the flag can be omitted there
})
export class FilterByCategoryPipe implements PipeTransform {
  transform(courses: Course[], category: string): Course[] {
    if (!courses) {
      return [];
    }
    return courses.filter(course => course.category === category);
  }
}
OutputGiven 5 courses with mixed categories and argument 'BEGINNER',
only the beginner-tagged courses are returned to the template.
<div class="course"
     *ngFor="let course of (courses | filterByCategory:'BEGINNER')">
  <h3>{{ course.title }}</h3>
  <span class="tag">{{ course.category }}</span>
</div>
OutputRendered cards: only titles whose category equals 'BEGINNER'.
// MODERN (standalone): add the pipe to the component that uses it
import { Component } from '@angular/core';
import { FilterByCategoryPipe } from './filter-by-category.pipe';

@Component({
  selector: 'app-courses',
  standalone: true,
  imports: [FilterByCategoryPipe],   // now usable in this component's template
  templateUrl: './courses.component.html'
})
export class CoursesComponent {}

// LEGACY (NgModule): declare it, and EXPORT it so other modules can use it
import { NgModule } from '@angular/core';

@NgModule({
  declarations: [FilterByCategoryPipe],
  exports: [FilterByCategoryPipe]      // drop this and only CoursesModule sees the pipe
})
export class CoursesModule {}
OutputEither registration makes filterByCategory resolvable in the template.
Standalone: import into the component. NgModule: declare + export, then the root module can consume it.

Reference: branch master (file src/app/courses/filter-by-category.pipe.ts) of the Angular Core Deep Dive repo · reimplemented here for clarity.

Common pitfallForgetting to register the pipe. A pipe missing from declarations (or, for standalone usage, from the component's imports) produces a "no pipe found with name" template error at build time.
AdvancedFiltering inside the template with a pipe is convenient but couples the view to list logic and, for large collections, re-runs on each qualifying change. For heavy or frequently changing data, prefer deriving the filtered list in the component (or a signal/computed value) so the work is explicit and cacheable, keeping the template purely declarative.

Angular Impure Pipes In Detail

Objective: Understand how pipe evaluation ties into change detection, and learn when to make a pipe impure (pure: false) versus keeping it pure — with the performance consequences of each.

By default every pipe is pure. A pure pipe re-runs its transform method only when Angular detects that its input has changed by reference identity — that is, when a genuinely new value is passed in. This is a deliberate optimization: computing a pipe can be expensive, so Angular avoids recomputing unless the input actually changed. The built-in pipes (date, currency, uppercase, and so on) are all pure for exactly this reason.

This model breaks down when data is mutated in place. Consider the filterByCategory pipe showing beginner courses. Say the course we edit is RxJS in Practice — it renders as the first beginner card on screen, yet it sits at index 1 in the underlying array (a non-beginner course occupies index 0). That is why the handler targets courses[1] and not courses[0]: on-screen display order and array position are not the same thing, a small gotcha worth watching for. If that "edit" handler reaches into the same array and flips the course's category to ADVANCED, Angular's change detection updates that course's own tag in the DOM — the interpolated {{ course.category }} binding visibly flips to "ADVANCED" — but the pipe does not re-run: the array reference never changed, so from the pipe's perspective its input is identical. This is the crux of the demo: change detection updating an interpolation binding is entirely independent of the pipe re-evaluating. The result is an inconsistent view: an advanced-tagged course still sitting in the beginner list. Adding a console.log inside transform confirms it fires once on load and never again after the mutation.

Setting pure: false makes the pipe impure. An impure pipe re-runs transform on every change-detection cycle, regardless of whether the input reference changed. Now the in-place mutation is picked up: the pipe re-filters, the advanced course drops out of the beginner list, and the console shows transform being called repeatedly. The cost is real, though — because the method executes on every cycle (which can be very frequent), any non-trivial work inside it can degrade the whole UI's responsiveness.

The guidance is therefore to keep pipes pure whenever possible, treat inputs as immutable (replace the array rather than mutate it), and only reach for an impure pipe when in-place mutation genuinely cannot be avoided — keeping its transform logic as lightweight as possible.

  • Pipes are pure by default; a pure pipe re-runs only when its input changes by reference identity.
  • Mutating the input array in place does not create a new reference, so a pure pipe will not re-run.
  • pure: false makes a pipe impure — transform executes on every change-detection cycle.
  • Impure pipes catch in-place mutations but repeat frequently, so heavy logic hurts performance.
  • Prefer immutable updates (new array) with a pure pipe; use impure pipes only as a last resort.
// PURE (default): re-runs only when the 'courses' reference changes
// (standalone: true is the default from Angular 19+ and can be omitted there)
@Pipe({ name: 'filterByCategory', standalone: true })
export class FilterByCategoryPipe implements PipeTransform {
  transform(courses: Course[], category: string): Course[] {
    console.log('pure transform called');
    return (courses ?? []).filter(c => c.category === category);
  }
}

// IMPURE: re-runs on EVERY change-detection cycle
@Pipe({ name: 'filterByCategory', standalone: true, pure: false })
export class FilterByCategoryImpurePipe implements PipeTransform {
  transform(courses: Course[], category: string): Course[] {
    console.log('impure transform called');
    return (courses ?? []).filter(c => c.category === category);
  }
}
OutputMutating courses[1].category in place, then clicking around:
pure → "pure transform called" logged once (stale list)
impure → "impure transform called" logged on every cycle (correct list)
// The hard-coded data. courses[1] ('RxJS in Practice') is the FIRST
// beginner card on screen but the SECOND entry here (index 0 is ADVANCED).
courses: Course[] = [
  { id: 1, title: 'Angular Testing',  category: 'ADVANCED' },
  { id: 2, title: 'RxJS in Practice', category: 'BEGINNER' },
  { id: 3, title: 'NgRx Deep Dive',   category: 'BEGINNER' }
];

// Handler that MUTATES the array in place (the problematic case)
onEditCourse() {
  this.courses[1].category = 'ADVANCED';   // same array reference
}

// Immutable alternative that lets a PURE pipe re-run correctly
onEditCourseImmutable() {
  this.courses = this.courses.map((c, i) =>
    i === 1 ? { ...c, category: 'ADVANCED' } : c
  );                                        // brand-new array reference
}
OutputonEditCourse: pure pipe stays stale (RxJS keeps its slot in the BEGINNER list).
onEditCourseImmutable: new reference triggers the pure pipe to re-filter.

Reference: branch master (file src/app/courses/filter-by-category.pipe.ts) of the Angular Core Deep Dive repo · reimplemented here for clarity.

Common pitfallReaching for pure: false to "fix" a stale list. It works, but it silently runs transform on every cycle. The cleaner fix is usually to update data immutably so a pure pipe sees a new reference.
AdvancedAngular's AsyncPipe is deliberately impure — it must react to values emitted by a Promise or Observable that arrive independently of reference changes to its input, and it manages subscription/unsubscription internally. This is the canonical example of a justified impure pipe: the impurity is essential to its purpose, and its per-cycle work is trivial.
Cheat-sheet
@Pipe({ name: 'myPipe' })            // pure by default
@Pipe({ name: 'myPipe', pure: false })  // impure: runs every CD cycle

class MyPipe implements PipeTransform {
  transform(value, ...args) { return /* result */; }
}

// Template
{{ value | myPipe:arg1:arg2 }}
*ngFor="let x of (list | filterByCategory:'BEGINNER')"

// Registration
imports: [MyPipe]        // standalone component (modern; default v17+)
declarations: [MyPipe]   // NgModule (legacy) — also add to exports to share
// v19+: standalone is the default, so 'standalone: true' can be omitted

// Rule of thumb: keep pipes pure + update data immutably;
// use impure only when in-place mutation is unavoidable.

Quick check

Which interface must a custom pipe class implement, and what single method does it require?

  1. OnInit with ngOnInit
  2. PipeTransform with transform
  3. Pipe with convert
Answerb — PipeTransform requires a single transform(value, ...args) method whose return value replaces the piped expression.

A pure pipe filters an array. You mutate one element of that same array in place. What happens?

  1. The pipe re-runs and the list updates
  2. The pipe does not re-run, so the list can become stale
  3. Angular throws a runtime error
Answerb — the array reference is unchanged, so a pure pipe sees no new input and does not re-execute, leaving the view inconsistent.

What is the main drawback of setting pure: false on a pipe?

  1. It can only be used once per template
  2. Its transform runs on every change-detection cycle, so heavy logic hurts performance
  3. It disables Angular's HTML escaping
Answerb — an impure pipe re-evaluates on every cycle; expensive work inside transform can noticeably slow the UI.

Angular Internationalization (i18n) In Depth

This section covers Angular's built-in internationalization support end to end: marking template text as translatable with the i18n attribute, understanding how translation IDs are generated (and when to override them), handling grammar edge cases with ICU plural and select expressions, and finally the full CLI workflow — extracting messages, feeding them to a translator, and building or serving a per-locale version of the app.

Introduction to Angular Internationalization (i18n)

Objective: Mark template text as translatable with the i18n attribute and extract every marked string into a message file with the Angular CLI.

Angular ships with first-class internationalization support. The abbreviation i18n stands for "internationalization" — the 18 is the count of letters between the leading i and the trailing n. To make a piece of static text in a template translatable, you attach the i18n attribute to the element that contains it. Despite the name, this is not a directive; it is a special build-time marker that tooling such as the Angular CLI scans for when it collects translatable strings.

Once text is marked, you run the extraction command. It walks the whole project, gathers every i18n-tagged string, and writes them into a single message file — by default src/messages.xlf. The XLF (XLIFF) format is one of several supported output formats; it is XML, so associating the .xlf extension with XML syntax highlighting in your editor makes it far easier to read. Inside the file, each translatable string becomes a <trans-unit> whose <source> tag holds the original text and whose attributes carry a unique identifier.

That message file is what you hand to a translator, who loads it into their own tooling, provides translations, and returns a translated file that you later feed back into your build. Because the whole pipeline keys off the unique ID, it is important to understand what that ID is derived from — a topic the next lecture explores.

  • The i18n attribute marks an element's text as translatable; it is a build marker, not a directive.
  • ng extract-i18n scans the project and produces a message file (default src/messages.xlf).
  • XLF is XML-based; each string maps to a <trans-unit> with a <source> and a unique id.
  • The message file is the exchange format between developers and translators.
<button i18n>Edit Course</button>

<h4 i18n>Welcome to the Angular Core Deep Dive course</h4>
OutputBoth strings are collected on the next extraction run.
# stop the dev server first, then extract translatable strings
ng extract-i18n
OutputExtraction complete.
Wrote: src/messages.xlf
<trans-unit id="3d…" datatype="html">
  <source>Edit Course</source>
</trans-unit>
OutputOne <trans-unit> per marked string, each with its own id.

Mirrors branch master of angular-university/angular-course · reimplemented for clarity.

Common pitfallExpecting i18n to behave like a directive. It carries no runtime behaviour on its own — if you never run the extraction command, marking text does nothing.
AdvancedThe extractor supports several formats via --format (xlf, xlf2, xmb, json, arb). XLIFF 1.2 is the default and the most widely supported by commercial translation tools, so it is usually the right choice unless a specific vendor requires otherwise.

Understanding Angular i18n Unique Identifiers

Objective: Learn how translation IDs are generated, add meaning and description metadata for the translator, and override the ID manually with @@customId when necessary.

Angular derives each translation ID automatically. Experimentation shows what feeds into it: moving a marked element elsewhere in the template leaves its ID unchanged, but altering the text produces a brand-new ID and therefore a fresh translation request. This is exactly what you want — edit a string during development and the next extraction transparently flags it for re-translation, so you never track that manually.

Sometimes a word's correct translation depends on context. You can supply extra guidance to the translator through the i18n value itself. The value can hold a meaning and a description separated by a pipe: i18n="meaning|description". Both are written into the trans-unit as <note> tags the translator can read. Crucially, the meaning also participates in ID generation — changing it yields a new ID — whereas the description does not, so tweaking a description alone never triggers re-translation.

In rare cases you may want to pin an ID yourself. Append a double-at prefix to the attribute value: i18n="…@@myCustomId". The trans-unit then uses your literal ID instead of the generated hash. This is discouraged for everyday use — hand-writing IDs across an entire app is a lot of work and error-prone. Prefer the automatic IDs; reach for meaning/description only occasionally, and for custom IDs almost never.

  • ID generation is driven by the string content and its meaning — moving text does not change the ID, editing text does.
  • Syntax: i18n="meaning|description@@customId"; every part is optional.
  • Meaning affects the ID; description does not.
  • Meaning and description surface to the translator as <note> entries.
  • Custom IDs (@@id) override the generated one but should be used sparingly.
<!-- meaning only -->
<h4 i18n="welcome message">Welcome to the course</h4>

<!-- meaning | description -->
<h4 i18n="welcome message|Greeting shown at the start of the course">
  Welcome to the course
</h4>

<!-- meaning | description @@ custom id -->
<h4 i18n="welcome message|Greeting shown at the start of the course@@welcomeMessage">
  Welcome to the course
</h4>
OutputEditing the text or the meaning changes the id; editing only the description does not.
<trans-unit id="welcomeMessage" datatype="html">
  <source>Welcome to the course</source>
  <note priority="1" from="meaning">welcome message</note>
  <note priority="1" from="description">Greeting shown at the start of the course</note>
</trans-unit>
OutputThe custom id replaces the generated hash; notes carry context to the translator.

Mirrors branch master of angular-university/angular-course · reimplemented for clarity.

Common pitfallAssuming a description change forces a new translation. Only the content and the meaning affect the ID — a description-only edit leaves the existing (possibly stale) translation in place.
AdvancedCustom IDs become fragile once two different strings are given the same ID — Angular then treats them as one translation. Stable custom IDs are useful for very long-lived strings whose wording is refined over time without wanting a re-translation, but the automatic scheme is safer for most projects.

Angular i18n Pluralization Support

Objective: Render grammatically correct, count-dependent sentences using an ICU plural expression that the extractor understands as a single translatable unit.

Natural language changes wording based on quantity: "no courses available", "one course available", "10 courses available". Rather than stitching this together with conditionals, Angular supports the ICU MessageFormat plural syntax directly inside a marked block. You wrap the whole expression in curly braces, name the numeric value, add the plural keyword, then list categories — each a match value followed by its own braced text.

The common explicit categories are =0, =1, and other (the catch-all for any remaining count). Inside a category's text you can interpolate the number again with the placeholder {{ ... }} so the actual value prints. Angular's runtime evaluates the expression and swaps in the matching branch as the bound value changes — set the courses array to empty, one item, or many, and the sentence updates accordingly.

When extracted, the entire plural expression becomes one trans-unit, with the interpolated count represented as a named placeholder (see the extracted unit below). The translator therefore receives all the alternatives together and full context, and can adapt the categories to the grammar of the target language.

  • ICU plural syntax: { value, plural, =0 {…} =1 {…} other {…} }.
  • Categories are matched top to bottom; other handles everything not matched explicitly.
  • Interpolate the number inside a branch with {{ total }}.
  • The whole expression extracts as a single trans-unit, so all forms travel together.
  • Mark the block with i18n just like ordinary text.
export class AppComponent {
  courses = [/* ... */];

  get totalCourses(): number {
    return this.courses.length;
  }
}
OutputtotalCourses reflects the current length of the courses array.
<span i18n>
  { totalCourses, plural,
    =0 { No courses are available }
    =1 { One course is available }
    other { A total of {{ totalCourses }} courses are available }
  }
</span>
Output0 → "No courses are available"
1 → "One course is available"
10 → "A total of 10 courses are available"
<trans-unit id="7a…" datatype="html">
  <source>{VAR_PLURAL, plural, =0 {No courses are available} =1 {One course is available} other {A total of {INTERPOLATION} courses are available}}</source>
</trans-unit>
OutputThe whole plural block extracts as one unit; the interpolated count becomes the {INTERPOLATION} placeholder, so every form travels to the translator together.

Mirrors branch master of angular-university/angular-course · reimplemented for clarity.

Common pitfallConfusing =1 (an exact numeric match) with the CLDR keyword one (a language-specific plural category). They are not interchangeable — =0/=1 match exact values, while one/few/many/other are locale grammar categories.
AdvancedICU categories follow Unicode CLDR plural rules. Some languages (e.g. Polish, Arabic, Russian) distinguish few and many beyond English's two-form system; providing an other branch guarantees a fallback, and translators can add the extra CLDR categories their language requires.

Angular i18n Alternative Expressions Support

Objective: Replace an ngSwitch-style set of mutually exclusive labels with an ICU select expression, giving the translator grouped, related alternatives.

Sometimes you pick one of several strings based on a non-numeric value — for example rendering "beginner", "intermediate", or "advanced" from a course's category. You could tag each label separately with i18n, but they would then appear to the translator as unrelated fragments. The ICU select expression instead groups them as clearly alternative choices, giving far better context.

The syntax mirrors plural but uses the select keyword and string match keys instead of numbers: name the value, write select, then list each possible key with its braced text. This is a translation-friendly alternative to ngSwitch and, unlike a plain switch, it is picked up by the i18n extractor as a single unit.

A practical gotcha: the value driving the select must actually match one of the keys. If a course record is missing its category (or its value matches no key) and there is no other branch, Angular renders nothing — a silent empty label, not a thrown error, which is easy to miss in production. ICU select supports an other {…} catch-all just like plural; add one as a fallback and/or keep the property populated. When extracted, the select — with all its alternatives — becomes one trans-unit the translator sees as a coherent group.

  • ICU select syntax: { value, select, keyA {…} keyB {…} }.
  • Best for a fixed set of string-keyed, mutually exclusive labels.
  • A translation-aware substitute for ngSwitch — it extracts as one unit; ngSwitch does not.
  • The driving value must match a key, so keep the source property populated.
  • All alternatives are grouped for the translator, improving context.
<!-- before: unrelated fragments via ngSwitch -->
<div [ngSwitch]="course.category">
  <div *ngSwitchCase="'BEGINNER'" i18n>Beginner</div>
  <div *ngSwitchCase="'INTERMEDIATE'" i18n>Intermediate</div>
  <div *ngSwitchCase="'ADVANCED'" i18n>Advanced</div>
</div>

<!-- after: grouped alternatives via ICU select -->
<div class="category" i18n>
  { course.category, select,
    BEGINNER { Beginner }
    INTERMEDIATE { Intermediate }
    ADVANCED { Advanced }
  }
</div>
Outputcategory "INTERMEDIATE" → "Intermediate"
missing/unmatched category → renders empty (silent, no error — add an other {…} branch)
<trans-unit id="5f…" datatype="html">
  <source>{VAR_SELECT, select, BEGINNER {Beginner} INTERMEDIATE {Intermediate} ADVANCED {Advanced}}</source>
</trans-unit>
OutputAll three alternatives extract together as one grouped unit.
<trans-unit id="5f…" datatype="html">
  <source>{VAR_SELECT, select, BEGINNER {Beginner} INTERMEDIATE {Intermediate} ADVANCED {Advanced}}</source>
  <target>{VAR_SELECT, select, BEGINNER {D&eacute;butant} INTERMEDIATE {Interm&eacute;diaire} ADVANCED {Avanc&eacute;}}</target>
</trans-unit>
OutputThe translator returns the same VAR_SELECT structure with each alternative translated in place — the group stays intact.

Mirrors branch master of angular-university/angular-course · reimplemented for clarity.

Common pitfallForgetting the comma after select (or after the value). A malformed ICU expression yields a build error and a blank screen — the message usually points at the missing separator.
Advancedselect and plural can be nested inside one another for compound grammar (e.g. count varying by gender). Keep nesting shallow — deeply nested ICU expressions are hard for translators to work with and easy to mis-brace.

Running a Translated Angular Application using the Angular CLI

Objective: Complete the workflow — record the source locale, place the returned translation file, configure per-locale build and serve targets in angular.json, then serve and build the French version.

Angular treats the source language as English (en) unless you say otherwise. In modern Angular the source locale is declared once in angular.json under a top-level i18n block via sourceLocale — if your app were authored in French you would set "sourceLocale": "fr". Ours stays English, so we leave the default and only add a target locale.

You send messages.xlf to a translator (by email, Dropbox, or an online TMS). They translate in their own tooling — never editing the XML by hand — and return a file such as messages.fr.xlf in which each <source> now has a matching <target> holding the translation. A good translation reorders words and uses natural target-language phrasing rather than translating word-for-word. The handoff can even be fully automated: with a connected translation-management system the translator works in a web app and your build pulls the finished file back through the platform's API, so nothing is emailed or dropped in a shared folder by hand. Place the returned file under source control, conventionally in src/locale/.

Next, register the translation in angular.json. Modern Angular — using @angular/localize, the same package behind ng extract-i18n — centralises this: list each locale under the project's top-level i18n.locales map, pointing it at the returned file, e.g. "fr": { "translation": "src/locale/messages.fr.xlf" }. You then choose which locales a build emits with the localize option: "localize": true builds every configured locale, while a per-locale configuration uses "localize": ["fr"]. Keep i18nMissingTranslation: "error" in the build options so an incomplete translation fails the production build — you do not want to ship half-translated pages; relax it to "warning" or "ignore" only while iterating. (The older per-configuration i18nFile/i18nFormat/i18nLocale keys from pre-v9 courses no longer exist — see the note at the end of this lecture.)

Because the locale is compiled into the bundle at build time, each language is its own set of output files. Preview one in development with ng serve --configuration=fr. For production, ng build --localize emits every configured locale in a single pass (into dist/<project>/<locale>/), or ng build --configuration=fr builds just that one. Note that --prod is not used here: it was removed in Angular 12+, where production is a configuration you select with --configuration=production. Either way a multilingual app ships one bundle per language, and a server routes each user to the right one by preference or detection.

  • Declare the source locale once in angular.json's top-level i18n block via sourceLocale (defaults to en).
  • The returned file adds a <target> per <source>; store it (e.g. src/locale/messages.fr.xlf) under version control.
  • Register each translation under i18n.locales, e.g. "fr": { "translation": "src/locale/messages.fr.xlf" }.
  • Pick which locales to emit with localize: true builds all, ["fr"] builds one.
  • ng serve --configuration=fr previews; ng build --localize (or --configuration=production) ships — --prod was removed in Angular 12+.
