  • i18nMissingTranslation: "error" in the build options guards production; relax to "ignore" only while developing.
# collect translatable strings into the locale folder
ng extract-i18n --output-path src/locale
OutputWrote: src/locale/messages.xlf (source locale read from angular.json)
<trans-unit id="a1…" datatype="html">
  <source>Welcome to the Angular Core Deep Dive course</source>
  <target>Bienvenue au cours Angular Core Deep Dive</target>
</trans-unit>
OutputEvery source string now carries its French target.
"i18n": {
  "sourceLocale": "en",
  "locales": {
    "fr": { "translation": "src/locale/messages.fr.xlf" }
  }
},
"architect": {
  "build": {
    "options": {
      "localize": true,
      "i18nMissingTranslation": "error"
    },
    "configurations": {
      "production": { "...": "..." },
      "fr": { "localize": ["fr"] }
    }
  }
}
OutputThe top-level i18n block maps fr to its translation file; localize selects which locales a build emits.
"serve": {
  "configurations": {
    "production": { "buildTarget": "angular-course:build:production" },
    "fr": { "buildTarget": "angular-course:build:fr" }
  }
}
OutputThe fr serve target reuses the fr build config (buildTarget replaced the older browserTarget in Angular 16+).
# preview the French app in development
ng serve --configuration=fr

# build one locale, or every configured locale at once
ng build --configuration=fr        # just French
ng build --localize                # all locales -> dist/<project>/<locale>
# note: --prod was removed in Angular 12+ — use --configuration=production
OutputDev: app served at localhost:4200 in French.
Prod: one bundle per locale under dist/<project>/.

Mirrors branch master of angular-university/angular-course · reimplemented for clarity.

Common pitfallLeaving i18nMissingTranslation at "ignore" in a production config. Untranslated strings then silently fall back to the source language and ship to users — set it to "error" for real builds.
Advanced — the legacy (pre-v9) flowBefore Angular 9 the pipeline looked different, and the two eras do not mix. Extraction was ng xi18n (not ng extract-i18n), and each translation was wired through per-configuration i18nFile, i18nFormat, and i18nLocale keys under a build configuration — there was no top-level i18n block and no localize option. If you meet those keys in an older codebase you are looking at the legacy approach; on any Angular that accepts ng extract-i18n and @angular/localize, use the modern i18n/localize configuration shown above.
Cheat-sheet
ng extract-i18n                        # collect i18n strings -> src/messages.xlf
ng extract-i18n --format json          # choose an alternate output format
# source locale & translations live in angular.json:
#   "i18n": { "sourceLocale": "en", "locales": { "fr": "src/locale/messages.fr.xlf" } }
ng serve   --configuration=fr          # preview one locale in development
ng build   --localize                  # build every configured locale at once
ng build   --configuration=fr          # build one locale (--prod removed in v12+; prod = --configuration=production)
# markup:  <el i18n>text</el>   |   i18n="meaning|description@@customId"
# plural:  { n, plural, =0 {…} =1 {…} other {…} }
# select:  { key, select, A {…} B {…} }

Quick check

Which two inputs determine the automatically generated translation ID for a marked string?

  1. The string content and its description
  2. The string content and its meaning
  3. The element's position in the template and its description
Answerb — the content plus the meaning drive the ID; the description does not affect it, and moving the element does not change it.

When should you reach for an ICU select expression instead of plural?

  1. When the alternatives depend on a numeric count
  2. When choosing among a fixed set of string-keyed labels
  3. When the text never varies
Answerb — select matches on string keys (e.g. a category), whereas plural matches on a numeric quantity.

What is the effect of i18nMissingTranslation: "error" in a build configuration?

  1. It skips untranslated strings silently
  2. It fails the build when any translation is missing
  3. It auto-translates missing strings
Answerb — the build fails on a missing translation, preventing a partially translated app from shipping to production.

Angular Elements In Depth

Angular Elements let you repackage an ordinary Angular component as a browser-native Custom Element (Web Component). Once registered, the tag can be dropped into any page — a CMS template, a React or Vue app, or a plain static HTML file — and the browser, not the Angular framework, is responsible for instantiating it. This section walks through adding the @angular/elements package, converting a component into a custom element, embedding that element in raw HTML, and wiring its inputs and outputs so external code can communicate with it.

Why Angular Elements — Framework-Agnostic Components

Objective: Understand the problem Angular Elements solves and how the browser's Custom Elements API makes an Angular component usable outside an Angular application.

In a normal Angular app, Angular owns the entire page: it bootstraps the root component and decides when every other component is created. That works fine when Angular is in charge, but it fails the moment you want to ship a piece of Angular UI into an environment Angular does not control. Consider a content management system where editors paste in dynamic snippets, or a React/Vue/plain-HTML page where a different framework (or no framework) has taken over the document. In those cases the host is not Angular, so nothing instantiates your component.

The way around this is to hand instantiation to the browser itself rather than to a specific framework. Browsers ship a standard Custom Elements API (customElements.define) that teaches the browser how to construct a bespoke tag whenever it appears in the DOM. Angular Elements is the bridge between that standard browser capability and an Angular component: it wraps the component so that any time the corresponding tag is added to a page, the browser triggers Angular to build the component behind the scenes — regardless of what framework, if any, owns the surrounding page.

  • A normal Angular app assumes Angular controls the whole page and creates all components.
  • To embed Angular UI in non-Angular hosts (CMS, React, Vue, static HTML), the browser must handle instantiation instead.
  • The Custom Elements API (customElements.define) is a browser standard, not Angular-specific.
  • Angular Elements bridges a component to that API, turning it into a self-instantiating tag.
  • Typical use cases: reusable widgets, third-party embeds, and CMS-authored dynamic content.
# Add the Angular Elements capability to an existing project.
# Pass the project name if your workspace has more than one app.
ng add @angular/elements angular-course
OutputInstalling packages for tooling via npm.
Installed packages for tooling via npm.
UPDATE package.json (added @angular/elements)
✔ Package successfully added.

Setup mirrors branch master of angular-university/angular-course · reimplemented for clarity.

Common pitfallAssuming you must run ng add @angular/elements on every branch or checkout. It is a one-time change to package.json; once the dependency is installed, the elements API is available across the project.
AdvancedCustom Elements are one half of the Web Components spec (alongside Shadow DOM and HTML templates). Angular Elements uses only the Custom Elements registry by default; Shadow DOM encapsulation is opt-in through the component's encapsulation: ViewEncapsulation.ShadowDom setting.

Defining a Custom Element with createCustomElement()

Objective: Build a simple component, convert it into a browser custom element with createCustomElement(), and register it via customElements.define().

Start with an ordinary component. Scaffold it first with ng generate component course-title — that command takes a moment and creates a new course-title/ folder with the usual TS, template, CSS, and spec files — then fill it in. Here a CourseTitleComponent renders a single title input inside a styled div, so it is visually obvious when the element mounts, and adds one @Output() named titleClicked that emits when the div is clicked. Nothing about the component itself is special — it is a normal component with an @Input() and an @Output(). (The finished snippet below inlines the template and styles for compactness; the scaffold's separate .html/.css files work identically.)

The conversion happens once, typically in the root component's ngOnInit. Call createCustomElement() from @angular/elements, passing the component class as the first argument and a configuration object as the second. That config must include an injector so Angular can resolve any dependencies the component needs through its constructor; inject Angular's Injector into the root component and forward it. The call returns a custom-element constructor, which you then register with the browser by calling customElements.define() with the desired tag name.

Older tutorials add one more step here, and it is worth knowing why it is now obsolete. Before the Ivy compiler (Angular 8 and earlier), because this component is never referenced declaratively in any template, the compiler would not generate a factory for it, so programmatically instantiated components had to be listed in the module's entryComponents array — skipping it produced the runtime error "No component factory found for CourseTitleComponent." Ivy changed that: since Angular 9 entryComponents has been a no-op, and Angular 13 removed it entirely. In the modern Angular this guide targets (v17–v22) you write no entryComponents at all, and that factory error no longer occurs — so treat the NgModule step below as a legacy artifact, not a present-day requirement.

  • createCustomElement(Component, { injector }) returns a custom-element constructor.
  • The injector lets the browser-created element resolve the component's dependencies.
  • customElements.define('tag-name', ctor) registers the tag with the browser.
  • Register once (e.g. in the root component's ngOnInit), not per instance.
  • Legacy only: pre-Ivy NgModules needed entryComponents; since Angular 9 it is a no-op and Angular 13 removed it, so modern code needs nothing here.
# Scaffold the component before writing any code.
ng generate component course-title
# CREATE src/app/course-title/course-title.component.ts
# CREATE src/app/course-title/course-title.component.html
# CREATE src/app/course-title/course-title.component.css
# CREATE src/app/course-title/course-title.component.spec.ts
OutputA new course-title/ folder appears with the component's TS, template, CSS, and spec files ready to fill in.
// course-title/course-title.component.ts
import { Component, Input, Output, EventEmitter } from '@angular/core';

@Component({
  selector: 'course-title',
  template: `<div class="course-title" (click)="titleClicked.emit(title)">{{ title }}</div>`,
  styles: [`.course-title { text-decoration: underline; cursor: pointer; }`]
})
export class CourseTitleComponent {
  @Input() title = '';
  @Output() titleClicked = new EventEmitter<string>();
}
OutputA component that renders its title input as underlined text and emits titleClicked with the title string whenever it is clicked.
// app.component.ts — register the element once
import { Component, Injector, OnInit } from '@angular/core';
import { createCustomElement } from '@angular/elements';
import { CourseTitleComponent } from './course-title/course-title.component';

@Component({ selector: 'app-root', templateUrl: './app.component.html' })
export class AppComponent implements OnInit {
  constructor(private injector: Injector) {}

  ngOnInit() {
    const htmlElement = createCustomElement(CourseTitleComponent, {
      injector: this.injector
    });
    customElements.define('course-title', htmlElement);
  }
}
OutputAfter reload the console is clean — the <course-title> tag is now registered with the browser.
// app.module.ts — LEGACY (pre-Ivy, Angular 8 and earlier). Do NOT copy into modern code.
@NgModule({
  declarations: [AppComponent, CourseTitleComponent],
  imports: [BrowserModule],
  entryComponents: [CourseTitleComponent], // no-op since Angular 9 (Ivy); removed in v13
  bootstrap: [AppComponent]
})
export class AppModule {}
OutputHistorical only. Pre-Ivy this avoided "No component factory found for CourseTitleComponent." Under Ivy (Angular 9+) entryComponents does nothing and was removed in v13 — modern Angular needs no entry here.

Setup mirrors branch master of angular-university/angular-course · reimplemented for clarity.

Common pitfallForgetting the injector in the config object. Without it the element cannot resolve constructor dependencies, and components that inject services will fail at construction time.
AdvancedNeither modern NgModules nor standalone components need entryComponents — Ivy made it a no-op in Angular 9 and v13 removed it outright, so the "No component factory found" error is gone for both. With standalone bootstrapping you still call createCustomElement() and customElements.define() the same way, typically after bootstrapApplication() resolves, using the returned ApplicationRef.injector.

Using the Element and Wiring Inputs & Outputs

Objective: Drop the registered custom element into raw HTML, bind data through attributes/properties, and react to outputs as DOM CustomEvents — and reuse the same element inside Angular templates.

Once course-title is registered, it behaves like any built-in tag. To watch this happen, open DevTools, right-click the page's top-of-page demo div and choose Edit as HTML, then hand-type a <course-title> tag and fill in its title attribute. Copy that line twice more so there are three, then click outside the inline editor: the instant the markup commits, the browser builds a fresh Angular component for each tag. Nothing on the page pushed them in — registering the tag with the browser is exactly what triggers instantiation. The cause-and-effect chain is worth stating plainly: the tag lands in the DOM → the browser looks it up in its Custom Elements registry → it finds the constructor Angular registered → Angular constructs and renders the component. Component @Input() properties are exposed as element attributes (and properties), so <course-title title="Angular Core Deep Dive"></course-title> feeds the input, and each of the three copies renders its own instance. This only works because the tag was registered; an unconverted component like course-card has no custom-element link, so pasting its tag into raw HTML does nothing — with no registry entry, the browser has no way to know how to build it.

Communication flows the other way too. Component @Output() emitters surface as native DOM CustomEvents, so external non-Angular code listens with the standard addEventListener. Setting an input is just assigning a property or attribute on the element; reading an output is just handling an event. That symmetry is what makes the element portable across frameworks.

The same element still works inside Angular templates. You can replace a plain title tag in another component with <course-title [title]="course.title"> and bind normally. To allow an unknown custom tag inside an Angular template, add CUSTOM_ELEMENTS_SCHEMA to that feature module; without it Angular's compiler rejects the unrecognized element.

  • Registered elements can be inserted into any HTML — even manually via DevTools — and self-instantiate.
  • @Input() properties map to element attributes/properties (e.g. title="…").
  • @Output() emitters map to DOM CustomEvents, consumed with addEventListener.
  • A non-element component (e.g. course-card) will not render if pasted into raw HTML.
  • To use the tag inside an Angular template, add CUSTOM_ELEMENTS_SCHEMA to the module.
<!-- Plain HTML page — no Angular in control here -->
<div class="demo">
  <course-title title="Angular Core Deep Dive"></course-title>
  <course-title title="RxJs In Practice"></course-title>
  <course-title title="NgRx Fundamentals"></course-title>
</div>
OutputThree underlined titles render, each an independently instantiated Angular component.
// External (non-Angular) code driving the element
const el = document.querySelector('course-title') as any;

// Set an @Input() — as a property or an attribute
el.title = 'Updated Title';
el.setAttribute('title', 'Updated Title');

// Read an @Output() — CourseTitleComponent emits titleClicked when its
// div is clicked, and that emitter surfaces here as a DOM CustomEvent
el.addEventListener('titleClicked', (e: CustomEvent) => {
  console.log('Clicked title:', e.detail); // e.detail is the emitted title
});
OutputAssigning title re-renders the widget; clicking it fires the component's titleClicked output, which arrives here as a CustomEvent and logs "Clicked title: …" from e.detail.
// courses.module.ts — allow the custom tag inside Angular templates
import { NgModule, CUSTOM_ELEMENTS_SCHEMA } from '@angular/core';

@NgModule({
  declarations: [CourseCardComponent],
  schemas: [CUSTOM_ELEMENTS_SCHEMA] // permits unknown <course-title> tag
})
export class CoursesModule {}
Output<course-title [title]="course.title"> now compiles and renders inside CourseCard.

Setup mirrors branch master of angular-university/angular-course · reimplemented for clarity.

Common pitfallExpecting a normal component tag to render when pasted into static HTML. Only components explicitly converted with createCustomElement() and registered via customElements.define() work as raw tags; everything else needs Angular to place it.
AdvancedCustom element tag names must contain a hyphen, per the spec — course-title is valid, coursetitle is not. Also note attribute values are always strings; complex inputs (objects, arrays) must be assigned through the element's JS property, not an HTML attribute.
Cheat-sheet
// 1. Install once
//    ng add @angular/elements

// 2. Convert + register (root ngOnInit or after bootstrap)
const ctor = createCustomElement(CourseTitleComponent, { injector: this.injector });
customElements.define('course-title', ctor);   // tag needs a hyphen

// 3. Legacy pre-Ivy NgModule only: entryComponents: [CourseTitleComponent]
//    (no-op since v9, removed in v13 — skip it in modern Angular)
//    Template host module: schemas: [CUSTOM_ELEMENTS_SCHEMA]

// 4. Use anywhere
//    <course-title title="..."></course-title>   // @Input  -> attribute
//    el.addEventListener('evt', e => e.detail);   // @Output -> CustomEvent

Quick check

Which two calls together turn an Angular component into a usable browser custom element?

  1. bootstrapApplication() and ngOnInit()
  2. createCustomElement() and customElements.define()
  3. @Component() and @Input()
Answerb — createCustomElement() produces the element constructor and customElements.define() registers the tag with the browser.

How are a component's @Output() emitters exposed to non-Angular host code?

  1. As HTML attributes
  2. As native DOM CustomEvents consumed with addEventListener
  3. As global functions on window
Answerb — outputs surface as DOM CustomEvents, so any framework or plain JS can listen with addEventListener and read event.detail.

Why does the config object passed to createCustomElement() include an injector?

  1. To set the element's tag name
  2. To let the browser-created element resolve the component's dependencies
  3. To register the event listeners
Answerb — the injector gives Angular access to any services the component needs when the browser instantiates it programmatically.

Angular Standalone Components In Depth

This section explains what standalone components are and why Angular introduced them as an alternative to NgModules, then walks through a hands-on, three-step migration of an existing module-based application into a fully module-free one. You will mark every component, directive, and pipe as standalone and import its own dependencies (step 1), delete the now-empty feature and application modules (step 2), and finally rewrite the application's bootstrap with bootstrapApplication and provider functions such as provideRouter and provideHttpClient (step 3).

Angular Standalone Components — New Section

Objective: Understand what standalone components are, the problem they solve, and how they replace the NgModule mental model.

Historically an Angular application was assembled from an application module plus a set of feature modules — a courses module, a shared module, and so on. That design enables features like lazy loading and keeps large codebases organized by grouping related declarations together. The downside is conceptual weight: newcomers must learn modules with their declarations, imports, and exports before they can build anything, and to many developers the module has always felt like internal framework plumbing they would rather not manage.

Standalone components are Angular's answer. A standalone building block sets standalone: true and declares its own template dependencies through an imports array on the component itself, so it lives entirely outside any NgModule and can be used directly. With this model an entire application can be written without a single module. The Angular team recommends migrating existing apps and authoring all new apps this way.

The CLI ships an automated migration (the standalone schematic) that runs in three ordered passes. It gets you most of the way, but for a larger app it typically will not remove NgModules completely — some manual refactoring remains. For that reason the rest of this section performs the migration by hand, step by step, so you see exactly what the schematic cannot finish on its own.

  • Standalone components set standalone: true and use their own imports: [] — no NgModule required.
  • They exist outside any module and can be consumed directly; an app can have zero modules.
  • Modules still enable organization and lazy loading, but add conceptual overhead many developers find unnecessary.
  • The CLI standalone schematic automates most of a migration but rarely finishes it for large apps.
  • New applications should be authored as standalone from the start.
# The automated migration is run as three ordered steps
ng generate @angular/core:standalone
# ? Choose the type of migration:
#   1) Convert all components, directives and pipes to standalone
#   2) Remove unnecessary NgModule classes
#   3) Bootstrap the application using standalone APIs
OutputRun option 1 first, then option 2, then option 3.
Each pass rewrites part of the codebase toward standalone.

Reworked from the standalone-migration lectures on branch ng17-recordings · reimplemented for clarity.

Common pitfallAssuming the schematic fully removes every NgModule. On non-trivial apps it leaves modules that still hold services or providers; those must be cleaned up manually before the module can be deleted.
AdvancedFrom Angular 19 onward standalone: true is the implicit default, so the flag can be omitted and NgModule-declared components must instead opt out with standalone: false. The examples here keep the explicit flag because they target the v17 recordings.

Migrating To Standalone — Step 1: Importing Dependencies

Objective: Mark every component, directive, and pipe as standalone, remove them from module declarations, and give each one its own imports array.

The first step is to flag all declarables — components, directives, and pipes — with standalone: true. The moment a class becomes standalone, Angular reports a compile error if it is still listed in an NgModule's declarations, because a standalone class cannot be declared in a module. So each converted class must be pulled out of the module's declarations and exports, which quickly leaves the feature module nearly empty.

A standalone component no longer inherits template dependencies from a surrounding module, so anything it uses in its template must be imported explicitly on the component. If a course-card template uses *ngIf, the component must import NgIf (or the broader CommonModule); otherwise the build fails. In practice the IDE's Angular Language Service auto-completes these imports for you, so the extra explicitness costs almost nothing. Simple components that use no directives — a plain image component, for example — need no imports at all.

The root AppComponent gets the same treatment, and here the order of operations matters. Make the component standalone first, then let the auto-import add its dependencies. If you trigger auto-import while the class is still module-declared, the tooling assumes a module is in charge and routes the new imports back into the application module's imports — the opposite of what a migration wants. With standalone: true in place up front, the child components it renders (course card, course image) land directly in the component's own imports array instead.

  • Add standalone: true to every component, directive, and pipe.
  • A standalone class must be removed from any module's declarations and exports — keeping it there is a compile error.
  • Each standalone component imports its own template dependencies via imports: [...].
  • Import individual directives (NgIf, NgFor) or the whole CommonModule — both work.
  • IDE auto-import makes the added explicitness effortless.
// BEFORE — declared in a module, no imports on the component
@Component({
  selector: 'course-card',
  templateUrl: './course-card.component.html'
})
export class CourseCardComponent { /* ... */ }

// AFTER — standalone, imports its own template dependencies
import { NgIf } from '@angular/common';

@Component({
  selector: 'course-card',
  standalone: true,
  imports: [NgIf, CourseImageComponent],
  templateUrl: './course-card.component.html'
})
export class CourseCardComponent { /* ... */ }
OutputTemplate using <div *ngIf="course"> now compiles.
Without importing NgIf: "NG8103: *ngIf is not a known directive".
// The feature module is now almost empty after removing declarations
@NgModule({
  declarations: [],   // every class moved to standalone
  imports: [CommonModule],
  exports: []
})
export class CoursesModule {}
OutputError if a standalone class is left here:
NG6008: Component CourseCardComponent is standalone,
and cannot be declared in an NgModule.

standalone: true is not a component-only flag — every declarable takes it. The recordings convert a custom pipe and custom directives exactly the same way, not just components: a @Pipe or @Directive that used to sit in a module's declarations becomes standalone, and any component that uses it then imports it directly.

// A custom pipe made standalone
@Pipe({ name: 'summary', standalone: true })
export class SummaryPipe implements PipeTransform {
  transform(value: string, max = 100): string {
    return value.length > max ? value.slice(0, max) + '…' : value;
  }
}

// A custom directive made standalone
@Directive({ selector: '[highlight]', standalone: true })
export class HighlightDirective {
  @HostBinding('style.backgroundColor') bg = 'yellow';
}

// A component consuming both — it imports them itself
@Component({
  selector: 'course-card',
  standalone: true,
  imports: [NgIf, SummaryPipe, HighlightDirective],
  templateUrl: './course-card.component.html'
})
export class CourseCardComponent {}
OutputPipe, directive, and component all leave the module's declarations.
Each is imported where it is used — nothing is inherited from a module.

Reworked from the standalone-migration lectures on branch ng17-recordings · reimplemented for clarity.

Common pitfallForgetting to import a directive a template actually uses. A standalone component gets nothing from its old module, so an un-imported *ngFor or custom pipe fails at build time even though the code looks unchanged.
AdvancedPrefer importing granular symbols (NgIf, NgFor, AsyncPipe) over the entire CommonModule — it makes each component's real dependencies explicit and gives the bundler more room to tree-shake unused directives.

Migrating To Standalone — Step 2: Removing Unused Modules

Objective: Empty out and delete every feature module now that its declarations are gone, relocating any remaining providers.

With step 1 complete, the feature modules have empty declarations and exports, but they may still hold something that blocks deletion — most commonly a provided service. A courses module that registers CoursesService can be freed by moving that registration onto the service itself with @Injectable({ providedIn: 'root' }). The service is now tree-shakable and available application-wide without the module.

What remains in the module is usually just an imports: [CommonModule] entry, which is also unnecessary: every standalone component in the folder already imports the specific Angular directives it needs. Once the module is genuinely empty, search for its usages — WebStorm's "Find Usages" (Alt+F7) is handy — and you will typically find it referenced only in the application module and a spec file. Remove those references, optimize imports, and delete the module file. Repeat this for each feature module until no NgModule remains in the codebase.

  • Move service registration off the module using @Injectable({ providedIn: 'root' }).
  • Drop the leftover CommonModule import — standalone components already import what they use.
  • Use "Find Usages" to confirm the empty module is referenced nowhere meaningful before deleting.
  • Delete stale references in the application module and any spec files, then remove the module file.
  • The CLI cannot always perform this step — manual cleanup is expected on real apps.
// BEFORE — service provided by the module
@NgModule({
  imports: [CommonModule],
  providers: [CoursesService]
})
export class CoursesModule {}

// AFTER — service provides itself; module can be deleted
@Injectable({ providedIn: 'root' })
export class CoursesService {
  constructor(private http: HttpClient) {}
}
OutputCoursesService injectable app-wide, tree-shakable.
CoursesModule is now empty and safe to delete.

Reworked from the standalone-migration lectures on branch ng17-recordings · reimplemented for clarity.

Common pitfallDeleting a module while it still provides a service. Remove the provider first (usually via providedIn: 'root'), or the service becomes unavailable and injection fails at runtime.
AdvancedStandalone imports arrays accept whole NgModules as well as individual components — useful for third-party libraries not yet migrated. Prefer importing single components when you can; import the full module only when a library exposes no standalone entry points.

Migrating To Standalone — Step 3: Refactor Application Bootstrapping

Objective: Replace module-based bootstrapping with bootstrapApplication and provider functions, then delete the application module entirely.

The final step rewrites main.ts. Instead of platformBrowserDynamic().bootstrapModule(AppModule), call bootstrapApplication and pass the root component directly — here AppComponent. Everything the old AppModule configured now moves into a providers array on the second argument.

Everything the old AppModule configured now becomes an entry in that providers array. It is worth translating them one at a time, in the order the recording works through them:

1. The browser module. The recording first adds it with importProvidersFrom(BrowserModule) — the general escape hatch for a module that has no dedicated provider function. In practice you can leave this line out entirely: bootstrapApplication already provides everything BrowserModule would, so wrapping it is redundant. That automatic provisioning is precisely why the finished main.ts below never mentions the browser module — it does not vanish by accident, the platform supplies it for you. 2. Animations. These do have a dedicated function, so provideAnimations() replaces the old BrowserAnimationsModule import. 3. The HTTP client. provideHttpClient() configures it, and passing withInterceptors([...]) registers interceptors through dependency injection rather than the old multi-provider HTTP_INTERCEPTORS token.

The finished example below adds one provider this particular lecture did not set up: provideRouter(routes). The recording configured only the browser module, animations, and the HTTP client; routing is shown here because it belongs to the recommended full provider set for any routed standalone app and follows the identical provideX() shape — treat it as "what you would normally include," distinct from "what this migration demonstrated." Once main.ts no longer references AppModule, "Find Usages" confirms the module is unused and it can be deleted — leaving an application with no NgModules at all that runs exactly as before.

  • bootstrapApplication(AppComponent, { providers: [...] }) replaces bootstrapModule(AppModule).
  • Use provider functions: provideRouter(routes), provideHttpClient(), provideAnimations().
  • Wrap legacy modules lacking a provider function with importProvidersFrom(...).
  • Register interceptors via provideHttpClient(withInterceptors([...])).
  • bootstrapApplication auto-provides BrowserModule — no importProvidersFrom(BrowserModule) needed.
  • provideRouter(routes) is the recommended addition for a routed app, beyond the browser/animations/HTTP set this lecture showed.
  • After bootstrap is converted, delete the now-unused application module.
// BEFORE — main.ts bootstrapping via NgModule
import { platformBrowserDynamic } from '@angular/platform-browser-dynamic';
import { AppModule } from './app/app.module';

platformBrowserDynamic()
  .bootstrapModule(AppModule)
  .catch(err => console.error(err));
OutputApp boots through AppModule (declarations, imports, providers).
// AFTER — main.ts bootstrapping standalone
import { bootstrapApplication } from '@angular/platform-browser';
import { provideAnimations } from '@angular/platform-browser/animations';
import { provideHttpClient, withInterceptors } from '@angular/common/http';
import { provideRouter } from '@angular/router';
import { AppComponent } from './app/app.component';
import { routes } from './app/app.routes';
import { authInterceptor } from './app/auth.interceptor';

bootstrapApplication(AppComponent, {
  providers: [
    provideRouter(routes),
    provideAnimations(),
    provideHttpClient(withInterceptors([authInterceptor]))
  ]
}).catch(err => console.error(err));
Output✔ Compiled successfully — localhost:4200 renders as before.
Zero NgModules remain in the codebase.

When a dependency ships only as a classic NgModule — a third-party library with no provideX() function, or a legacy module you have not migrated yet — importProvidersFrom(...) is the bridge. It flattens that module's providers into the application injector so you can adopt standalone bootstrapping without waiting for every library to catch up.

// Bridging a non-standalone module into standalone bootstrap
import { importProvidersFrom } from '@angular/core';
import { NgxChartsModule } from '@swimlane/ngx-charts';

bootstrapApplication(AppComponent, {
  providers: [
    provideHttpClient(),
    // Third-party module that exposes no provideX() function yet:
    importProvidersFrom(NgxChartsModule)
  ]
}).catch(err => console.error(err));
OutputNgxChartsModule's providers are flattened into the app injector.
Reach for this only when a library offers no standalone / provider API.

Reworked from the standalone-migration lectures on branch ng17-recordings · reimplemented for clarity.

Common pitfallMigrating bootstrap but forgetting a provider the old module supplied (routing, HTTP, animations). Missing provideHttpClient(), for instance, produces a "No provider for HttpClient" error at runtime, not at build time.
AdvancedFor readability, group related providers into a feature helper that returns makeEnvironmentProviders([...]), then add its result directly to the providers array — providers: [provideMyFeature()]. makeEnvironmentProviders() returns a single opaque EnvironmentProviders object (branded and non-iterable, so it cannot be spread with ...); it drops in exactly like the built-in provideX() functions and keeps main.ts declarative as the provider list grows.
Cheat-sheet
// 1. Make a component standalone with its own imports
@Component({ standalone: true, imports: [NgIf, NgFor], /* ... */ })

// 2. Provide services without a module
@Injectable({ providedIn: 'root' })

// 3. Bootstrap without NgModules
bootstrapApplication(AppComponent, {
  providers: [
    provideRouter(routes),
    provideHttpClient(withInterceptors([authInterceptor])),
    provideAnimations(),
    importProvidersFrom(SomeLegacyModule)   // bridge non-standalone libs
  ]
});

Quick check

How does a standalone component gain access to a directive like *ngIf used in its template?

  1. It inherits it automatically from the root module
  2. It lists NgIf (or CommonModule) in its own imports array
  3. It adds NgIf to a module's declarations
Answerb — standalone components receive nothing from a surrounding module, so each template dependency must appear in the component's own imports.

After making its components standalone, a feature module still provides a service. What is the cleanest way to let the module be deleted?

  1. Move the registration to @Injectable({ providedIn: 'root' }) on the service
  2. Leave the service in the module's declarations
  3. Re-import the module into every component
Answera — providing the service in root removes its dependency on the module, which can then be emptied and removed.

Which API replaces bootstrapModule(AppModule) when moving to standalone bootstrapping?

  1. provideRouter(AppComponent)
  2. platformBrowserDynamic(AppComponent)
  3. bootstrapApplication(AppComponent, { providers: [...] })
Answerc — bootstrapApplication takes the root component directly and receives configuration through provider functions.

Angular 17 @defer — Partial Template Loading

This section covers @defer, Angular's control-flow block for splitting a slice of a template into its own lazily-loaded JavaScript bundle. It is a performance optimization: functionality that is heavy and rarely used stays out of the main bundle until it is actually needed. You learn the companion blocks (@placeholder, @loading, @error), the two independent levels of control (when code is prefetched versus when it is displayed), and the full family of built-in triggers — idle, immediate, timer, viewport, interaction, hover — before finishing with fully custom when triggers.

@defer Section Introduction

Objective: Understand what problem @defer solves and the two separate levels of control it gives you over loading and displaying a template fragment.

Every extra kilobyte added to the main application bundle makes the whole app feel slower to start. If a page contains functionality that is both heavyweight (it pulls in a large third-party library such as a PDF generator or a charting engine) and rarely exercised (the user must click a button or scroll far down to reach it), shipping that code in the initial bundle is wasteful. The ideal is to download only the JavaScript a given session truly needs.

@defer lets you carve such a fragment out of the main bundle into a separate chunk that loads on demand. Two classic examples are below-the-fold content — anything that only appears after the user scrolls past the initially visible viewport, a phrase borrowed from print, where a newspaper folded into a magazine shows only its upper half until you unfold it, so whatever sat past the crease was literally "below the fold" — and dialogs whose heavy dependencies should load in the background as the user approaches, then display only on an explicit action.

The crucial mental model: @defer exposes two independent controls. One decides when (and whether) the bundle is prefetched from the server; the other decides when the already-loaded content is applied to the page and shown. If a prefetch condition never fires, the code is never downloaded at all — the best possible outcome. Keep these two concerns strictly separate.

  • @defer is an opt-in optimization — reach for it when a page is slow, or apply it preventively from the start.
  • Best candidates: heavy dependencies that are rarely used, or content below the fold.
  • Control 1: when/if the bundle is prefetched (downloaded) in the background.
  • Control 2: when the loaded content is displayed to the user.
  • These two levels are configured separately and must not be conflated.

Concepts follow branch ng17-recordings of the Angular University course · reimplemented for clarity.

Common pitfallTreating @defer as a required feature. It is purely a performance tool — a small, lightweight component gains nothing from being deferred and only adds indirection.
AdvancedBecause prefetch and display are separable, you can express nuanced flows such as "download the chart library when the browser goes idle, but only render the chart once the user scrolls it into view" — spending idle time on the network without committing pixels until they are needed.

How Does @defer Work?

Objective: Wrap a fragment in @defer and confirm, in the Network tab, that Angular extracts it into a separate JavaScript chunk with a default trigger.

Wrapping template markup in an @defer block tells the Angular build to pull everything inside it — and its dependencies — into a distinct bundle rather than the main one. In the Network tab (development mode) you can see the main bundle no longer carries that code; a new chunk appears instead. Exact file names and counts vary between Angular versions, so do not rely on them — the point is that the deferred code lives apart from main.js.

With no trigger specified, @defer uses its default behavior: the chunk is fetched and the content displayed once the browser reaches its idle state — after it has finished loading the page's other resources. In a trivial app this happens almost immediately after bootstrap, so both events appear instantaneous; in a real app with a heavy dependency, that dependency now ships in the deferred chunk instead of bloating the main bundle.

  • The compiler moves @defer contents (plus dependencies) into their own lazy chunk.
  • The main bundle shrinks by exactly that amount.
  • With no trigger, both prefetch and display default to the browser idle event.
  • Verify the split in DevTools → Network, filtered to JS.
<!-- courses-card-list is now split into its own chunk -->
@defer {
  <courses-card-list [courses]="courses"></courses-card-list>
}
Output (Network tab)main.js ...... (no longer contains the card list)
chunk-XXXX.js ... (deferred card-list code)
Content appears once the browser goes idle.

Mirrors branch ng17-recordings · reimplemented for clarity.

Common pitfallExpecting to observe the deferred load "kick in" in a tiny demo app. The idle state is reached instantly, so the delay is invisible. Use a timer trigger to make the two phases observable.
AdvancedThe extraction is static — Angular must be able to see the dependencies at build time. Components, directives, and pipes used only inside a @defer block become part of its lazy chunk, which is why @defer works most cleanly with standalone components.

@defer with @placeholder

Objective: Render substitute content in place of the deferred block until it is displayed, and hold it on screen with the minimum parameter.

The @defer content only appears once its trigger fires; until then the region would be empty. The @placeholder block fills that gap. It is part of the @defer syntax and can only accompany a defer block. Whatever you put inside stays visible in the block's spot until Angular decides to swap in the real content.

In a fast-loading demo the placeholder may only flash for a fraction of a second. To make it observable — and, more importantly, to prevent jarring flicker — pass minimum, the least amount of time the placeholder must stay on screen before it can be replaced. With @placeholder (minimum 3s) the substitute content is guaranteed to show for at least three seconds.

Critically, a placeholder is not a loading spinner. It is meant to be a lightweight visual stand-in for the real feature — for example a plain "Print as PDF" button with no behavior, later replaced by the fully-wired button that lazily pulls in the PDF library on click. Also note that placeholder content is eagerly loaded with the rest of the template, so any dependency you reference inside it lands back in the main bundle. Keep placeholders free of heavy dependencies.

  • @placeholder shows stand-in content until the deferred block is displayed.
  • @placeholder (minimum 3s) forces a minimum visible duration, avoiding flicker.
  • It is a lightweight visual substitute, not a loading indicator.
  • Placeholder contents load eagerly — do not put heavy dependencies inside.
@defer {
  <courses-card-list [courses]="courses"></courses-card-list>
} @placeholder (minimum 3s) {
  <p>This is placeholder content</p>
}
Output"This is placeholder content" is shown for ≥ 3 seconds,
then replaced by the course card list once the trigger fires.

Mirrors branch ng17-recordings · reimplemented for clarity.

Common pitfallWiring a click handler that needs a heavy library inside @placeholder. Because the placeholder is eager, that dependency is pulled back into the main bundle — defeating the purpose of deferring.
AdvancedThe placeholder is also what several triggers reference by default — the viewport, interaction, and hover triggers observe the placeholder element unless you point them at a specific template reference instead.

@defer with @loading (minimum & after)

Objective: Show a genuine loading indicator while the chunk downloads, and tune it with minimum and after to avoid confusing flicker.

Whereas @placeholder stands in before the trigger fires, @loading is shown while the deferred bundle is actually downloading in the background. Put a spinner or "Loading…" message inside. On a fast connection with a light chunk this may never appear, so — as with the placeholder — a minimum parameter guarantees a visible duration.

The after parameter solves a UX problem: flashing a spinner for a few milliseconds looks broken. @loading (after 1s; minimum 3s) reads as "only start showing the loading indicator if the download has taken longer than one second, and once shown keep it up for at least three seconds." If the chunk loads faster than one second, the loading block is skipped entirely and Angular goes straight from placeholder to loaded content.

  • @loading is visible only while the deferred chunk is downloading.
  • minimum keeps the indicator up long enough for the user to perceive it.
  • after suppresses the indicator entirely for fast loads.
  • Placeholder vs loading: substitute-before-trigger vs indicator-during-download.
@defer {
  <courses-card-list [courses]="courses"></courses-card-list>
} @loading (after 1s; minimum 3s) {
  <h2>Loading...</h2>
} @placeholder {
  <p>This is placeholder content</p>
}
OutputFast download (< 1s): placeholder → content, no spinner.
Slow download (> 1s): placeholder → "Loading..." (≥ 3s) → content.

Mirrors branch ng17-recordings · reimplemented for clarity.

Common pitfallOmitting after and showing a loading indicator for a chunk that loads in milliseconds. The flash makes the app feel buggy — always pair a spinner with a sensible after threshold.
AdvancedThe two parameters are not symmetric across the blocks. @loading is the only block that accepts both — @loading (after 1s; minimum 3s) — where after is measured from the moment the download starts, while minimum is measured from when the @loading block is actually shown (after the after delay elapses), guaranteeing the indicator stays visible long enough to be perceived once it appears. @placeholder accepts minimum alone (measured from when the placeholder was first shown); there is no after on a placeholder, so @placeholder (after 1s) is a compile error. Choosing these durations well is a deliberate UX decision, not a default.

@defer Triggers — idle & immediate

Objective: Learn that the no-argument default equals the idle trigger, that prefetch and display each have their own trigger, and meet the immediate trigger.

Everything so far used the implicit default. That default is exactly equivalent to writing @defer (on idle): the block acts when the browser reports it is idle — a state detected via the requestIdleCallback API, which is supported across all modern browsers, reached once the page has finished loading all its resources.

There are two distinct triggers to configure. The display trigger (written directly on @defer (on …)) decides when to show the block; the prefetch trigger (written as @defer (prefetch on …)) decides when to download it. Specifying only one applies it to display, and prefetch defaults to the same event. Setting prefetch on idle alongside the default display therefore changes nothing.

The immediate trigger fires the moment the surrounding component first renders — no waiting for idle. Used for display, the block appears right away; used for prefetch, the chunk starts downloading immediately. It behaves very similarly to idle in a simple app, differing only in the exact moment each fires.

  • The no-argument default is identical to on idle.
  • idle fires via requestIdleCallback once page resources have loaded.
  • Display trigger: @defer (on …). Prefetch trigger: @defer (prefetch on …).
  • immediate fires as soon as the component renders.
<!-- these two are equivalent -->
@defer { ... }
@defer (on idle; prefetch on idle) { ... }

<!-- fire the moment the component renders -->
@defer (on immediate) {
  <courses-card-list [courses]="courses"></courses-card-list>
}
Outputon idle: content appears shortly after the page settles.
on immediate: content appears as soon as the component renders.

Mirrors branch ng17-recordings · reimplemented for clarity.

Common pitfallAssuming on idle and on immediate are interchangeable. In a busy app idle may wait noticeably for the browser to settle, while immediate never waits — the choice affects when the network request is issued.
AdvancedBecause immediate bypasses idle detection, it is a poor default for genuinely heavy chunks — it competes with initial page resources for bandwidth. Reserve it for content you are confident the user needs right away.

@defer timer Trigger — Understanding prefetch Triggers

Objective: Use the timer trigger to make prefetch and display observable, proving the two triggers run on fully independent clocks.

The timer trigger fires after a fixed delay, expressed in milliseconds or seconds — for example on timer(15s). Because the delay is long enough to watch, it is the ideal tool for seeing prefetch and display as separate events. Configure them independently: prefetch on timer(10s) downloads the chunk ten seconds after the component initializes, while on timer(15s) displays it at the fifteen-second mark.

Both timers start from the same instant — the component's first render. In the Network tab you observe the deferred chunk arrive at ten seconds while the placeholder is still on screen, then the content swaps in five seconds later at fifteen. This concretely demonstrates the two levels of control operating on their own schedules.

  • timer(Ns) fires after a fixed delay from the component's initial render.
  • Give prefetch and display different timers to separate download from display.
  • Both clocks start together, at first render.
  • Any built-in trigger can drive either prefetch or display.
@defer (on timer(15s); prefetch on timer(10s)) {
  <courses-card-list [courses]="courses"></courses-card-list>
} @placeholder {
  <p>This is placeholder content</p>
}
Output (timeline)t=0s component renders, placeholder visible
t=10s deferred chunk downloaded (Network tab)
t=15s content displayed, placeholder removed

Mirrors branch ng17-recordings · reimplemented for clarity.

Common pitfallSetting the display timer earlier than the prefetch timer. If the block is asked to display before its chunk has downloaded, the display trigger forces an immediate prefetch — the ordering you intended is silently overridden.
AdvancedThe display trigger always takes precedence: if display fires before prefetch, Angular downloads the chunk on the spot. This priority rule holds for every trigger type, including custom ones — design timings so display never outruns a still-pending prefetch unless you intend the fallback.

@defer @error Block

Objective: Add an @error block that renders when the deferred chunk fails to download, and observe it by simulating a network failure.

@error is the last of the companion blocks. Its content is displayed only if fetching the deferred bundle from the backend fails. To see it, configure a visible schedule (e.g. prefetch on timer(10s); on timer(15s)), then use DevTools to switch the browser to offline mode before the prefetch fires, forcing the request to fail.

The failed request surfaces in the Network tab, but the @error block itself only appears when the display trigger fires — five seconds later in this example. That is because Angular decides at display time whether to show the loaded content or the error block. Like @placeholder and @loading, the @error block's dependencies are loaded eagerly into the main bundle, so keep it to simple HTML and CSS — a friendly error message, nothing heavy.

  • @error renders when the deferred bundle fails to load.
  • Simulate it by toggling offline mode in DevTools before prefetch.
  • The error block appears when the display trigger fires, not at the moment of failure.
  • Its dependencies load eagerly — keep it lightweight.
@defer (on timer(15s); prefetch on timer(10s)) {
  <courses-card-list [courses]="courses"></courses-card-list>
} @error {
  <h2>An error occurred while loading this section</h2>
} @placeholder {
  <p>This is placeholder content</p>
}
Outputt=10s chunk request fails (offline) — shown in Network tab
t=15s "An error occurred while loading this section" is displayed

Mirrors branch ng17-recordings · reimplemented for clarity.

Common pitfallPutting the feature's real (heavy) dependencies inside @error to "reuse" them. They will be eagerly bundled into main.js — the error block must stay a minimal static message.
AdvancedAll four companion blocks — content (implicit), @placeholder, @loading, @error — share the rule that only the primary @defer content is lazily chunked. This is why the design guidance is: heavy logic lives inside @defer, everything else stays trivially light.

@defer — The viewport Built-in Trigger

Objective: Display a deferred block only when it (or a referenced element) scrolls into the visible viewport — the canonical below-the-fold optimization.

The viewport trigger fires when the observed element enters the browser's visible area. This is the textbook case for below-the-fold content: prefetch the chunk early (e.g. prefetch on idle, so it is ready in the background almost immediately), but only render it with on viewport once the user actually scrolls to it. In many sessions the user never scrolls that far, so display never happens — though here the chunk is still prefetched.

By default viewport observes the @placeholder element. To key off a different element, give that element a template reference variable and pass it into the trigger: on viewport(title). Then the block displays only when the referenced #title element scrolls into view, rather than the placeholder. Because these element-observing triggers need something concrete to watch, Angular enforces a rule: viewport, interaction, and hover must be paired with either a @placeholder block or an explicit template-reference target — supply neither and the template fails to compile.

  • on viewport fires when the observed element enters the visible viewport.
  • Ideal for below-the-fold content — combine with an early prefetch.
  • Defaults to observing the placeholder; pass a template ref to observe a specific element.
  • Like all triggers, it can drive prefetch or display.
<!-- tall content above (a hero/banner, or a spacer div) keeps #title below the initial fold -->
<div class="scroll-space"></div>

<h1 #title>Available Courses</h1>

@defer (on viewport(title); prefetch on idle) {
  <courses-card-list [courses]="courses"></courses-card-list>
} @placeholder {
  <p>This is placeholder content</p>
}
OutputChunk prefetched shortly after load (idle).
Content stays hidden until #title scrolls into view,
then the card list is displayed.

Mirrors branch ng17-recordings · reimplemented for clarity.

Common pitfallExpecting on viewport alone to also defer the download in a way that never risks a blank flash. Without a separate prefetch, the download only starts once the element nears view, so slow chunks may briefly show the placeholder before content — pair viewport display with an earlier prefetch.
Advancedviewport is backed by IntersectionObserver, which reports the observed element's initial intersection state. Referencing an element that is initially rendered but off-screen (like the placeholder) works naturally; but referencing an element that is already inside the viewport on page load fires the trigger immediately and displays the content right away — so the observed element must start below the fold for the deferral to be meaningful. Referencing an element inside the deferred content itself would be a chicken-and-egg error, since it does not exist until the block loads.

@defer — The Built-in interaction Trigger

Objective: Trigger prefetch and/or display when the user interacts (click or keydown) with a specific element.

The interaction trigger fires when the user interacts with the observed element — a click on any element, or a keydown when the element is an input the user is typing into. By default it observes the @placeholder, so clicking the placeholder reveals the deferred block.

To target other elements, give them template reference variables and pass them into separate triggers — and note the key point: those references can point at any ordinary element on the page, not just the placeholder. In the example below the input and button sit outside the @defer block entirely; they are plain page elements, and the triggers observe them by reference. A common pattern wires prefetch to interaction with the input and display to interaction with the button: prefetch on interaction(prefetchRef); on interaction(displayRef). Tabbing into the input and pressing a key issues the first keydown, which prefetches the chunk (visible in the Network tab) while the placeholder stays put; only clicking the display button then swaps in the deferred content. Omit the reference — writing plain on interaction — and the trigger falls back to observing the placeholder instead. This lets you download in the background on early engagement and reveal on an explicit action — perfect for a dialog whose heavy library should load as the user approaches but render only on demand.

  • on interaction fires on click, or on keydown for inputs.
  • Defaults to observing the placeholder.
  • Point it at any page element — inside or outside the block — via a template reference variable.
  • Split prefetch and display across different elements for advanced flows.
<!-- ordinary page elements, sitting OUTSIDE the @defer block -->
<input #prefetchRef placeholder="Type to prefetch">
<button #displayRef>Show courses</button>

@defer (on interaction(displayRef);
        prefetch on interaction(prefetchRef)) {
  <courses-card-list [courses]="courses"></courses-card-list>
} @placeholder {
  <p>This is placeholder content</p>
}
OutputFirst keydown in the input → chunk prefetched (Network tab).
Placeholder still visible.
Click "Show courses" → deferred content displayed.

Mirrors branch ng17-recordings · reimplemented for clarity.

Common pitfallAssuming everything outside @defer is free. The trigger targets themselves are cheap plain inputs and buttons, but the @placeholder, @loading, and @error blocks all load eagerly with the main bundle — attach a heavy dependency to any of them and it lands back in main.js.
AdvancedCombining interaction for prefetch with a later display trigger gives near-instant perceived performance: by the time the user clicks to display, the chunk is already cached locally, so the swap is immediate with no spinner.

@defer — The Built-in hover Trigger

Objective: Prefetch a deferred chunk when the pointer hovers a region, then display it only on a later interaction — a highly anticipatory pattern.

The hover trigger fires when the pointer enters the observed element. Its classic use is anticipatory prefetch: as the mouse approaches a button, download the chunk in the background, then reveal the content only when the user actually clicks. This pairs naturally with the interaction trigger from the previous lecture — hover to prefetch, click to display.

By default hover observes the placeholder. To watch a specific region, give it a template reference and pass it in: prefetch on hover(triggerDisplay). Now hovering that bordered region (and nothing else) starts the download, and the deferred block appears only once a separately-referenced button is clicked. In the Network tab you can confirm the chunk loads exactly when the cursor enters the target area, not before.

  • on hover fires when the pointer enters the observed element.
  • Defaults to the placeholder; retarget with a template reference variable.
  • Canonical combo: prefetch on hover + display on interaction.
  • Downloads heavy code just before the user is likely to need it.
<div #triggerDisplay class="hover-zone">Hover here to prefetch</div>
<button #displayBtn>Show courses</button>

@defer (on interaction(displayBtn);
        prefetch on hover(triggerDisplay)) {
  <courses-card-list [courses]="courses"></courses-card-list>
} @placeholder {
  <p>This is placeholder content</p>
}
OutputPointer enters .hover-zone → chunk downloaded (Network tab).
Nothing displayed yet.
Click "Show courses" → deferred content displayed.

Mirrors branch ng17-recordings · reimplemented for clarity.

Common pitfallAccidentally hovering the target during testing and concluding the trigger "always fires." Reload with the pointer away from the zone and clear the Network log before each run to observe the true behavior.
AdvancedHover-to-prefetch is a bet: you spend bandwidth on the guess that hovering predicts intent. On touch devices there is no hover, so always provide a non-hover fallback path (interaction, viewport, or timer) for the same content.

@defer Custom Triggers (when)

Objective: Drive prefetch and display from arbitrary template expressions using when, for use cases no built-in trigger covers.

When none of the built-in triggers fit a peculiar condition, use when for a fully custom trigger. Whereas on takes a predefined trigger name, when takes any truthy template expression. Back the block with component fields — for example two booleans, performPrefetch and display, both starting false — and flip them from click handlers on your own buttons. Then write prefetch when performPrefetch and when display.

Clicking the prefetch button sets its flag true, and the chunk downloads (Network tab) while the placeholder remains; clicking the display button sets its flag true, and the deferred content replaces the placeholder. The same precedence rule from earlier applies: if you click display first, Angular performs the prefetch immediately, because the display step always outranks prefetch — true for both built-in and custom triggers. The expression need not be a bare boolean; any valid template expression, including a function call, works, and Angular evaluates its truthiness.

  • when <expr> is the custom trigger; on <name> is for built-ins.
  • Drive it with component state (booleans, function calls — any truthy expression).
  • prefetch when … and when … are configured independently.
  • Display always takes precedence — triggering display forces prefetch if not yet done.
<button (click)="onPrefetch()">Trigger prefetch</button>
<button (click)="onDisplay()">Trigger display</button>

@defer (when display; prefetch when performPrefetch) {
  <courses-card-list [courses]="courses"></courses-card-list>
} @placeholder {
  <p>This is placeholder content</p>
}
OutputClick "Trigger prefetch" → chunk downloads, placeholder stays.
Click "Trigger display" → deferred content shown.
export class AppComponent {
  performPrefetch = false;
  display = false;

  onPrefetch() {
    this.performPrefetch = true;   // fires: prefetch when performPrefetch
  }

  onDisplay() {
    this.display = true;           // fires: when display (prefetch first if needed)
  }
}
OutputonDisplay() before onPrefetch() → Angular prefetches, then displays.

To close the section, here is one realistic scenario that puts the whole family to work at once — a "Print as PDF" button whose heavy PDF library is the deferred chunk. The library is prefetched on hover as the pointer approaches the button, then the dialog is displayed on interaction when the button is clicked; an @loading indicator (tuned with after and minimum) covers a slow download, and an @error block catches a failed fetch — every companion block and both control axes cooperating in a single flow.

<!-- capstone: prefetch on hover, display on click, with loading + error -->
<button #printBtn>Print as PDF</button>

@defer (on interaction(printBtn); prefetch on hover(printBtn)) {
  <pdf-export-dialog [invoice]="invoice"></pdf-export-dialog>
} @loading (after 1s; minimum 3s) {
  <p>Preparing PDF tools…</p>
} @error {
  <p>Sorry — the PDF exporter could not be loaded.</p>
} @placeholder {
  <p>Print-ready export</p>
}
OutputPointer approaches "Print as PDF" → chunk prefetched on hover (Network tab).
Click the button → dialog displayed; if the download is still running (> 1s) a spinner shows for ≥ 3s.
If the fetch fails (offline) → the @error message replaces the placeholder.

Mirrors branch ng-17-recordings-2 · reimplemented for clarity.

Common pitfallConfusing on and when. on idle is valid; when idle is not (there is no idle variable). Use on for named triggers and when only for your own expressions.
AdvancedBecause when accepts any expression, you can encode combinations built-ins cannot express — e.g. "prefetch only after the user has both hovered a region and clicked a specific button in a given order" — by composing several component flags into one predicate.
Cheat-sheet
@defer { ... }                      <!-- default: on idle -->
@defer (on idle) { ... }            <!-- browser idle (default) -->
@defer (on immediate) { ... }       <!-- as soon as it renders -->
@defer (on timer(5s)) { ... }       <!-- fixed delay -->
@defer (on viewport) { ... }        <!-- scrolls into view -->
@defer (on viewport(ref)) { ... }   <!-- specific element in view -->
@defer (on interaction) { ... }     <!-- click / keydown -->
@defer (on hover) { ... }           <!-- pointer enters -->
@defer (when cond) { ... }          <!-- custom expression -->
@defer (prefetch on idle; on viewport) { ... }  <!-- split control -->

@placeholder (minimum 3s) { ... }   <!-- stand-in before trigger -->
@loading (after 1s; minimum 3s) { ... } <!-- indicator during load -->
@error { ... }                      <!-- download failed -->

Quick check

What is the difference between the @placeholder and @loading blocks?

  1. They are identical aliases
  2. Placeholder is shown before the trigger fires; loading is shown while the chunk downloads
  3. Placeholder handles errors; loading handles success
Answerb — @placeholder is a stand-in shown until the trigger fires, while @loading is the indicator visible only during the background download.

Which keyword introduces a fully custom trigger driven by a component expression?

  1. on
  2. prefetch
  3. when
Answerc — when evaluates any truthy template expression; on is reserved for the built-in named triggers.

If the display trigger fires before the prefetch trigger has downloaded the chunk, Angular will:

  1. Show the error block
  2. Wait indefinitely for prefetch
  3. Perform the prefetch immediately, since display takes precedence
Answerc — the display step always outranks prefetch, so triggering display forces an immediate download if one has not happened yet.

Angular 17 Signals In Depth

Signals are Angular's reactive primitive: a container that wraps a value and notifies interested consumers whenever that value changes. This section explains why signals exist (fine-grained, Zone-less change detection), how to create and update them, how to derive new signals with computed(), how dependency tracking actually works, how to run side effects with effect(), and how to package signals into reusable data services and component inputs. Throughout, the emphasis is on writing declarative, reactive components while sidestepping the classic pitfalls.

Angular 17 Signals — Section Introduction

Objective: Understand why Angular introduced signals by contrasting them with the cost of default and OnPush change detection.

With default change detection, Angular has no reliable way to know which component's data changed after an event. A plain mutable class field could have been read or written anywhere in the app, possibly shared through a service, so Angular's only safe option is to walk the entire component tree and re-evaluate every template expression, comparing before and after. On a small counter this is invisible, but as pages grow to many components and bindings, that tree-wide diffing becomes a real performance cost.

OnPush change detection is faster but harder to use correctly: it comes with a set of rules — never mutate inputs in place, always hand down a fresh object reference, or lean on a state-management library — each of which adds complexity. Signals offer a third path: because a signal knows exactly when its value changes and exactly which parts of the view consume it, Angular can update only what is affected, without scanning the whole tree.

  • Default change detection must diff the entire component tree because plain variables give Angular no change signal.
  • OnPush is more efficient but introduces mutation rules and extra mental overhead.
  • A signal is a wrapper that tells Angular precisely when a value changed and who depends on it.
  • This enables targeted, optimized view updates and, eventually, dropping Zone.js entirely.
Common pitfallAssuming signals are only a performance tweak. Their bigger value is enabling a declarative reactive style where changes propagate automatically to consumers.
AdvancedSignals decouple change detection from Zone.js monkey-patching. A fully signal-based, zoneless app lets Angular schedule updates based on signal reads rather than intercepting every async browser API, which removes a class of hard-to-debug Zone-related errors.

Writing Your First Signal

Objective: Convert a plain mutable field into a signal, read it by calling it, and write it with set().

A signal is created with the signal() factory, passing an initial value: signal(0) returns a WritableSignal<number> — a container holding a number. The argument you pass is the starting value the container holds, so signal(100) reads back as 100 on first render and signal(0) as 0. To read the wrapped value you call the signal like a function: count(). Think of it as an auto-generated getter that returns whatever the signal currently holds; in a template you write {{ count() }}.

Because the field is no longer a plain number, count++ no longer works. To change the value you call set() with the new value. Every write through set() notifies Angular that the value changed, so change detection can update just the consumers of that signal instead of diffing the whole tree. The rule of thumb: any data a template reads should live inside a signal.

  • signal(initial) creates a writable signal wrapping the initial value.
  • Read a signal by invoking it: count() returns the current value.
  • Write with set(newValue); this is what notifies Angular of the change.
  • Put every piece of template-bound data inside a signal to get reactivity.
import { Component, signal } from '@angular/core';

@Component({
  selector: 'app-counter',
  standalone: true,
  template: `
    <p>Counter: {{ count() }}</p>
    <button (click)="increment()">Increment</button>
  `,
})
export class CounterComponent {
  count = signal<number>(0);

  increment() {
    this.count.set(this.count() + 1);
  }
}
OutputCounter: 0
after one click → Counter: 1

Reimplemented from branch ng-17-1-signal-inputs-update of the Angular Core Deep Dive · rewritten for clarity.

Common pitfallForgetting the parentheses. Writing {{ count }} renders the signal function itself, not its value — you must call it as count().
AdvancedReading a signal inside a reactive context (a template, computed, or effect) registers a dependency. Reading it in ordinary code just returns the value without tracking, which is why the same call syntax behaves differently depending on where it runs.

The update() API and Read-Only Signals

Objective: Update a signal from its current value with update(), and expose a non-writable view with asReadonly().

When the new value derives from the current one, update() is cleaner than set(). It takes a function whose argument is the current value and whose return value becomes the new value: count.update(current => current + 1). This is equivalent to reading the value and calling set(), just more concise.

From any writable signal you can derive a read-only handle with asReadonly(). The result is a Signal<number> rather than a WritableSignal<number>, so it exposes no set() or update() — it can only be read. This is the same read-only shape that computed() produces, and it is the standard way to hand a signal to the outside world while keeping mutation private.

  • update(fn) computes the next value from the current one; equivalent to set() but terser.
  • set() and update() are interchangeable ways to write a writable signal.
  • asReadonly() returns a Signal<T> with no write methods.
  • Read-only signals protect a value from being modified by consumers.
const count = signal<number>(0);

// increment using the current value
count.update(current => current + 1);   // count() === 1

// expose a read-only view
const readonlyCount = count.asReadonly();
// readonlyCount.set(...)  &larr; not available, compile error
console.log(readonlyCount());            // 1
Output1

Reimplemented from branch ng-17-1-signal-inputs-update · rewritten for clarity.

Common pitfallTrying to call set() or update() on a read-only signal (from asReadonly() or computed()). Those methods do not exist on Signal<T> and the code will not compile.
AdvancedasReadonly() does not copy the value — it returns a lightweight view backed by the same underlying signal. Writes through the original writable handle are still observed by everyone holding the read-only view.

Signals with Objects and Arrays — Don't Mutate, Set a New Reference

Objective: Learn why mutating an object or array inside a signal breaks reactivity, and update it correctly instead.

With non-primitive values it is tempting to reach into the signal and mutate it directly — course().title = 'New' or courses().push(newItem). This may appear to work under default change detection, but it bypasses the signal's notification mechanism entirely: Angular never learns the value changed, so in a signal-only (zoneless) setup nothing updates. The signal's whole purpose — telling consumers a change happened — is defeated.

The correct approach is to write through set() or update() with a new reference. For an object, hand in a new object; for an array, return a fresh array (for example by spreading the old one and appending). Because the reference changes through the signal's own API, the signal can notify every consumer that its value is now different.

  • Never mutate the value in place: course().title = ... or courses().push(...) are mistakes.
  • In-place mutation skips the signal's change notification, so consumers are never informed.
  • Set a brand-new object/array reference through set() or update().
  • Build the new array immutably, e.g. [...current, newItem].
const course = signal({ id: 1, title: 'Angular Basics' });
const courses = signal<string[]>(['Angular Basics']);

// WRONG: mutates in place, no notification
// course().title = 'Hello world';
// courses().push('Angular Core Deep Dive');

// RIGHT: new reference via set / update
course.set({ id: 1, title: 'Hello world' });
courses.update(list => [...list, 'Angular Core Deep Dive']);

console.log(course().title);
console.log(courses().join(', '));
OutputHello world
Angular Basics, Angular Core Deep Dive

Reimplemented from branch ng-17-1-signal-inputs-update · rewritten for clarity.

Common pitfallBeing fooled by default change detection. Mutating in place "works" only because Zone.js is still diffing the tree; the moment you rely on signals for updates, those mutations silently stop propagating.
AdvancedSignals compare with reference equality (Object.is) by default, which is exactly why a new reference is required. You can supply a custom equal function in the signal options if you need structural comparison to suppress redundant notifications.

Derived Signals with computed()

Objective: Create a signal whose value is computed from one or more source signals using computed().

Beyond reacting to changes, signals can derive new values. computed() defines a signal built from one or more source signals: you pass a function that reads the sources and returns the derived value. For example, a derived signal equal to the counter times ten reads counter() inside the computed function and returns counter() * 10. Reading the result — derived() — gives the up-to-date computed value, and it recomputes automatically whenever a source changes.

A computed signal is always read-only: its type is Signal<number>, not WritableSignal<number>, so it has no set() or update(). Its value is determined solely by its sources. In real applications you end up with a chain of signals depending on one another, all derived declaratively.

  • computed(fn) creates a signal derived from the source signals read inside fn.
  • The derived value recalculates automatically when any source changes.
  • Computed signals are read-only — no set() or update().
  • Signals can be chained, forming a dependency graph of derived values.
import { signal, computed } from '@angular/core';

const counter = signal<number>(0);
const derived = computed<number>(() => counter() * 10);

console.log(counter(), derived());   // 0 0
counter.set(3);
console.log(counter(), derived());   // 3 30
Output0 0
3 30

Reimplemented from branch ng-17-1-signal-inputs-update · rewritten for clarity.

Common pitfallTrying to assign to a computed signal. It has no writer — the only way to change its output is to change one of its source signals.
Advancedcomputed() is lazy and memoized: the function does not run until the value is read, and the cached result is reused until a source actually changes. This makes even long derivation chains cheap.

How Dependencies Between Signals Are Created

Objective: Understand that dependency tracking is automatic and dynamic, based on the sources read during the last run of the computed function.

Angular does not require you to declare dependencies — it discovers them by watching which signals are read while it executes a computed (or effect) function. Because Angular calls that function itself, it knows exactly when execution starts and ends, and it records every signal invoked during that run as a dependency. When one of those recorded sources emits, the function re-runs.
