Crucially, this tracking is dynamic, recomputed on every execution — not fixed for the signal's lifetime. If a source read sits inside a conditional branch that does not run, that dependency is not registered. The classic bug: reading counter() only inside an if (multiplier >= 10) block. On the first run multiplier is 0, the branch is skipped, counter() is never read, and Angular never links the two signals — so later counter changes are ignored. The fix is to read every source signal unconditionally, outside any if, so the dependency is always established.

  • Dependencies are detected automatically from the signals read during execution.
  • Tracking is dynamic: it reflects the last run, and can change between runs.
  • A source read hidden inside a skipped branch creates no dependency.
  • Keep source-signal reads outside conditionals to preserve the link reliably.
const counter = signal(0);
let multiplier = 0;   // plain field, not a signal

// BROKEN: counter() only read when the branch runs
const broken = computed(() => {
  if (multiplier >= 10) {
    return counter() * 10;   // dependency not registered on first run
  }
  return 0;
});

// FIXED: read the source unconditionally
const fixed = computed(() => {
  const value = counter();   // dependency always registered
  return multiplier >= 10 ? value * 10 : 0;
});
Outputbroken: stops reacting to counter changes
fixed: recomputes every time counter changes

Reimplemented from branch ng-17-1-signal-inputs-update · rewritten for clarity.

Common pitfallWrapping a source-signal read in an if. If the branch is skipped on the first execution, the dependency is never formed and the derived value silently stops updating.
AdvancedDynamic tracking is a feature, not just a hazard: a computed can legitimately depend on different signals over time as conditions change, which avoids over-subscribing. The discipline is simply to read anything you always want to depend on before any conditional.

The effect() API

Objective: Run a side effect reactively whenever one or more signals change, and understand why writing signals inside an effect is disallowed.

Where computed() produces a new value, effect() performs a pure side effect — logging, persisting to local storage, writing to a cookie, or firing a background save — in response to signal changes. You pass a function to effect(); Angular tracks whichever signals it reads and re-runs it whenever any of them emits. Like a computed, an effect runs at least once initially to establish its dependencies.

Effects run only after all dependent signals have settled: if a single change updates a source and its downstream computed, the effect still fires just once with the final values. A key rule — do not write to signals inside an effect (or a computed). By default Angular throws "writing to signals is not allowed in a computed or effect," because it risks infinite loops. There is an escape hatch (allowSignalWrites), but needing it usually signals a design problem. Version note: allowSignalWrites applied to Angular 17–18; it was deprecated and removed in v19+, where effects allow signal writes by default (with different scheduling), so on newer Angular you simply avoid writing state from effects rather than opting in. Either way, effects should be reserved for genuine, rare side effects, not for updating application state.

  • effect(fn) runs fn reactively whenever a signal it reads changes.
  • Reserve effects for true side effects (logging, storage, network) — not deriving state.
  • An effect runs once up front to record its dependencies.
  • Multiple simultaneous source changes coalesce into a single effect run.
  • Writing to signals inside an effect throws by default to prevent infinite loops.
import { Component, signal, computed, effect } from '@angular/core';

@Component({ selector: 'app-counter', standalone: true, template: '...' })
export class CounterComponent {
  counter = signal(0);
  derived = computed(() => this.counter() * 10);

  constructor() {
    effect(() => {
      // reads register dependencies on counter and derived
      console.log('counter:', this.counter(), 'derived:', this.derived());
      // this.counter.update(v => v + 1); &larr; would throw: writes not allowed
    });
  }
}
Outputcounter: 0 derived: 0
after one increment → counter: 1 derived: 10 (logged once)

The coalescing is easiest to see when one effect reads two independent signals and both change in the same tick — Angular still runs the effect just once, with the final values of both, rather than once per write:

import { signal, effect } from '@angular/core';

const counter = signal<number>(0);
const name = signal('Ada');

// ONE effect reads BOTH signals
effect(() => {
  console.log(`${name()}: ${counter()}`);
});

// change both signals in the same synchronous tick
counter.set(1);
name.set('Grace');
// the effect runs ONCE with the final values — not once per set()
OutputAda: 0
after both writes in one tick → Grace: 1 (logged once, not twice)

Reimplemented from branch ng-17-1-signal-inputs-update · rewritten for clarity.

Common pitfallUsing an effect to update other signals. Besides throwing by default, doing so with allowSignalWrites can create an endless update cycle that crashes the app — or even the browser tab.
AdvancedBy default an effect() must be created in an injection context (like a constructor) so Angular can tie its lifecycle to the enclosing component. To create one elsewhere, pass an explicit Injector via the effect options.

Cleaning Up Signal Effects Manually

Objective: Destroy an effect on demand and run teardown logic with the effect's cleanup callback.

Normally you don't manage effect cleanup: an effect created in a component's context is destroyed automatically when that component is destroyed, releasing its resources. Manual cleanup matters only for things like clearing a timer/interval, cancelling a timeout, closing a network connection, or otherwise releasing resources at a chosen moment rather than at component teardown.

By default an effect created inside a component is destroyed automatically with that component, so to take its lifetime into your own hands you pass { manualCleanup: true } as the second argument to effect(). That option opts out of the automatic destroy-on-teardown and is the actual prerequisite for managing the effect by hand. effect() returns an EffectRef: store it, and call ref.destroy() to stop the effect early — after that, source changes no longer trigger it. Separately, the effect function receives a lowercase onCleanup parameter (its type is EffectCleanupRegisterFn; note there is no OnCleanup lifecycle interface to import from @angular/core): register a function with it and Angular runs that right before each re-run and on final destruction, which is exactly where you release intervals, timeouts, or sockets and drop closures so their captured resources can be garbage-collected.

  • Effects tied to a component are cleaned up automatically on destroy — no action needed.
  • Pass { manualCleanup: true } to opt out of that automatic teardown and manage the effect yourself.
  • Manual cleanup is for timers, subscriptions, connections, or explicit resource release.
  • effect() returns an EffectRef; ref.destroy() stops it early.
  • The effect's lowercase onCleanup(fn) parameter runs teardown before each re-run and on destroy.
// NOTE: there is no `OnCleanup` export in @angular/core — do not import one.
// The cleanup hook is the lowercase `onCleanup` param passed to the callback.
import { Component, signal, effect, EffectRef } from '@angular/core';

@Component({ selector: 'app-counter', standalone: true, template: '...' })
export class CounterComponent {
  counter = signal(0);
  private effectRef: EffectRef;

  constructor() {
    this.effectRef = effect((onCleanup) => {
      const value = this.counter();
      const id = setInterval(() => console.log('tick', value), 1000);

      onCleanup(() => {
        clearInterval(id);          // release the timer
        console.log('cleanup performed');
      });
    }, { manualCleanup: true });    // opt out of auto destroy-on-teardown
  }

  stop() {
    this.effectRef.destroy();       // effect no longer reacts
  }
}
Outputtick 0 (each second)
on stop() → cleanup performed (once); effect no longer logs

Reimplemented from branch ng-17-1-signal-inputs-update · rewritten for clarity.

Common pitfallCalling destroy() repeatedly expecting repeated teardown. Cleanup runs only once; further clicks do nothing because the effect is already gone.
AdvancedThe onCleanup callback also fires between successive runs, not just on destruction — so an effect that starts an interval on each run should clear the previous one in cleanup to avoid leaking overlapping timers.

Signal-based Data Services

Objective: Share a signal across unrelated components by encapsulating it in an injectable service with a controlled public API.

When a signal must be shared between components that have no parent–child relationship and live in different parts of the tree, defining it as one component's field won't do. Exporting it as a global variable is tempting but leads to the same maintenance problems as any global — anything can change it, from anywhere. The better pattern is a data service that encapsulates the signal and only permits controlled mutation.

Inside an @Injectable({ providedIn: 'root' }) service, keep the writable signal private so only the service can call set()/update(). Expose a read-only view via asReadonly() for consumers to read, and provide explicit public methods (e.g. increment()) as the only way to change it. This centralizes validation and makes every mutation discoverable — finding all callers of the method reveals everywhere the value changes. Components inject the service and read the public read-only signal; they cannot set it directly.

  • Use a service to share a signal across unrelated components — avoid globals.
  • Keep the writable signal private; expose a read-only version with asReadonly().
  • Offer public methods as the only controlled path to mutate the value.
  • Centralized methods make validation and "find all usages" trivial.
import { Injectable, signal } from '@angular/core';

@Injectable({ providedIn: 'root' })
export class CounterService {
  private counter = signal<number>(0);          // private, writable
  readonly counterPublic = this.counter.asReadonly();  // read-only view

  increment() {
    if (this.counter() >= 10) {
      throw new Error('Maximum value reached');   // centralized validation
    }
    this.counter.update(v => v + 1);
  }
}
OutputcounterPublic() → 0
after increment() → 1
increment() past 10 → Error: Maximum value reached
@Component({
  selector: 'app-counter',
  standalone: true,
  template: `
    <p>{{ counterService.counterPublic() }}</p>
    <button (click)="counterService.increment()">+</button>
  `,
})
export class CounterComponent {
  constructor(public counterService: CounterService) {}
}
OutputComponent can read the signal and call increment(), but cannot set() it.

Reimplemented from branch ng-17-1-signal-inputs-update · rewritten for clarity.

Common pitfallReaching for this pattern for every signal. It only pays off for state genuinely shared across unrelated components — for local state, a plain component field signal is simpler and preferred.
AdvancedprovidedIn: 'root' makes the service a singleton, so all injectors share the same signal instance. Scoping the provider to a specific route or component instead would give each subtree its own isolated copy of the state.

Input Signals — input() vs @Input

Objective: Declare component inputs as signals with input() and read them like any other signal.

The classic @Input decorator is easy to understand, but it produces a plain value, which is awkward when you want to write a component in a fully reactive, signal-driven style. Angular's input() primitive declares an input that is a signal. You pass the value type as a generic — input<Course>() — and the field's type becomes an InputSignal<Course> rather than a bare value (with no default, its emitted type is Course | undefined).

Because the input is now a signal, you access its value by calling it: course(). Everywhere the old code read the raw property, you switch to invoking the signal. The payoff is composition: a signal input slots directly into computed() or effect() like any other signal, so a derived value that combines, say, the course description and category recalculates automatically whenever the input receives a new value from the parent.

  • input<T>() declares an input that is a signal (InputSignal<T>).
  • Without a default, the value type is T | undefined.
  • Read the input by calling it: course(), exactly like other signals.
  • Signal inputs compose naturally with computed() and effect().
import { Component, input, computed } from '@angular/core';

interface Course { description: string; category: string; }

@Component({
  selector: 'app-course-card',
  standalone: true,
  template: `<h3>{{ courseLabel() }}</h3>`,
})
export class CourseCardComponent {
  course = input<Course>();   // InputSignal<Course | undefined>

  courseLabel = computed(() => {
    const c = this.course();
    return c ? `${c.description} — ${c.category}` : '';
  });
}
Output<h3>Angular Deep Dive — Frameworks</h3>

Reimplemented from branch ng-17-1-signal-inputs-update · rewritten for clarity.

Common pitfallForgetting to invoke a signal input. After migrating from @Input, leaving a bare course reference in the template or class yields the signal function, not the value — call it as course().
AdvancedSignal inputs are read-only by design — a child cannot write back to the parent's binding. For two-way binding scenarios Angular provides model(), which pairs a writable signal input with an automatic ...Change output.

Using Signal Inputs to Avoid ngOnChanges

Objective: Replace the ngOnChanges lifecycle hook by reacting to a signal input with effect() (or computed()).

With a decorator input, reacting to a new value from the parent typically means implementing ngOnChanges and inspecting the change — for example logging every time the course input updates. This works but couples your reactive logic to a lifecycle hook. A signal input removes that need entirely.

Since the input is a signal, you can react to it directly: put a call to course() inside an effect(), and Angular registers the dependency. Every time the parent passes a new value, the input signal emits and the effect re-runs. You can drop ngOnChanges (and often ngOnInit) and express the same behavior declaratively — and for derived data, a computed() reading the input is even cleaner than reacting imperatively.

  • Decorator inputs push you toward ngOnChanges to observe changes.
  • Reading a signal input inside an effect() reacts to every new value automatically.
  • This lets you remove ngOnChanges (and frequently ngOnInit).
  • For derived values, prefer computed() over an imperative effect.
// BEFORE: decorator input + lifecycle hook
export class CourseCardComponent implements OnChanges {
  @Input() course!: Course;
  ngOnChanges() { console.log('course changed', this.course); }
}

// AFTER: signal input + effect, no ngOnChanges
import { Component, input, effect } from '@angular/core';

export class CourseCardComponent {
  course = input<Course>();
  constructor() {
    effect(() => console.log('course changed', this.course()));
  }
}
Outputcourse changed { ... } // logged whenever the parent passes a new course

Reimplemented from branch ng-17-1-signal-inputs-update · rewritten for clarity.

Common pitfallAssuming ngOnChanges stops working with a signal input. It doesn't — ngOnChanges still fires when a signal input receives a new value, and the change even appears in the SimpleChanges argument. The reason to drop it is that keeping it is redundant: reacting through the imperative lifecycle hook re-introduces exactly the style signals aim to remove. Prefer effect() for side effects and computed() for derived values, and delete the now-redundant hook.
AdvancedPrefer computed() when you only need a derived value, and reserve effect() for genuine side effects triggered by input changes (logging, analytics, persistence). Overusing effects to compute state reintroduces the imperative style signals aim to eliminate.

Signal Input Options — required, alias, transform

Objective: Configure signal inputs with input.required(), an alias, and a transform function.

Signal inputs support the same configuration options as the decorator input. To make an input mandatory, use input.required<T>() — notice you provide no default, because the value must come from the template; if a consumer omits the binding, Angular reports that the required input is missing. Because input.required() is relatively new, some editors (WebStorm, for one) briefly flagged spurious errors on it, and the exact wording of the missing-input message varies by Angular version — so don't be thrown by tooling that lags the feature. For optional inputs you can pass an initial value plus an options object as a second argument.

The alias option lets the binding name differ from the member name: the field might be course internally but be bound in templates as tutorial. The transform option supplies a function that maps the incoming value to the stored value — for instance multiplying an incoming number, or coercing a string. These are the same alias and transform semantics the decorator input offers, now integrated cleanly with the signal API.

  • input.required<T>() makes the input mandatory and takes no default value.
  • Omitting a required input's binding produces a compile/runtime error.
  • alias exposes the input under a different binding name than the member.
  • transform maps the incoming value before it is stored in the signal.
import { Component, input } from '@angular/core';

@Component({ selector: 'app-course-card', standalone: true, template: '...' })
export class CourseCardComponent {
  // required: no default, must be bound by the parent
  title = input.required<string>();

  // optional with alias + transform
  course = input<number>(0, {
    alias: 'tutorial',
    transform: (value: number) => value * 100,
  });
}
Output<app-course-card [title]="'Angular'" [tutorial]="2">
title() → 'Angular', course() → 200

Reimplemented from branch ng-17-1-signal-inputs-update · rewritten for clarity.

Common pitfallPassing an initial value to input.required(). Required inputs must not have a default — the value has to be supplied through the template binding.
AdvancedA transform can change the accepted binding type versus the stored type. Angular ships built-in transforms like booleanAttribute and numberAttribute for coercing string-ish template values into proper booleans or numbers.
Cheat-sheet
const count = signal(0);              // create writable signal
count();                             // read (call it)
count.set(5);                        // write a new value
count.update(v => v + 1);            // write from current value
const ro = count.asReadonly();       // read-only view (no set/update)
const doubled = computed(() => count() * 2);   // derived, read-only
effect(() => console.log(count()));  // reactive side effect
const ref = effect((onCleanup) => { onCleanup(() => {/* teardown */}); });
ref.destroy();                       // stop an effect manually
value = input<T>();                   // signal input (read as value())
req = input.required<T>();            // mandatory signal input
aliased = input<T>(def, { alias: 'x', transform: fn });

Quick check

How do you read the current value of a signal declared as count = signal(0)?

  1. count.value
  2. count()
  3. count.get()
Answerb — a signal is read by invoking it like a function.

Why can mutating an object inside a signal (course().title = 'x') break reactivity?

  1. It throws a compile error
  2. It bypasses the signal's change notification, so consumers aren't informed
  3. It permanently freezes the signal
Answerb — you must set a new reference through set()/update() so the signal can notify consumers.

A computed() that reads a source signal only inside an if block that is skipped on first run will:

  1. Always recompute correctly
  2. Register the dependency lazily on the next change
  3. Fail to register the dependency, so it stops reacting to that source
Answerc — dependency tracking reflects the signals actually read during the last run; keep source reads outside conditionals.

Conclusion & Bonus

The closing section wraps up the Angular Core Deep Dive. A short bonus lecture points to living, always-current material, and the conclusion revisits every major topic covered — from components and the view layer through dependency injection, change detection, lifecycle hooks, pipes, i18n and Angular Elements — with guidance on when each feature actually earns its place in a real application.

Bonus Lecture — Staying Current with Angular

Objective: Understand how to keep learning after the course by tracking new Angular releases and follow-up resources.

This closing bonus lecture (no captions in the source) serves as a signpost rather than a lesson. Angular ships new major versions on a predictable cadence, and features that were experimental during recording — signals, the built-in control flow, deferrable views, standalone-by-default scaffolding — mature and shift over time. The bonus material therefore directs learners toward continually updated content and companion resources so their knowledge does not go stale as the framework advances.

The practical takeaway is to build a habit of following official channels: the Angular release notes and update guide, the framework blog, and the course's own refreshed lectures. Treating the framework as a moving target — rather than a fixed snapshot — is what keeps an Angular skill set employable across versions.

  • Angular evolves on a regular major-release schedule; expect APIs and defaults to keep shifting.
  • The bonus lecture points to updated, living material rather than teaching a new concept.
  • Follow the official release notes, update guide, and blog to learn what changed and why.
  • Revisit refreshed course sections as newer authoring styles (signals, control flow) become the default.
# See what a version bump involves before committing to it
ng update

# Update the core framework and CLI together
ng update @angular/core @angular/cli
OutputLists available updates and any migration schematics that will run automatically.
Common pitfallSkipping intermediate major versions when upgrading. Angular's automated migrations assume a step-by-step path — jumping several versions at once often breaks the ng update schematics.
Advancedng update runs Angular's automatic migrations — the schematics that rewrite your code for renamed or changed APIs as you step through each major version, which is why upgrading one major at a time lets them apply cleanly. The template and structural conversions, however, are separate opt-in schematics you invoke yourself: ng generate @angular/core:control-flow converts *ngIf/*ngFor to the @if/@for blocks, and ng generate @angular/core:standalone moves an app to standalone bootstrap. ng update does not perform those two for you.

Course Conclusion & Key Takeaways

Objective: Consolidate the whole course into a mental map of what each feature does and — crucially — how often you will reach for it in day-to-day work.

The course began by scaffolding a simple app and framing Angular's core benefits, then worked systematically through the framework. It covered the view layer in depth — local template querying, content projection, dynamic template instantiation, and passing templates as component inputs. It moved to the service layer with the built-in HttpClient and the async pipe for streaming observable data into templates, then built a custom injectable service from scratch.

Dependency injection was a central theme: tree-shakable injectables, the hierarchical injector, and hand-written providers and injection tokens. Writing those by hand is the rare advanced case, but the exercise made the underlying principle concrete — whenever something is injected in an Angular application there is always an injection token identifying what to inject and always a provider describing how to create it, even when the framework supplies both for you. Change detection followed — the default strategy versus OnPush, when each fits, the debugging pitfalls of OnPush, and even how to build a custom change-detection mechanism when a scenario genuinely calls for one. The course also revisited view encapsulation: by default Angular uses emulated encapsulation, so a component's styles are scoped to that component and never leak into the rest of the page — but you can switch it off and style the app with a plain external stylesheet like any other web page. The recommendation is to keep the default, since component-scoped styles are simpler to write and reason about. From there the course walked the lifecycle hooks and their exact firing order, built a custom feature module, dove into pipes (pure versus impure, and their relationship to DI), completed a section on internationalization, and closed with Angular Elements for using components inside non-Angular hosts like React or Vue.

The most important framing is proportional emphasis. The material from the early sections — components with inputs, outputs and custom events, composing a component tree, template reference variables, custom services, and the async pipe — is what you will use roughly ninety percent of the time and deserves the most mastery. The advanced tools are worth understanding but reached for sparingly: ViewChild/ViewChildren only when you need programmatic access beyond a plain template reference; custom directives rarely, though you lean on the built-in ones (ngFor, ngIf and friends) constantly — which is exactly why they repay the time spent learning their full feature set; custom providers and tokens only for genuinely advanced DI; and OnPush only after profiling confirms change detection is the real bottleneck, which usually only happens when a template renders a lot of data through many binding expressions (more often the true culprit is backend latency instead). Of the lifecycle hooks, ngOnInit is near-daily while most others are occasional.

  • Spend the most effort on the everyday core: components, inputs/outputs, services, and the async pipe.
  • Prefer plain template references; use ViewChild/ViewChildren only for programmatic access.
  • Keep the default view encapsulation and default change detection unless you have a proven reason not to.
  • Favor tree-shakable providers; write custom providers/tokens only for advanced DI needs.
  • Under the hood, every injected dependency resolves through an injection token (what to inject) and a provider (how to build it) — even the ones Angular wires up for you.
  • Reach for OnPush only after profiling shows change detection is the bottleneck — typically lots of data with many binding expressions; it is faster but trickier.
  • You will use built-in directives constantly and author custom ones rarely — so invest study time in the built-in directives' full feature set.
// Default: emulated encapsulation keeps styles scoped to THIS component,
// and <ng-content> projects markup the parent passed in.
@Component({
  selector: 'app-badge',
  encapsulation: ViewEncapsulation.Emulated,        // the default — recommended
  styles: [`.badge { color: rebeccapurple; }`],
  template: `<span class="badge">
    <ng-content select="[icon]" /><ng-content />   <!-- named slot, then default slot -->
  </span>`
})
export class BadgeComponent {}

// Opt out: styles behave like a plain global stylesheet (leak everywhere).
@Component({ selector: 'app-theme', encapsulation: ViewEncapsulation.None /* ... */ })
export class ThemeComponent {}

// Usage — the projected content fills the two <ng-content> slots:
// <app-badge><svg icon>...</svg> New</app-badge>
ResultWith Emulated, the .badge rule only ever styles this component's own markup. With None, the same rule leaks out and can restyle any .badge on the page — exactly like an ordinary external stylesheet. The <svg icon> lands in the select="[icon]" slot; the text "New" falls into the plain <ng-content />.
If you are just starting outDo not feel you must master every advanced topic immediately. Concentrate first on components, inputs, outputs, template references, custom injectable services, and the async pipe — the foundation from the course's opening sections — and layer the advanced features on as real projects demand them.
Common pitfallReaching for advanced tooling by default — switching an entire app to OnPush, or hand-writing providers everywhere — before profiling or a concrete requirement justifies the added complexity.
AdvancedThe same proportional judgment applies to the newest APIs: signals and the @if/@for control flow are becoming the everyday authoring style, while @defer and custom change-detection strategies remain targeted tools for specific performance and loading scenarios.
Whole-course cheat-sheet
// ── Component with input & output (decorator style) ──
@Component({ selector: 'app-course', standalone: true, template: `...` })
export class CourseComponent {
  @Input() course!: Course;              // parent -> child data
  @Output() saved = new EventEmitter<Course>();  // child -> parent event
  save() { this.saved.emit(this.course); }
}

// ── Signal-based inputs/outputs (modern style) ──
course = input.required<Course>();        // signal input
saved   = output<Course>();               // signal output

// ── New built-in control flow (template) ──
// @if (course) { {{ course.title }} } @else { Loading... }
// @for (c of courses; track c.id) { {{ c.title }} }
// @switch (status) { @case ('ok') { ... } @default { ... } }

// ── Deferrable views: lazy-load on demand ──
// @defer (on viewport) { <app-heavy /> } @placeholder { Loading... }

// ── Dependency injection ──
@Injectable({ providedIn: 'root' })      // tree-shakable singleton
export class CoursesService {
  private http = inject(HttpClient);      // inject() function form
  list() { return this.http.get<Course[]>('/api/courses'); }
}

// ── OnPush change detection ──
@Component({ changeDetection: ChangeDetectionStrategy.OnPush })
// Re-renders only on new @Input reference, emitted event, or async pipe.

// ── Lifecycle hooks (firing order) ──
// ngOnChanges -> ngOnInit -> ngDoCheck
//   -> ngAfterContentInit -> ngAfterContentChecked
//   -> ngAfterViewInit -> ngAfterViewChecked -> ngOnDestroy

// ── Signals: reactive state ──
const count = signal(0);
const doubled = computed(() => count() * 2);
effect(() => console.log(count()));       // runs on change
count.set(1); count.update(n => n + 1);

// ── Async pipe (template) — auto subscribe/unsubscribe ──
// {{ course$ | async }}   or   @if (course$ | async; as course) { ... }

// ── View encapsulation (styles scope) ──
@Component({ encapsulation: ViewEncapsulation.Emulated })  // default: styles scoped to the component
@Component({ encapsulation: ViewEncapsulation.None })      // opt out: styles behave globally

// ── Content projection (template) ──
// <ng-content />                     default slot
// <ng-content select="[header]" />   named slot, fills <div header>...</div>

// ── Standalone bootstrap (no NgModule) ──
bootstrapApplication(AppComponent, {
  providers: [provideHttpClient(), provideRouter(routes)]
});

Final check

According to the course's closing advice, when should you switch a component to OnPush change detection?

  1. Always, on every component, from the start
  2. Only after profiling confirms change detection is the actual performance bottleneck
  3. Never — the default strategy is always superior
Answerb — OnPush is faster but trickier to use, so reach for it only when profiling proves change detection is the problem, not backend latency or something else.

Which lifecycle hook is described as one you will use nearly all the time, unlike most of the others?

  1. ngAfterViewChecked
  2. ngOnInit
  3. ngDoCheck
Answerb — ngOnInit runs once after the first input binding and is used constantly; the remaining hooks are only occasionally needed.

What does making an injectable tree-shakable (via providedIn: 'root') achieve?

  1. It forces every component to create its own instance
  2. It lets the build drop the service from the bundle when nothing uses it, reducing app size
  3. It disables dependency injection for that service
Answerb — tree-shakable providers are only bundled when actually referenced, implicitly shrinking the application bundle.
